**Expected Answer:** Use ratio test: |a_{n+1}/a_n| = |(n+1)! · x^{n+1} / (n+1)^{n+1}| / |n! · x^n / n^n| = |x| · (n+1)! / n! · n^n / (n+1)^{n+1} = |x| · (n+1) · n^n / (n+1)^{n+1} = |x| · n^n / (n+1)^n = |x| · [n/(n+1)]^n = |x| · [1/(1+1/n)]^n. As n → ∞, [1/(1+1/n)]^n → 1/e. So limit = |x|/e. Converges when |x|/e < 1, i.e., |x| < e. Radius R = e. At endpoints x = ±e: terms are (n! · (±e)^n) / n^n. By Stirling: n! ≈ √(2πn)(n/e)^n, so |a_n| ≈ √(2πn)(n/e)^n · e^n / n^n = √(2πn) → ∞. Terms don't → 0, so diverges at both endpoints. Interval: (-e, e).
**Skills Tested:** Power series, ratio test, Stirling's approximation, endpoint analysis.

**ID:** AAD-197
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Real Analysis — Taylor Series
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Find the Taylor series of f(x) = ln(1+x) centered at x = 0. Determine its radius of convergence and behavior at the endpoints.
**Expected Answer:** f(x) = ln(1+x). f'(x) = 1/(1+x) = Σ(n=0 to ∞) (-1)^n x^n for |x| < 1 (geometric series). Integrate term by term: f(x) = C + Σ(n=0 to ∞) (-1)^n x^{n+1}/(n+1). Since f(0) = 0, C = 0. So ln(1+x) = Σ(n=0 to ∞) (-1)^n x^{n+1}/(n+1) = x - x²/2 + x³/3 - x⁴/4 + ... = Σ(n=1 to ∞) (-1)^{n+1} x^n/n. Radius R = 1 (from ratio test or since 1/(1+x) has singularity at x = -1). At x = 1: Σ (-1)^{n+1}/n = alternating harmonic series, converges (to ln 2). At x = -1: Σ (-1)^{n+1}(-1)^n/n = Σ (-1)^{2n+1}/n = Σ -1/n = -Σ 1/n, diverges (harmonic series). Interval of convergence: (-1, 1].
**Skills Tested:** Taylor series, term-by-term integration, endpoint convergence.

**ID:** AAD-198
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Real Analysis — Multivariable Calculus
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Let f(x,y) = xy(x² - y²)/(x² + y²) for (x,y) ≠ (0,0) and f(0,0) = 0. Compute f_xy(0,0) and f_yx(0,0). Are they equal? Explain why or why not.
**Expected Answer:** First compute partial derivatives at (0,0). f_x(0,0) = lim(h→0) [f(h,0) - f(0,0)]/h = lim(h→0) [0 - 0]/h = 0. f_y(0,0) = lim(k→0) [f(0,k) - f(0,0)]/k = 0. For (x,y) ≠ (0,0): f_x = ∂/∂x [xy(x²-y²)/(x²+y²)]. This is complex, but we need f_xy(0,0) = ∂/∂y [f_x](0,0) = lim(k→0) [f_x(0,k) - f_x(0,0)]/k. Compute f_x(0,k): for x=0, f(0,y) = 0, so f_x(0,y) = lim(h→0) [f(h,y) - f(0,y)]/h = lim(h→0) [hy(h²-y²)/(h²+y²)]/h = lim(h→0) y(h²-y²)/(h²+y²) = y(-y²)/y² = -y. So f_x(0,k) = -k. Thus f_xy(0,0) = lim(k→0) [-k - 0]/k = -1. Similarly, f_y(x,0) = lim(k→0) [f(x,k) - f(x,0)]/k = lim(k→0) [xk(x²-k²)/(x²+k²)]/k = x(x²)/x² = x. So f_yx(0,0) = lim(h→0) [f_y(h,0) - f_y(0,0)]/h = lim(h→0) [h - 0]/h = 1. Thus f_xy(0,0) = -1 ≠ 1 = f_yx(0,0). They're not equal because the mixed partials are not continuous at (0,0), so Clairaut's theorem doesn't apply.
**Skills Tested:** Multivariable calculus, mixed partial derivatives, Clairaut's theorem.

**ID:** AAD-199
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Real Analysis — Lebesgue Integration
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** State and prove the Dominated Convergence Theorem. Give an example showing the domination condition is necessary.
**Expected Answer:** DCT: If f_n → f pointwise a.e., and |f_n| ≤ g for all n where g is integrable, then f is integrable and ∫f_n → ∫f. Proof sketch: Since |f_n| ≤ g, we have f_n + g ≥ 0 and g - f_n ≥ 0. Apply Fatou's lemma to both: ∫liminf(f_n + g) ≤ liminf ∫(f_n + g), so ∫f + ∫g ≤ liminf ∫f_n + ∫g, giving ∫f ≤ liminf ∫f_n. Similarly, ∫liminf(g - f_n) ≤ liminf ∫(g - f_n), so ∫g - ∫f ≤ ∫g - limsup ∫f_n, giving limsup ∫f_n ≤ ∫f. Thus limsup ∫f_n ≤ ∫f ≤ liminf ∫f_n, so ∫f_n → ∫f. Example showing necessity: f_n = n · χ_{(0,1/n)} on [0,1]. f_n → 0 pointwise, but ∫f_n = 1 ↛ 0. No integrable dominating function exists since sup f_n(x) = ∞ near 0.
**Skills Tested:** Measure theory, Lebesgue integration, convergence theorems.

**ID:** AAD-200
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Real Analysis — Functional Analysis
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** State the Hahn-Banach theorem and explain its significance. Use it to prove that for any x₀ ≠ 0 in a normed space X, there exists a continuous linear functional f with f(x₀) = ||x₀|| and ||f|| = 1.
**Expected Answer:** Hahn-Banach: If p is a sublinear functional on vector space X, f is a linear functional on subspace Y ⊆ X with f(y) ≤ p(y) for all y ∈ Y, then f extends to linear functional F on X with F(x) ≤ p(x) for all x ∈ X. Significance: guarantees existence of continuous linear functionals, separates points from convex sets, foundational for duality theory. Proof: Define p(x) = ||x₀|| · ||x|| (sublinear). On Y = span{x₀}, define f(αx₀) = α||x₀||. Check: f(αx₀) = α||x₀|| ≤ ||αx₀|| = p(αx₀) for α ≥ 0; for α < 0, f(αx₀) = α||x₀|| < 0 ≤ ||αx₀||. So f ≤ p on Y. By Hahn-Banach, extend to F on X with F(x) ≤ ||x₀|| · ||x||. This gives |F(x)| ≤ ||x₀|| · ||x|| (apply to x and -x), so ||F|| ≤ ||x₀||. Also F(x₀) = f(x₀) = ||x₀||, so ||F|| ≥ F(x₀)/||x₀|| = 1. Thus ||F|| = 1 and F(x₀) = ||x₀||.
**Skills Tested:** Functional analysis, Hahn-Banach theorem, normed spaces.

### ABSTRACT ALGEBRA (Questions 201-210)

**ID:** AAD-201
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Abstract Algebra — Group Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Let G be a group of order pq where p < q are primes and p does not divide q-1. Prove G is cyclic.
**Expected Answer:** By Sylow's theorems: n_q | p and n_q ≡ 1 (mod q). Since p < q, n_q = 1 (only divisor of p that's ≡ 1 mod q is 1). So unique (hence normal) Sylow q-subgroup Q ≅ Z_q. Similarly, n_p | q and n_p ≡ 1 (mod p). Divisors of q are 1 and q. If n_p = q, then q ≡ 1 (mod p), so p | (q-1), contradicting hypothesis. Thus n_p = 1, so unique normal Sylow p-subgroup P ≅ Z_p. Since P ∩ Q = {e} (orders are coprime) and both normal, PQ is a subgroup of order pq = |G|, so G = PQ ≅ P × Q ≅ Z_p × Z_q ≅ Z_{pq} (since gcd(p,q) = 1). Thus G is cyclic.
**Skills Tested:** Sylow theorems, group structure, direct products.

**ID:** AAD-202
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Abstract Algebra — Ring Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Prove that every finite integral domain is a field.
**Expected Answer:** Let R be a finite integral domain. Need to show every nonzero element has a multiplicative inverse. Let a ∈ R, a ≠ 0. Define φ: R → R by φ(x) = ax. Since R is an integral domain (no zero divisors), ax = ay implies a(x-y) = 0, so x = y (since a ≠ 0). Thus φ is injective. Since R is finite, injective implies surjective. So there exists b ∈ R with φ(b) = ab = 1. Thus a has an inverse. Since a was arbitrary nonzero, R is a field.
**Skills Tested:** Ring theory, integral domains, fields, finiteness arguments.

**ID:** AAD-203
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Abstract Algebra — Field Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Find the degree of the splitting field of x⁴ - 2 over Q, and determine the Galois group.
**Expected Answer:** Roots of x⁴ - 2 are ±∜2, ±i∜2. Splitting field K = Q(∜2, i). [Q(∜2):Q] = 4 (x⁴ - 2 is irreducible by Eisenstein with p=2). Since i ∉ Q(∜2) ⊂ ℝ, [Q(∜2, i):Q(∜2)] = 2 (minimal polynomial of i is x² + 1). So [K:Q] = 4 × 2 = 8. Galois group G = Gal(K/Q) has order 8. Automorphisms determined by σ(∜2) ∈ {∜2, -∜2, i∜2, -i∜2} and σ(i) ∈ {i, -i}. Let σ: ∜2 ↦ i∜2, i ↦ i and τ: ∜2 ↦ ∜2, i ↦ -i. Then σ⁴ = id, τ² = id, and τστ = σ⁻¹ (check: τστ(∜2) = τσ(∜2) = τ(i∜2) = -i∜2 = σ⁻¹(∜2); τστ(i) = τσ(-i) = τ(-i) = i = σ⁻¹(i)). So G ≅ D₄ (dihedral group of order 8).
**Skills Tested:** Field extensions, Galois theory, splitting fields.

**ID:** AAD-204
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Abstract Algebra — Module Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** State the Structure Theorem for Finitely Generated Modules over a PID. Use it to classify all abelian groups of order 360.
**Expected Answer:** The 6 groups are direct products of the 2, 3, and 5 parts: (Z₈ or Z₄⊕Z₂ or Z₂³) × (Z₉ or Z₃²) × Z₅.
**Skills Tested:** Module theory, structure theorem, classification of finite abelian groups.

**ID:** AAD-205
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Abstract Algebra — Group Actions
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Let G be a finite group acting on a finite set X. State the Orbit-Stabilizer Theorem and Burnside's Lemma. Use them to count the number of distinct colorings of a cube's faces with 3 colors (up to rotation).
**Expected Answer:** (729 + 6·27 + 3·81 + 8·9 + 6·27)/24 = (729 + 162 + 243 + 72 + 162)/24 = 1368/24 = 57.
**Skills Tested:** Group actions, counting under symmetry, Burnside's lemma.

**ID:** AAD-206
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Abstract Algebra — Polynomial Rings
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Determine whether the polynomial x⁴ + 1 is irreducible over Q. If not, factor it.
**Expected Answer:** x⁴ + 1 has no rational roots (roots are primitive 8th roots of unity: e^{iπ/4}, e^{3iπ/4}, etc., all complex). Could factor as product of two quadratics: x⁴ + 1 = (x² + ax + b)(x² + cx + d). Expanding: x⁴ + (a+c)x³ + (ac+b+d)x² + (ad+bc)x + bd. Matching: a+c = 0, ac+b+d = 0, ad+bc = 0, bd = 1. From a+c = 0: c = -a. From bd = 1: d = 1/b. From ad+bc = 0: a/b - ab = 0, so a(1/b - b) = 0. Either a = 0 or b = ±1. If a = 0: c = 0, b+d = 0, but bd = 1 gives b = -d and -b² = 1, impossible over R. If b = 1: d = 1, ac+b+d = -a²+2 = 0, so a² = 2, a = √2 ∉ Q. If b = -1: d = -1, -a²-2 = 0, impossible. So x⁴ + 1 is irreducible over Q. (It factors over R as (x² + √2x + 1)(x² - √2x + 1).)
**Skills Tested:** Polynomial irreducibility, factorization, field extensions.

**ID:** AAD-207
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Abstract Algebra — Sylow Theorems
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Let G be a group of order 30. Prove G has a normal subgroup of order 15.
**Expected Answer:** |G| = 30 = 2 · 3 · 5. By Sylow: n₅ | 6 and n₅ ≡ 1 (mod 5), so n₅ ∈ {1, 6}. n₃ | 10 and n₃ ≡ 1 (mod 3), so n₃ ∈ {1, 10}. Suppose n₅ = 6 and n₃ = 10. Then G has 6 · 4 = 24 elements of order 5 and 10 · 2 = 20 elements of order 3. Total: 24 + 20 = 44 > 30, contradiction. So n₅ = 1 or n₃ = 1 (or both). Case 1: n₅ = 1. Unique Sylow 5-subgroup P₅ ⊲ G, |P₅| = 5. Case 2: n₃ = 1. Unique Sylow 3-subgroup P₃ ⊲ G, |P₃| = 3. In either case, G has a normal subgroup N of order 3 or 5. If N has order 5: G/N has order 6. Groups of order 6 are Z₆ or S₃. In either case, G/N has a normal subgroup of order 3 (since index 2 subgroups are normal). Its preimage in G is normal of order 15. If N has order 3: G/N has order 10. Groups of order 10 are Z₁₀ or D₅. Both have normal subgroup of order 5. Its preimage is normal of order 15 in G.
**Skills Tested:** Sylow theorems, normal subgroups, group structure.

**ID:** AAD-208
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Abstract Algebra — Homomorphisms
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** State the First Isomorphism Theorem for groups. Use it to prove that (Z × Z) / ⟨(2,2)⟩ ≅ Z × Z₂.
**Expected Answer:** First Isomorphism Theorem: If φ: G → H is a group homomorphism, then G/ker(φ) ≅ im(φ). Define φ: Z × Z → Z × Z₂ by φ(a,b) = (a-b, b mod 2). Check homomorphism: φ((a,b)+(c,d)) = φ(a+c, b+d) = (a+c-b-d, (b+d) mod 2) = (a-b, b mod 2) + (c-d, d mod 2) = φ(a,b) + φ(c,d). Surjective: for any (m, n̄) ∈ Z × Z₂, need (a,b) with a-b = m and b ≡ n (mod 2). Choose b = n, a = m+n. Then φ(m+n, n) = (m, n̄). Kernel: φ(a,b) = (0, 0̄) means a-b = 0 and b ≡ 0 (mod 2), so a = b and b is even. Thus ker(φ) = {(2k, 2k) : k ∈ Z} = ⟨(2,2)⟩. By First Isomorphism Theorem: (Z × Z) / ⟨(2,2)⟩ ≅ Z × Z₂.
**Skills Tested:** Group homomorphisms, isomorphism theorems, quotient groups.

**ID:** AAD-209
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Abstract Algebra — Vector Spaces
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Prove that any two bases of a vector space V have the same cardinality (dimension is well-defined).
**Expected Answer:** For finite-dimensional case: Let B₁ = {v₁, ..., vₙ} and B₂ = {w₁, ..., wₘ} be bases. Since B₁ spans and B₂ is linearly independent, Steinitz Exchange Lemma gives m ≤ n. Since B₂ spans and B₁ is linearly independent, n ≤ m. Thus m = n. For infinite-dimensional case: Let B₁, B₂ be bases. Each element of B₁ is a finite linear combination of elements of B₂. For each b ∈ B₁, let S(b) ⊂ B₂ be the finite set of basis elements used. Then B₂ = ∪_{b∈B₁} S(b) (since B₂ is spanned by B₁, each element of B₂ uses finitely many from B₁, each of which uses finitely many from B₂... actually need more careful argument). Better: if B₁ is infinite, |B₂| ≤ |finite subsets of B₁| · ℵ₀ = |B₁| · ℵ₀ = |B₁|. By symmetry, |B₁| ≤ |B₂|. By Cantor-Schröder-Bernstein, |B₁| = |B₂|.
**Skills Tested:** Linear algebra, basis, cardinality, Zorn's lemma.

**ID:** AAD-210
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Abstract Algebra — Quotient Structures
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Let R = Z[x] and I = ⟨2, x⟩. Prove that R/I ≅ Z₂ and that I is a maximal ideal.
**Expected Answer:** Define φ: Z[x] → Z₂ by φ(f(x)) = f(0) mod 2 (evaluate at 0, reduce mod 2). Check homomorphism: φ(f+g) = (f+g)(0) mod 2 = f(0)+g(0) mod 2 = φ(f)+φ(g). φ(fg) = f(0)g(0) mod 2 = φ(f)φ(g). Surjective: φ(0) = 0, φ(1) = 1. Kernel: φ(f) = 0 means f(0) ≡ 0 (mod 2), so constant term is even. Write f(x) = a₀ + a₁x + ... + aₙxⁿ with a₀ = 2k. Then f = 2k + x(a₁ + ... + aₙx^{n-1}) ∈ ⟨2, x⟩. Conversely, any element of ⟨2, x⟩ is 2g(x) + xh(x), which has even constant term. So ker(φ) = ⟨2, x⟩ = I. By First Isomorphism Theorem: Z[x]/I ≅ Z₂. Since Z₂ is a field, I is maximal (ideal is maximal iff quotient is field).
**Skills Tested:** Ring homomorphisms, quotient rings, maximal ideals.

### COMPLEX ANALYSIS (Questions 211-218)

**ID:** AAD-211
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Complex Analysis — Contour Integration
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Evaluate ∫_{-∞}^{∞} dx/(x² + 1)² using contour integration.
**Expected Answer:** Consider ∮ dz/(z²+1)² over semicircular contour in upper half-plane: real axis from -R to R, then semicircle C_R. Poles of 1/(z²+1)² = 1/((z+i)²(z-i)²) at z = ±i, both order 2. Only z = i is in upper half-plane. Residue at z = i: since order 2, Res = lim(z→i) d/dz [(z-i)²/(z²+1)²] = lim(z→i) d/dz [1/(z+i)²] = lim(z→i) -2/(z+i)³ = -2/(2i)³ = -2/(-8i) = 1/(4i). By residue theorem: ∮ = 2πi · 1/(4i) = π/2. As R → ∞, integral over C_R → 0 (|1/(z²+1)²| ~ 1/R⁴, length ~ πR, so integral ~ π/R³ → 0). Thus ∫_{-∞}^{∞} dx/(x²+1)² = π/2.
**Skills Tested:** Contour integration, residue calculus, higher-order poles.

**ID:** AAD-212
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Complex Analysis — Laurent Series
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Find the Laurent series of f(z) = 1/(z(z-1)) centered at z = 0, valid in 0 < |z| < 1 and in |z| > 1.
**Expected Answer:** Partial fractions: 1/(z(z-1)) = -1/z + 1/(z-1). For 0 < |z| < 1: -1/z is already a Laurent term. 1/(z-1) = -1/(1-z) = -Σ(n=0 to ∞) z^n (geometric series, valid for |z| < 1). So f(z) = -1/z - Σ(n=0 to ∞) z^n = -1/z - 1 - z - z² - ... For |z| > 1: -1/z stays. 1/(z-1) = 1/z · 1/(1-1/z) = (1/z)Σ(n=0 to ∞) (1/z)^n = Σ(n=0 to ∞) 1/z^{n+1} = 1/z + 1/z² + 1/z³ + ... (valid for |1/z| < 1, i.e., |z| > 1). So f(z) = -1/z + 1/z + 1/z² + 1/z³ + ... = 1/z² + 1/z³ + 1/z⁴ + ... = Σ(n=2 to ∞) 1/z^n.
**Skills Tested:** Laurent series, partial fractions, annular regions.

**ID:** AAD-213
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Complex Analysis — Conformal Mapping
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Find a conformal map from the upper half-plane to the unit disk. Verify it's bijective and conformal.
**Expected Answer:** Möbius transformation: w = (z-i)/(z+i). Check: maps i ↦ 0 (center of disk). For z real: |w| = |z-i|/|z+i|. Since z is real, z-i and z+i are complex conjugates, so |z-i| = |z+i|, giving |w| = 1. So real axis maps to unit circle. For z = i (upper half-plane): w = 0, inside disk. Since Möbius transformations are bijective on Riemann sphere and map circles/lines to circles/lines, and upper half-plane is connected component containing i (which maps to 0), the upper half-plane maps bijectively to unit disk. Conformal: w'(z) = [(z+i) - (z-i)]/(z+i)² = 2i/(z+i)² ≠ 0 for z ≠ -i. Since -i is not in upper half-plane, w is conformal there. Inverse: z = i(1+w)/(1-w), which maps disk to upper half-plane.
**Skills Tested:** Conformal mapping, Möbius transformations, bijectivity.

**ID:** AAD-214
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Complex Analysis — Analytic Continuation
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain analytic continuation. Show that the function defined by Σ(n=0 to ∞) z^n for |z| < 1 can be analytically continued to C \ {1}, and identify the continued function.
**Expected Answer:** Analytic continuation: extending the domain of an analytic function while preserving analyticity. For |z| < 1: f(z) = Σ z^n = 1/(1-z) (geometric series). The function g(z) = 1/(1-z) is analytic on C \ {1} and agrees with f on |z| < 1. By uniqueness of analytic continuation, g is the analytic continuation of f. To verify: f is analytic on |z| < 1, g is analytic on C \ {1}, they agree on the open set |z| < 1 (which has accumulation points), so by identity theorem they define the same analytic function. The continued function g has a simple pole at z = 1, which is a natural boundary for the original series (series diverges for |z| ≥ 1).
**Skills Tested:** Analytic continuation, identity theorem, singularities.

**ID:** AAD-215
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Complex Analysis — Argument Principle
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Use the argument principle to determine how many zeros of f(z) = z⁴ + 3z² + 1 lie in the first quadrant.
**Expected Answer:** Zeros of z⁴ + 3z² + 1 = 0: let w = z², then w² + 3w + 1 = 0, so w = (-3 ± √5)/2. Both roots are negative real: w₁ = (-3+√5)/2 ≈ -0.38, w₂ = (-3-√5)/2 ≈ -2.62. So z² = w₁ or z² = w₂. For z² = w₁ < 0: z = ±i√|w₁| = ±i√0.38 ≈ ±0.62i. For z² = w₂ < 0: z = ±i√|w₂| = ±i√2.62 ≈ ±1.62i. All four zeros are on the imaginary axis: ±0.62i, ±1.62i. None are in the first quadrant (which requires Re(z) > 0 and Im(z) > 0). So answer: 0 zeros in first quadrant. Alternatively, by argument principle: track arg(f(z)) along boundary of first quadrant (positive real axis, quarter circle, positive imaginary axis). On positive real axis: f(x) = x⁴+3x²+1 > 0, arg = 0. On quarter circle |z|=R: f(z) ≈ z⁴, arg changes by 4 · π/2 = 2π. On positive imaginary axis: z = iy, f(iy) = y⁴ - 3y² + 1. This is real, changes sign... need to track carefully. But direct computation shows all zeros are on imaginary axis, so 0 in first quadrant.
**Skills Tested:** Argument principle, zero counting, complex roots.

**ID:** AAD-216
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Complex Analysis — Residue Theorem Applications
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Evaluate ∫₀^{2π} dθ/(2 + cos θ) using contour integration.
**Expected Answer:** Let z = e^{iθ}, so dθ = dz/(iz) and cos θ = (z + 1/z)/2. Integral becomes ∮_{|z|=1} [1/(2 + (z+1/z)/2)] · dz/(iz) = ∮ dz/[iz(2 + z/2 + 1/(2z))] = ∮ dz/[i(2z + z²/2 + 1/2)] = (2/i) ∮ dz/(z² + 4z + 1). Poles: z² + 4z + 1 = 0 → z = (-4 ± √12)/2 = -2 ± √3. z₁ = -2 + √3 ≈ -0.27 (inside unit circle), z₂ = -2 - √3 ≈ -3.73 (outside). Residue at z₁: 1/(z₁ - z₂) = 1/(2√3). Integral = (2/i) · 2πi · 1/(2√3) = 4π/(2√3) = 2π/√3 = 2π√3/3.
**Skills Tested:** Contour integration, trigonometric integrals, residue theorem.

**ID:** AAD-217
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Complex Analysis — Maximum Modulus Principle
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** State the Maximum Modulus Principle. Use it to prove that if f is analytic and non-constant on a bounded domain D, continuous on D̄, then |f| attains its maximum on ∂D.
**Expected Answer:** Maximum Modulus Principle: If f is analytic and non-constant on domain D, then |f| has no local maximum in D. Proof of boundary maximum: Since f is continuous on compact set D̄, |f| attains its maximum somewhere in D̄ (extreme value theorem). Suppose maximum is at z₀ ∈ D (interior). By Maximum Modulus Principle, since f is non-constant and analytic, |f| cannot have local maximum at z₀ — contradiction. Thus maximum must be on ∂D. (Note: minimum of |f| can be in interior if f has zeros there, by minimum modulus principle.)
**Skills Tested:** Complex analysis, maximum principle, proof techniques.

**ID:** AAD-218
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Complex Analysis — Schwarz Lemma
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** State and prove the Schwarz Lemma. Use it to show that any automorphism of the unit disk fixing 0 is a rotation.
**Expected Answer:** Schwarz Lemma: If f: D → D is analytic, f(0) = 0, then |f(z)| ≤ |z| for all z ∈ D and |f'(0)| ≤ 1. If equality holds for some z ≠ 0 or |f'(0)| = 1, then f(z) = e^{iα}z (rotation). Proof: Define g(z) = f(z)/z for z ≠ 0, g(0) = f'(0). Since f(0) = 0, f has zero at 0, so f(z)/z is analytic (removable singularity). For |z| = r < 1: |f(z)| < 1 (since f maps to D), so |g(z)| < 1/r. By maximum modulus on |z| ≤ r: |g(z)| ≤ 1/r for |z| ≤ r. Let r → 1: |g(z)| ≤ 1 for all z ∈ D. Thus |f(z)| ≤ |z|. Also |f'(0)| = |g(0)| ≤ 1. If |g(z₀)| = 1 for some z₀ ≠ 0, by maximum modulus g is constant: g(z) = e^{iα}, so f(z) = e^{iα}z. Automorphism of disk fixing 0: f: D → D bijective, analytic, f(0) = 0. By Schwarz, |f(z)| ≤ |z|. Since f⁻¹ also fixes 0, |f⁻¹(w)| ≤ |w|. Let w = f(z): |z| ≤ |f(z)|. Thus |f(z)| = |z|, so by Schwarz f(z) = e^{iα}z.
**Skills Tested:** Schwarz lemma, automorphisms, maximum principle.

### TOPOLOGY

**ID:** AAD-219
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Topology — Fundamental Group
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Compute the fundamental group of the torus T² = S¹ × S¹ using the Seifert-van Kampen theorem.
**Expected Answer:** T² = S¹ × S¹. Use CW structure: one 0-cell, two 1-cells (a, b), one 2-cell. Alternatively, use Seifert-van Kampen. Decompose T² as union of two open sets: U = T² minus a point in the 2-cell, V = small disk around that point. U deformation retracts to the 1-skeleton (wedge of two circles S¹ ∨ S¹), so π₁(U) = F₂ = ⟨a, b⟩ (free group on 2 generators). V is contractible, π₁(V) = 0. U ∩ V is homotopy equivalent to S¹ (circle around the puncture), π₁(U ∩ V) = Z = ⟨c⟩. The inclusion U ∩ V → U sends c to the boundary of the 2-cell, which is aba⁻¹b⁻¹ (commutator). By Seifert-van Kampen: π₁(T²) = π₁(U) *_{π₁(U∩V)} π₁(V) = ⟨a, b | aba⁻¹b⁻¹ = 1⟩ = ⟨a, b | ab = ba⟩ = Z × Z.
**Skills Tested:** Algebraic topology, fundamental group, Seifert-van Kampen theorem.

**ID:** AAD-220
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Topology — Homology
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Compute the singular homology groups H_n(T²) of the torus using the CW structure and cellular homology.
**Expected Answer:** CW structure: one 0-cell (v), two 1-cells (a, b), one 2-cell (e). Cellular chain complex: 0 → C₂ → C₁ → C₀ → 0 where C₀ = Z⟨v⟩ ≅ Z, C₁ = Z⟨a,b⟩ ≅ Z², C₂ = Z⟨e⟩ ≅ Z. Boundary maps: ∂₁(a) = v - v = 0, ∂₁(b) = 0 (both 1-cells are loops). So ∂₁ = 0. ∂₂(e) = a + b - a - b = 0 (boundary of 2-cell traverses a, b, a⁻¹, b⁻¹). So ∂₂ = 0. All boundary maps are zero. Thus H₀ = ker ∂₀ / im ∂₁ = Z / 0 = Z. H₁ = ker ∂₁ / im ∂₂ = Z² / 0 = Z². H₂ = ker ∂₂ / im ∂₃ = Z / 0 = Z. H_n = 0 for n ≥ 3. So H_n(T²) = Z for n=0,2; Z² for n=1; 0 otherwise.
**Skills Tested:** Algebraic topology, cellular homology, CW complexes.

**ID:** AAD-221
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Topology — Covering Spaces
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Classify all covering spaces of S¹ ∨ S¹ (figure-eight) corresponding to subgroups of index 2 in π₁(S¹ ∨ S¹) = F₂.
**Expected Answer:** π₁(S¹ ∨ S¹) = F₂ = ⟨a, b⟩ (free group on 2 generators). Subgroups of index 2 correspond to surjective homomorphisms F₂ → Z₂. Such homomorphisms are determined by where a, b map: each can go to 0 or 1 in Z₂, but not both to 0 (otherwise not surjective). So 3 homomorphisms: (a↦1, b↦0), (a↦0, b↦1), (a↦1, b↦1). Each has kernel of index 2. Covering spaces: 2-sheeted covers of figure-eight. For φ(a)=1, φ(b)=0: kernel consists of words with even total power of a. Covering space: 2 vertices, with a-edges swapping vertices, b-edges as loops at each vertex. Similarly for other homomorphisms. All three covering spaces are graphs with 2 vertices and 4 edges (2 lifts of each loop). They're non-isomorphic as labeled graphs but homeomorphic as topological spaces (all are graphs with Euler characteristic -2, so homotopy equivalent to wedge of 3 circles).
**Skills Tested:** Covering space theory, fundamental group, graph covers.

**ID:** AAD-222
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Topology — Compactness
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Prove that the continuous image of a compact space is compact.
**Expected Answer:** Let f: X → Y be continuous, X compact. Let {V_α} be an open cover of f(X). Since f is continuous, {f⁻¹(V_α)} is an open cover of X. By compactness of X, finite subcover exists: X = f⁻¹(V_{α₁}) ∪ ... ∪ f⁻¹(V_{αₙ}). Then f(X) = f(∪ f⁻¹(V_{αᵢ})) = ∪ f(f⁻¹(V_{αᵢ})) ⊆ ∪ V_{αᵢ}. So {V_{α₁}, ..., V_{αₙ}} is a finite subcover of f(X). Thus f(X) is compact.
**Skills Tested:** Topology, compactness, continuous functions.

**ID:** AAD-223
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Topology — Connectedness
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Prove that the continuous image of a connected space is connected.
**Expected Answer:** Let f: X → Y be continuous, X connected. Suppose f(X) is disconnected: f(X) = A ∪ B where A, B are nonempty, disjoint, open in f(X). Then f⁻¹(A) and f⁻¹(B) are open in X (by continuity), nonempty (since A, B ⊆ f(X)), disjoint, and f⁻¹(A) ∪ f⁻¹(B) = f⁻¹(A ∪ B) = f⁻¹(f(X)) = X. This gives a separation of X, contradicting connectedness. Thus f(X) is connected.
**Skills Tested:** Topology, connectedness, continuous functions.

**ID:** AAD-224
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Topology — Homeomorphisms
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Prove that [0,1) and [0,1] are not homeomorphic.
**Expected Answer:** [0,1] minus any point is disconnected (if interior point) or connected (if endpoint). [0,1) minus 0 is (0,1) connected, minus interior point is disconnected. So they have different topological properties.
**Skills Tested:** Topological invariants, compactness, homeomorphisms.

**ID:** AAD-225
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Topology — Metric Spaces
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Prove that every compact metric space is complete and totally bounded.
**Expected Answer:** Complete: let (x_n) be Cauchy in compact metric space X. By compactness (sequential), (x_n) has convergent subsequence x_{n_k} → x. Since (x_n) is Cauchy and has convergent subsequence, (x_n) itself converges to x. Thus X is complete. Totally bounded: suppose not. Then ∃ε > 0 such that no finite ε-net exists. Build sequence: pick x₁. Since {x₁} is not ε-net, ∃x₂ with d(x₂, x₁) ≥ ε. Since {x₁, x₂} not ε-net, ∃x₃ with d(x₃, x₁) ≥ ε and d(x₃, x₂) ≥ ε. Continue: get sequence (x_n) with d(x_n, x_m) ≥ ε for all n ≠ m. No subsequence can be Cauchy (since terms are ε-apart), contradicting sequential compactness. Thus X is totally bounded.
**Skills Tested:** Metric spaces, compactness, completeness, total boundedness.

### ADVANCED CALCULUS / MULTIVARIABLE

**ID:** AAD-226
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Multivariable Calculus — Multiple Integrals
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Evaluate ∫∫∫_E z dV where E is bounded by z = 0, z = √(1-x²-y²), and z = 0 (upper hemisphere of unit sphere).
**Expected Answer:** Use spherical coordinates: x = ρ sin φ cos θ, y = ρ sin φ sin θ, z = ρ cos φ, dV = ρ² sin φ dρ dφ dθ. Hemisphere: 0 ≤ ρ ≤ 1, 0 ≤ φ ≤ π/2, 0 ≤ θ ≤ 2π. Integral: ∫₀^{2π} ∫₀^{π/2} ∫₀¹ (ρ cos φ) ρ² sin φ dρ dφ dθ = ∫₀^{2π} dθ · ∫₀^{π/2} cos φ sin φ dφ · ∫₀¹ ρ³ dρ = 2π · [sin²φ/2]₀^{π/2} · [ρ⁴/4]₀¹ = 2π · (1/2) · (1/4) = π/4.
**Skills Tested:** Triple integrals, spherical coordinates, multivariable calculus.

**ID:** AAD-227
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Multivariable Calculus — Line Integrals
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Evaluate ∫_C F · dr where F(x,y) = (y², 2xy) and C is the parabola y = x² from (0,0) to (1,1). Then verify using the Fundamental Theorem for Line Integrals.
**Expected Answer:** Direct computation: parametrize C as r(t) = (t, t²), 0 ≤ t ≤ 1. dr = (1, 2t) dt. F(r(t)) = (t⁴, 2t³). F · dr = t⁴ · 1 + 2t³ · 2t = t⁴ + 4t⁴ = 5t⁴. Integral: ∫₀¹ 5t⁴ dt = [t⁵]₀¹ = 1. Check if F is conservative: ∂/∂y (y²) = 2y, ∂/∂x (2xy) = 2y. Equal, so F is conservative on simply connected domain. Find potential: ∂f/∂x = y² → f = xy² + g(y). ∂f/∂y = 2xy + g'(y) = 2xy → g'(y) = 0 → g = C. So f(x,y) = xy². By FT for line integrals: ∫_C F · dr = f(1,1) - f(0,0) = 1 - 0 = 1. ✓
**Skills Tested:** Line integrals, conservative fields, potential functions.

**ID:** AAD-228
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Multivariable Calculus — Surface Integrals
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Evaluate ∬_S F · dS where F(x,y,z) = (x, y, z) and S is the upper hemisphere x² + y² + z² = 1, z ≥ 0, oriented outward.
**Expected Answer:** Parametrize S: r(φ,θ) = (sin φ cos θ, sin φ sin θ, cos φ), 0 ≤ φ ≤ π/2, 0 ≤ θ ≤ 2π. r_φ = (cos φ cos θ, cos φ sin θ, -sin φ), r_θ = (-sin φ sin θ, sin φ cos θ, 0). r_φ × r_θ = (sin²φ cos θ, sin²φ sin θ, sin φ cos φ). This points outward (positive z-component for φ < π/2). F(r(φ,θ)) = (sin φ cos θ, sin φ sin θ, cos φ). F · (r_φ × r_θ) = sin³φ cos²θ + sin³φ sin²θ + sin φ cos²φ = sin³φ + sin φ cos²φ = sin φ(sin²φ + cos²φ) = sin φ. Integral: ∫₀^{2π} ∫₀^{π/2} sin φ dφ dθ = 2π · [-cos φ]₀^{π/2} = 2π · (0 - (-1)) = 2π. Alternatively, by Divergence Theorem: ∇ · F = 3. Volume of hemisphere = 2π/3. But need to include bottom disk. ∬_S F · dS + ∬_{bottom} F · dS = ∭ 3 dV = 3 · 2π/3 = 2π. On bottom disk (z=0): F = (x,y,0), normal = (0,0,-1), F · n = 0. So ∬_S F · dS = 2π. ✓
**Skills Tested:** Surface integrals, parametrization, Divergence Theorem.

**ID:** AAD-229
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Multivariable Calculus — Green's Theorem
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Use Green's Theorem to evaluate ∮_C (x²y dx + xy² dy) where C is the circle x² + y² = 4, counterclockwise.
**Expected Answer:** Green's Theorem: ∮_C P dx + Q dy = ∬_D (∂Q/∂x - ∂P/∂y) dA. Here P = x²y, Q = xy². ∂Q/∂x = y², ∂P/∂y = x². So ∂Q/∂x - ∂P/∂y = y² - x². D is disk x² + y² ≤ 4. Integral: ∬_D (y² - x²) dA. Use polar: x = r cos θ, y = r sin θ, dA = r dr dθ. y² - x² = r²(sin²θ - cos²θ) = -r² cos 2θ. Integral: ∫₀^{2π} ∫₀² (-r² cos 2θ) r dr dθ = ∫₀^{2π} cos 2θ dθ · ∫₀² (-r³) dr = [sin 2θ/2]₀^{2π} · [-r⁴/4]₀² = 0 · (-4) = 0. (The θ integral is 0 since cos 2θ integrates to 0 over full period.)
**Skills Tested:** Green's Theorem, double integrals, polar coordinates.

**ID:** AAD-230
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Multivariable Calculus — Stokes' Theorem
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Use Stokes' Theorem to evaluate ∮_C F · dr where F(x,y,z) = (y², z², x²) and C is the boundary of the surface S: z = 1 - x² - y², z ≥ 0, oriented counterclockwise when viewed from above.
**Expected Answer:** Stokes' Theorem: ∮_C F · dr = ∬_S (∇ × F) · dS. Compute curl: ∇ × F = (∂/∂y(x²) - ∂/∂z(z²), ∂/∂z(y²) - ∂/∂x(x²), ∂/∂x(z²) - ∂/∂y(y²)) = (0 - 2z, 0 - 2x, 0 - 2y) = (-2z, -2x, -2y). Parametrize S: r(x,y) = (x, y, 1-x²-y²) for x²+y² ≤ 1. r_x = (1, 0, -2x), r_y = (0, 1, -2y). r_x × r_y = (2x, 2y, 1) (points upward). (∇ × F) · (r_x × r_y) = (-2z)(2x) + (-2x)(2y) + (-2y)(1) = -4xz - 4xy - 2y. Substitute z = 1-x²-y²: = -4x(1-x²-y²) - 4xy - 2y = -4x + 4x³ + 4xy² - 4xy - 2y. Integral over disk x²+y² ≤ 1: by symmetry, odd powers of x or y integrate to 0. Terms -4x, -4xy, -2y all odd → 0. Term 4x³ odd → 0. Term 4xy² odd in x → 0. So integral = 0. Thus ∮_C F · dr = 0.
**Skills Tested:** Stokes' Theorem, curl, surface integrals.

**ID:** AAD-231
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Multivariable Calculus — Divergence Theorem
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Use the Divergence Theorem to evaluate ∬_S F · dS where F(x,y,z) = (x³, y³, z³) and S is the sphere x² + y² + z² = 9, oriented outward.
**Expected Answer:** Divergence Theorem: ∬_S F · dS = ∭_E ∇ · F dV. ∇ · F = 3x² + 3y² + 3z² = 3(x² + y² + z²). E is ball x² + y² + z² ≤ 9. Use spherical: x²+y²+z² = ρ², dV = ρ² sin φ dρ dφ dθ. Integral: ∭ 3ρ² · ρ² sin φ dρ dφ dθ = 3 ∫₀^{2π} dθ ∫₀^π sin φ dφ ∫₀³ ρ⁴ dρ = 3 · 2π · 2 · [ρ⁵/5]₀³ = 3 · 2π · 2 · 243/5 = 2916π/5.
**Skills Tested:** Divergence Theorem, triple integrals, spherical coordinates.

**ID:** AAD-232
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Multivariable Calculus — Jacobians
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Evaluate ∫∫_R (x+y) dA where R is the region bounded by x+y = 1, x+y = 3, x-y = -1, x-y = 1 using the change of variables u = x+y, v = x-y.
**Expected Answer:** u = x+y, v = x-y. Solve: x = (u+v)/2, y = (u-v)/2. Jacobian: ∂(x,y)/∂(u,v) = |∂x/∂u ∂x/∂v; ∂y/∂u ∂y/∂v| = |1/2 1/2; 1/2 -1/2| = -1/2 - 1/4 = -1/2. |J| = 1/2. Region in uv-plane: 1 ≤ u ≤ 3, -1 ≤ v ≤ 1 (rectangle). Integrand: x+y = u. Integral: ∫₁³ ∫₋₁¹ u · (1/2) dv du = (1/2) ∫₁³ u du ∫₋₁¹ dv = (1/2) · [u²/2]₁³ · 2 = (1/2) · (9/2 - 1/2) · 2 = (1/2) · 4 · 2 = 4.
**Skills Tested:** Change of variables, Jacobians, double integrals.

**ID:** AAD-233
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Multivariable Calculus — Lagrange Multipliers
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Find the maximum and minimum of f(x,y,z) = xyz subject to the constraint x² + y² + z² = 1.
**Expected Answer:** for max, need xyz > 0, so all positive or two negative. For min, xyz < 0, so one negative or all negative.
**Skills Tested:** Lagrange multipliers, constrained optimization, multivariable calculus.

**ID:** AAD-234
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Multivariable Calculus — Taylor Series
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Find the second-order Taylor polynomial of f(x,y) = e^{x+y²} centered at (0,0).
**Expected Answer:** f(0,0) = e⁰ = 1. Partial derivatives: f_x = e^{x+y²}, f_y = 2y e^{x+y²}. f_x(0,0) = 1, f_y(0,0) = 0. Second derivatives: f_xx = e^{x+y²}, f_xy = 2y e^{x+y²}, f_yy = 2e^{x+y²} + 4y² e^{x+y²}. f_xx(0,0) = 1, f_xy(0,0) = 0, f_yy(0,0) = 2. Taylor polynomial: P₂(x,y) = f(0,0) + f_x(0,0)x + f_y(0,0)y + (1/2)[f_xx(0,0)x² + 2f_xy(0,0)xy + f_yy(0,0)y²] = 1 + x + 0 + (1/2)[x² + 0 + 2y²] = 1 + x + x²/2 + y².
**Skills Tested:** Multivariable Taylor series, partial derivatives, approximation.

**ID:** AAD-235
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Multivariable Calculus — Implicit Function Theorem
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Let F(x,y,z) = x² + y² + z² - 1 = 0 define z implicitly as a function of (x,y) near (0,0,1). Use the Implicit Function Theorem to find ∂z/∂x and ∂z/∂y at (0,0,1).
**Expected Answer:** Implicit Function Theorem: if F(x₀,y₀,z₀) = 0 and F_z(x₀,y₀,z₀) ≠ 0, then z = f(x,y) locally with ∂z/∂x = -F_x/F_z, ∂z/∂y = -F_y/F_z. F_x = 2x, F_y = 2y, F_z = 2z. At (0,0,1): F_x = 0, F_y = 0, F_z = 2 ≠ 0. So ∂z/∂x = -0/2 = 0, ∂z/∂y = -0/2 = 0. This makes sense: near (0,0,1), z ≈ √(1-x²-y²) ≈ 1 - (x²+y²)/2, so first-order terms vanish.
**Skills Tested:** Implicit Function Theorem, partial derivatives, multivariable calculus.

### DIFFERENTIAL EQUATIONS (Questions 236-243)

**ID:** AAD-236
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Differential Equations — Systems of ODEs
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Solve the system x' = 2x + y, y' = x + 2y with initial conditions x(0) = 1, y(0) = 0.
**Expected Answer:** Matrix form: [x'; y'] = [[2,1],[1,2]] [x; y]. Eigenvalues of A = [[2,1],[1,2]]: det(A-λI) = (2-λ)² - 1 = λ² - 4λ + 3 = (λ-1)(λ-3) = 0. λ₁ = 1, λ₂ = 3. Eigenvectors: λ₁=1: (A-I)v = [[1,1],[1,1]]v = 0, v₁ = (1,-1). λ₂=3: (A-3I)v = [[-1,1],[1,-1]]v = 0, v₂ = (1,1). General solution: [x; y] = c₁ e^t [1;-1] + c₂ e^{3t} [1;1]. Initial: [1;0] = c₁[1;-1] + c₂[1;1]. c₁ + c₂ = 1, -c₁ + c₂ = 0 → c₁ = c₂ = 1/2. Solution: x(t) = (1/2)e^t + (1/2)e^{3t}, y(t) = -(1/2)e^t + (1/2)e^{3t}.
**Skills Tested:** Systems of ODEs, eigenvalues, matrix exponentials.

**ID:** AAD-237
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Differential Equations — Laplace Transforms
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Solve y'' + 4y = δ(t-1) with y(0) = 0, y'(0) = 0 using Laplace transforms.
**Expected Answer:** Take Laplace: L{y''} + 4L{y} = L{δ(t-1)}. s²Y - sy(0) - y'(0) + 4Y = e^{-s}. (s²+4)Y = e^{-s}. Y = e^{-s}/(s²+4). Inverse Laplace: L⁻¹{1/(s²+4)} = (1/2)sin(2t). By time-shifting: L⁻¹{e^{-s}/(s²+4)} = (1/2)sin(2(t-1)) · u(t-1) where u is Heaviside step function. So y(t) = (1/2)sin(2(t-1)) for t ≥ 1, and y(t) = 0 for t < 1.
**Skills Tested:** Laplace transforms, Dirac delta, time-shifting.

**ID:** AAD-238
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Differential Equations — Phase Portraits
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Classify the equilibrium point at the origin for the system x' = x - y, y' = x + y. Determine stability and sketch the phase portrait.
**Expected Answer:** Matrix A = [[1,-1],[1,1]]. Eigenvalues: det(A-λI) = (1-λ)² + 1 = λ² - 2λ + 2 = 0. λ = (2 ± √(4-8))/2 = 1 ± i. Complex eigenvalues with positive real part (Re(λ) = 1 > 0). Equilibrium is an unstable spiral (spiral source). Solutions spiral outward from origin. Phase portrait: trajectories spiral counterclockwise (check: at (1,0), x'=1>0, y'=1>0, so motion is up and right) away from origin.
**Skills Tested:** Phase portraits, stability analysis, eigenvalues.

**ID:** AAD-239
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Differential Equations — Power Series Solutions
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Find the power series solution of y'' - xy = 0 (Airy equation) about x = 0 with y(0) = 1, y'(0) = 0.
**Expected Answer:** Let y = Σ(n=0 to ∞) aₙ xⁿ. y'' = Σ(n=2 to ∞) n(n-1)aₙ x^{n-2} = Σ(n=0 to ∞) (n+2)(n+1)a_{n+2} xⁿ. xy = Σ(n=0 to ∞) aₙ x^{n+1} = Σ(n=1 to ∞) a_{n-1} xⁿ. Equation: Σ(n=0 to ∞) (n+2)(n+1)a_{n+2} xⁿ - Σ(n=1 to ∞) a_{n-1} xⁿ = 0. For n=0: 2a₂ = 0, so a₂ = 0. For n ≥ 1: (n+2)(n+1)a_{n+2} - a_{n-1} = 0, so a_{n+2} = a_{n-1}/[(n+2)(n+1)]. Recurrence: a₃ = a₀/(3·2) = 1/6, a₄ = a₁/(4·3) = 0, a₅ = a₂/(5·4) = 0, a₆ = a₃/(6·5) = 1/(6·30) = 1/180, etc. With y(0) = a₀ = 1, y'(0) = a₁ = 0: y(x) = 1 + x³/6 + x⁶/180 + ... (Airy function Ai(x) up to normalization).
**Skills Tested:** Power series solutions, recurrence relations, special functions.

**ID:** AAD-240
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Differential Equations — Boundary Value Problems
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Solve the boundary value problem y'' + λy = 0, y(0) = 0, y(π) = 0. Find all eigenvalues λ and corresponding eigenfunctions.
**Expected Answer:** Cases: λ < 0: let λ = -μ², μ > 0. y'' - μ²y = 0, y = A cosh(μx) + B sinh(μx). y(0) = A = 0. y(π) = B sinh(μπ) = 0. Since sinh(μπ) ≠ 0, B = 0. Trivial solution only. λ = 0: y'' = 0, y = Ax + B. y(0) = B = 0. y(π) = Aπ = 0, A = 0. Trivial. λ > 0: let λ = μ². y'' + μ²y = 0, y = A cos(μx) + B sin(μx). y(0) = A = 0. y(π) = B sin(μπ) = 0. Nontrivial: B ≠ 0, so sin(μπ) = 0, μπ = nπ, μ = n for n = 1, 2, 3, ... Eigenvalues: λₙ = n². Eigenfunctions: yₙ(x) = sin(nx) for n = 1, 2, 3, ...
**Skills Tested:** Sturm-Liouville theory, eigenvalue problems, boundary value problems.

**ID:** AAD-241
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Differential Equations — Partial Differential Equations
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Solve the wave equation u_tt = c² u_xx on 0 < x < L with boundary conditions u(0,t) = u(L,t) = 0 and initial conditions u(x,0) = f(x), u_t(x,0) = g(x).
**Expected Answer:** Separation of variables: u(x,t) = X(x)T(t). X T'' = c² X'' T, so T''/(c²T) = X''/X = -λ. X'' + λX = 0, X(0) = X(L) = 0 gives eigenvalues λₙ = (nπ/L)², eigenfunctions Xₙ = sin(nπx/L). T'' + c²λₙ T = 0, Tₙ(t) = Aₙ cos(nπct/L) + Bₙ sin(nπct/L). General solution: u(x,t) = Σ[n=1 to ∞] [Aₙ cos(nπct/L) + Bₙ sin(nπct/L)] sin(nπx/L). Initial conditions: u(x,0) = Σ Aₙ sin(nπx/L) = f(x), so Aₙ = (2/L)∫₀ᴸ f(x) sin(nπx/L) dx (Fourier sine coefficients). u_t(x,0) = Σ Bₙ (nπc/L) sin(nπx/L) = g(x), so Bₙ (nπc/L) = (2/L)∫₀ᴸ g(x) sin(nπx/L) dx, giving Bₙ = (2/(nπc))∫₀ᴸ g(x) sin(nπx/L) dx.
**Skills Tested:** PDEs, wave equation, separation of variables, Fourier series.

**ID:** AAD-242
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Differential Equations — Existence and Uniqueness
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** State the Picard-Lindelöf theorem. Use it to determine whether the IVP y' = y^{2/3}, y(0) = 0 has a unique solution.
**Expected Answer:** Picard-Lindelöf: If f(t,y) is continuous and Lipschitz in y on rectangle R containing (t₀, y₀), then y' = f(t,y), y(t₀) = y₀ has a unique solution locally. For y' = y^{2/3}, y(0) = 0: f(t,y) = y^{2/3} is continuous. Check Lipschitz: |f(y₁) - f(y₂)| = |y₁^{2/3} - y₂^{2/3}|. Near y = 0: derivative f'(y) = (2/3)y^{-1/3} → ∞ as y → 0. So f is not Lipschitz at y = 0. Theorem doesn't apply. Indeed, solution is not unique: y(t) = 0 is a solution, and y(t) = (t/3)³ = t³/27 is also a solution (check: y' = t²/9 = (t³/27)^{2/3} = y^{2/3} ✓, y(0) = 0 ✓). Non-uniqueness due to failure of Lipschitz condition.
**Skills Tested:** ODE theory, existence and uniqueness, Lipschitz condition.

**ID:** AAD-243
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Differential Equations — Numerical Methods
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Apply the fourth-order Runge-Kutta method to approximate y(0.2) for y' = y, y(0) = 1 with step size h = 0.1. Compare with exact solution.
**Expected Answer:** RK4: k₁ = hf(tₙ, yₙ), k₂ = hf(tₙ + h/2, yₙ + k₁/2), k₃ = hf(tₙ + h/2, yₙ + k₂/2), k₄ = hf(tₙ + h, yₙ + k₃). y_{n+1} = yₙ + (k₁ + 2k₂ + 2k₃ + k₄)/6. For y' = y: k₁ = 0.1yₙ, k₂ = 0.1(yₙ + 0.05yₙ) = 0.105yₙ, k₃ = 0.1(yₙ + 0.0525yₙ) = 0.10525yₙ, k₄ = 0.1(yₙ + 0.10525yₙ) = 0.110525yₙ. y_{n+1} = yₙ[1 + (0.1 + 0.21 + 0.2105 + 0.110525)/6] = yₙ[1 + 0.631025/6] = yₙ(1.105170833...). Step 1: y(0.1) ≈ 1.105170833. Step 2: y(0.2) ≈ 1.105170833² ≈ 1.22140. Exact: y(0.2) = e^{0.2} ≈ 1.22140. Excellent agreement (RK4 is very accurate for this smooth problem).
**Skills Tested:** Numerical ODEs, Runge-Kutta methods, error analysis.

### NUMBER THEORY (Questions 244-251)

**ID:** AAD-244
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Number Theory — Diophantine Equations
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Find all integer solutions to the equation x² + y² = z² (Pythagorean triples). Characterize the primitive solutions.
**Expected Answer:** Primitive solutions: gcd(x,y,z) = 1. One of x,y is even, other odd (can't both be odd since x²+y² ≡ 2 (mod 4) but z² ≡ 0 or 1). WLOG x even, y odd. x² = z² - y² = (z+y)(z-y). Since z,y both odd, z+y, z-y both even. Let z+y = 2u, z-y = 2v. x² = 4uv, so (x/2)² = uv. Since gcd(z+y, z-y) = 2 (as gcd(y,z) = 1), gcd(u,v) = 1. Since uv is perfect square and gcd(u,v) = 1, both u,v are perfect squares: u = m², v = n². Then x/2 = mn, y = u-v = m²-n², z = u+v = m²+n². Primitive Pythagorean triples: (x,y,z) = (2mn, m²-n², m²+n²) for m > n > 0, gcd(m,n) = 1, m-n odd. All solutions are multiples of primitive ones.
**Skills Tested:** Diophantine equations, Pythagorean triples, number theory.

**ID:** AAD-245
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Number Theory — Quadratic Reciprocity
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** State the Law of Quadratic Reciprocity. Use it to determine whether x² ≡ 17 (mod 97) has a solution.
**Expected Answer:** Quadratic Reciprocity: For odd primes p, q: (p/q)(q/p) = (-1)^{(p-1)/2 · (q-1)/2}. Equivalently: if p ≡ 1 or q ≡ 1 (mod 4), then (p/q) = (q/p). If p ≡ q ≡ 3 (mod 4), then (p/q) = -(q/p). Also supplementary laws: (-1/p) = (-1)^{(p-1)/2}, (2/p) = (-1)^{(p²-1)/8}. For x² ≡ 17 (mod 97): need (17/97). 17 ≡ 1 (mod 4), so (17/97) = (97/17). 97 = 5·17 + 12, so 97 ≡ 12 (mod 17). (97/17) = (12/17) = (4/17)(3/17) = (3/17) (since 4 is perfect square). 3 ≡ 3 (mod 4), 17 ≡ 1 (mod 4), so (3/17) = (17/3). 17 ≡ 2 (mod 3). (17/3) = (2/3) = (-1)^{(9-1)/8} = (-1)^1 = -1. So (17/97) = -1. Thus x² ≡ 17 (mod 97) has no solution.
**Skills Tested:** Quadratic reciprocity, Legendre symbol, number theory.

**ID:** AAD-246
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Number Theory — Euler's Theorem
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Find the last two digits of 7^{1000} (i.e., compute 7^{1000} mod 100).
**Expected Answer:** Need 7^{1000} mod 100. φ(100) = φ(4)φ(25) = 2·20 = 40. By Euler's theorem: 7^{40} ≡ 1 (mod 100) since gcd(7,100) = 1. 1000 = 25·40, so 7^{1000} = (7^{40})^{25} ≡ 1^{25} = 1 (mod 100). Last two digits: 01.
**Skills Tested:** Modular arithmetic, Euler's theorem, totient function.

**ID:** AAD-247
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Number Theory — Prime Number Theorem
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** State the Prime Number Theorem. Use it to estimate the number of primes less than 10⁶ and discuss the error term.
**Expected Answer:** Prime Number Theorem: π(x) ~ x/ln(x) as x → ∞, where π(x) is number of primes ≤ x. More precisely: lim(x→∞) π(x)/(x/ln(x)) = 1. Better approximation: π(x) ~ Li(x) = ∫₂ˣ dt/ln(t). For x = 10⁶: π(10⁶) ≈ 10⁶/ln(10⁶) = 10⁶/(6 ln 10) = 10⁶/13.816 ≈ 72,382. Actual value: π(10⁶) = 78,498. Error: about 8%. Better: Li(10⁶) ≈ 78,628, much closer. Error term: PNT with error says π(x) = Li(x) + O(x e^{-c√ln x}) for some c > 0. Under Riemann Hypothesis: π(x) = Li(x) + O(√x ln x).
**Skills Tested:** Analytic number theory, prime distribution, asymptotic analysis.

**ID:** AAD-248
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Number Theory — Continued Fractions
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Find the continued fraction expansion of √7 and use it to find the fundamental solution to x² - 7y² = 1 (Pell's equation).
**Expected Answer:** √7 ≈ 2.646. a₀ = ⌊√7⌋ = 2. √7 - 2 = (√7-2). 1/(√7-2) = (√7+2)/3. a₁ = ⌊(√7+2)/3⌋ = ⌊4.646/3⌋ = 1. (√7+2)/3 - 1 = (√7-1)/3. 3/(√7-1) = 3(√7+1)/6 = (√7+1)/2. a₂ = ⌊(√7+1)/2⌋ = ⌊3.646/2⌋ = 1. (√7+1)/2 - 1 = (√7-1)/2. 2/(√7-1) = 2(√7+1)/6 = (√7+1)/3. a₃ = ⌊(√7+1)/3⌋ = ⌊3.646/3⌋ = 1. (√7+1)/3 - 1 = (√7-2)/3. 3/(√7-2) = 3(√7+2)/3 = √7+2. a₄ = ⌊√7+2⌋ = 4. √7+2 - 4 = √7-2, back to start. So √7 = [2; 1, 1, 1, 4, 1, 1, 1, 4, ...] = [2; 1, 1, 1, 4̄]. Period length 4 (even). Convergents: p₀/q₀ = 2/1, p₁/q₁ = 3/1, p₂/q₂ = 5/2, p₃/q₃ = 8/3. Fundamental solution to x² - 7y² = 1: (x,y) = (p₃, q₃) = (8, 3). Check: 8² - 7·3² = 64 - 63 = 1. ✓
**Skills Tested:** Continued fractions, Pell's equation, quadratic irrationals.

**ID:** AAD-249
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Number Theory — Algebraic Number Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Prove that Z[√-5] is not a unique factorization domain by exhibiting an element with two distinct factorizations into irreducibles.
**Expected Answer:** In Z[√-5], norm N(a+b√-5) = a² + 5b². Consider 6 = 2·3 = (1+√-5)(1-√-5). Show these are distinct factorizations into irreducibles. N(2) = 4, N(3) = 9, N(1±√-5) = 6. If 2 = αβ with α,β non-units, then N(α)N(β) = 4, so N(α) = 2. But a²+5b² = 2 has no integer solutions. So 2 is irreducible. Similarly 3 is irreducible (N(α) = 3 impossible). For 1+√-5: if reducible, N(α) = 2 or 3, both impossible. So irreducible. Same for 1-√-5. Are 2 and 1+√-5 associates? Units in Z[√-5] have norm 1: a²+5b² = 1 gives a=±1, b=0, so units are ±1. 2 ≠ ±(1+√-5). So factorizations are genuinely distinct. Thus Z[√-5] is not a UFD.
**Skills Tested:** Algebraic number theory, unique factorization, norm arguments.

**ID:** AAD-250
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Number Theory — Modular Forms
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** State Ramanujan's congruence for the tau function: τ(n) ≡ σ₁₁(n) (mod 691). Explain the significance of 691.
**Expected Answer:** Ramanujan's tau function τ(n) is defined by Δ(z) = q Π(n=1 to ∞) (1-qⁿ)^{24} = Σ(n=1 to ∞) τ(n)qⁿ where q = e^{2πiz} and Δ is the discriminant modular form of weight 12. Ramanujan conjectured (proved by Mordell) that τ(n) ≡ σ₁₁(n) (mod 691) where σ₁₁(n) = Σ(d|n) d¹¹. Significance of 691: it's the numerator of the Bernoulli number B₁₂ = -691/2730. By von Staudt-Clausen theorem, primes dividing numerator of B₂ₖ are "irregular primes" related to special values of zeta functions. The congruence arises because Δ ≡ E₁₂ (mod 691) where E₁₂ is the Eisenstein series of weight 12, whose Fourier coefficients are proportional to σ₁₁(n). This connects modular forms to arithmetic and was precursor to deep connections between modular forms and Galois representations (leading to proof of Fermat's Last Theorem).
**Skills Tested:** Modular forms, number theory, arithmetic geometry.

**ID:** AAD-251
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Number Theory — Analytic Methods
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** State the Riemann Hypothesis and explain its connection to the distribution of prime numbers.
**Expected Answer:** Riemann Hypothesis (RH): All non-trivial zeros of the Riemann zeta function ζ(s) = Σ(n=1 to ∞) 1/nˢ (for Re(s) > 1, analytically continued) lie on the critical line Re(s) = 1/2. Connection to primes: Euler product ζ(s) = Π(p prime) 1/(1-p^{-s}) connects ζ to primes. Explicit formula: ψ(x) = Σ(n≤x) Λ(n) = x - Σ(ρ) x^ρ/ρ - ln(2π) - (1/2)ln(1-x^{-2}) where sum is over non-trivial zeros ρ. If RH holds, Re(ρ) = 1/2, so |x^ρ| = x^{1/2}, giving ψ(x) = x + O(x^{1/2} ln²x). This is equivalent to π(x) = Li(x) + O(√x ln x), optimal error term in prime number theorem. RH implies primes are as regularly distributed as possible. Many results in number theory are proved "assuming RH."
**Skills Tested:** Analytic number theory, zeta function, prime distribution.

### PROBABILITY THEORY 

**ID:** AAD-252
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Probability Theory — Measure-Theoretic Foundations
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** State the Kolmogorov axioms of probability. Prove that P(A ∪ B) = P(A) + P(B) - P(A ∩ B).
**Expected Answer:** Kolmogorov axioms: (1) P(A) ≥ 0 for all events A. (2) P(Ω) = 1 where Ω is sample space. (3) For countable sequence of pairwise disjoint events A₁, A₂, ...: P(∪Aᵢ) = ΣP(Aᵢ). Proof: Write A ∪ B = A ∪ (B \ A) where A and B \ A are disjoint. Also B = (A ∩ B) ∪ (B \ A) where these are disjoint. By axiom 3: P(A ∪ B) = P(A) + P(B \ A) and P(B) = P(A ∩ B) + P(B \ A). From second: P(B \ A) = P(B) - P(A ∩ B). Substitute into first: P(A ∪ B) = P(A) + P(B) - P(A ∩ B).
**Skills Tested:** Probability theory, measure theory, proof techniques.

**ID:** AAD-253
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Probability Theory — Law of Large Numbers
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** State and prove the Weak Law of Large Numbers for i.i.d. random variables with finite variance.
**Expected Answer:** WLLN: If X₁, X₂, ... are i.i.d. with E[Xᵢ] = μ and Var(Xᵢ) = σ² < ∞, then X̄ₙ = (1/n)ΣXᵢ converges in probability to μ: for all ε > 0, P(|X̄ₙ - μ| ≥ ε) → 0 as n → ∞. Proof: E[X̄ₙ] = μ. Var(X̄ₙ) = Var((1/n)ΣXᵢ) = (1/n²)ΣVar(Xᵢ) = (1/n²)·nσ² = σ²/n. By Chebyshev's inequality: P(|X̄ₙ - μ| ≥ ε) ≤ Var(X̄ₙ)/ε² = σ²/(nε²). As n → ∞, σ²/(nε²) → 0. Thus P(|X̄ₙ - μ| ≥ ε) → 0.
**Skills Tested:** Probability theory, convergence in probability, Chebyshev's inequality.

**ID:** AAD-254
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Probability Theory — Central Limit Theorem
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** State the Central Limit Theorem. Explain why it's fundamental in statistics and give an example of its application.
**Expected Answer:** CLT: If X₁, X₂, ... are i.i.d. with mean μ and variance σ² > 0, then Zₙ = (X̄ₙ - μ)/(σ/√n) converges in distribution to N(0,1): P(Zₙ ≤ z) → Φ(z) for all z, where Φ is standard normal CDF. Equivalently: √n(X̄ₙ - μ)/σ → N(0,1) in distribution. Fundamental because: (1) explains why normal distribution appears ubiquitously in nature (sums of many small independent effects), (2) justifies normal-based inference (confidence intervals, hypothesis tests) even when population isn't normal, (3) provides approximation for sums/averages. Example: polling — if survey n = 1000 people, sample proportion p̂ ≈ N(p, p(1-p)/n). 95% CI: p̂ ± 1.96√(p̂(1-p̂)/n). Works regardless of population distribution (binary in this case).
**Skills Tested:** Probability theory, statistical inference, asymptotic theory.

**ID:** AAD-255
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Probability Theory — Conditional Expectation
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Define conditional expectation E[X|Y] rigorously. Prove the tower property: E[E[X|Y]|Z] = E[X|Z] if Z is a function of Y (or more generally, if σ(Z) ⊆ σ(Y)).
**Expected Answer:** Conditional expectation: E[X|Y] is a σ(Y)-measurable random variable satisfying ∫_A E[X|Y] dP = ∫_A X dP for all A ∈ σ(Y). Equivalently, E[X|Y] = E[X|σ(Y)] is the Radon-Nikodym derivative of the signed measure ν(A) = ∫_A X dP restricted to σ(Y). Tower property: Let G = σ(Z) ⊆ F = σ(Y). Need to show E[E[X|F]|G] = E[X|G]. By definition, E[X|G] is G-measurable and ∫_A E[X|G] dP = ∫_A X dP for A ∈ G. Let W = E[X|F]. W is F-measurable and ∫_B W dP = ∫_B X dP for B ∈ F. Since G ⊆ F, for A ∈ G: ∫_A E[W|G] dP = ∫_A W dP (by definition of E[W|G]) = ∫_A X dP (since A ∈ F). So E[W|G] satisfies the defining properties of E[X|G]. By a.s. uniqueness of conditional expectation, E[W|G] = E[X|G] a.s.
**Skills Tested:** Measure-theoretic probability, conditional expectation, sigma-algebras.

**ID:** AAD-256
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Probability Theory — Martingales
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Define a martingale. State and prove the Optional Stopping Theorem for bounded stopping times.
**Expected Answer:** Martingale: sequence (Xₙ) adapted to filtration (Fₙ) with E[|Xₙ|] < ∞ and E[X_{n+1}|Fₙ] = Xₙ a.s. Optional Stopping Theorem (bounded case): If (Xₙ) is a martingale and T is a stopping time with T ≤ N a.s. (bounded), then E[X_T] = E[X₀]. Proof: X_T = X₀ + Σ(n=0 to N-1) (X_{n+1} - Xₙ) · 1(T > n). Note 1(T > n) is Fₙ-measurable (since {T > n} = {T ≤ n}^c ∈ Fₙ). Take expectation: E[X_T] = E[X₀] + Σ E[(X_{n+1} - Xₙ) · 1(T > n)]. By tower property: E[(X_{n+1} - Xₙ) · 1(T > n)] = E[E[(X_{n+1} - Xₙ) · 1(T > n)|Fₙ]] = E[1(T > n) · E[X_{n+1} - Xₙ|Fₙ]] = E[1(T > n) · 0] = 0. Thus E[X_T] = E[X₀].
**Skills Tested:** Martingale theory, stopping times, measure-theoretic probability.

**ID:** AAD-257
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Probability Theory — Markov Chains
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Define a Markov chain. Prove that for an irreducible, positive recurrent, aperiodic Markov chain, the stationary distribution π exists, is unique, and lim(n→∞) Pⁿ(i,j) = π(j) for all i,j.
**Expected Answer:** Markov chain: stochastic process (Xₙ) with Markov property: P(X_{n+1} = j | Xₙ = i, X_{n-1} = i_{n-1}, ...) = P(X_{n+1} = j | Xₙ = i) = P(i,j). Stationary distribution: πP = π, Σπ(i) = 1. Irreducible: all states communicate. Positive recurrent: expected return time to each state is finite. Aperiodic: gcd of return times is 1. Theorem (convergence): Under these conditions, unique stationary distribution π exists, and Pⁿ(i,j) → π(j) as n → ∞. Proof sketch: By irreducibility and positive recurrence, π(j) = 1/E[T_j] where T_j is return time to j. Uniqueness: if π, π' both stationary, use coupling argument — run two chains, one from stationary distribution, one from i. They eventually meet (by irreducibility), after which they're identical. So distributions converge. Aperiodicity ensures no periodic oscillations. Full proof requires renewal theory or coupling.
**Skills Tested:** Markov chains, stationary distributions, ergodic theory.

**ID:** AAD-258
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Probability Theory — Stochastic Processes
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Define Brownian motion. Prove that it has continuous paths but is nowhere differentiable (almost surely).
**Expected Answer:** Brownian motion (Wiener process): stochastic process (B_t)_{t≥0} with (1) B₀ = 0, (2) independent increments: B_t - B_s independent of F_s for t > s, (3) stationary Gaussian increments: B_t - B_s ~ N(0, t-s), (4) continuous paths. Continuity: by construction (Kolmogorov continuity theorem or explicit construction via Fourier series). Nowhere differentiable: for fixed t, lim(h→0) (B_{t+h} - B_t)/h does not exist a.s. Proof sketch: (B_{t+h} - B_t)/h ~ N(0, 1/h). As h → 0, variance → ∞, so quotient oscillates wildly. Rigorous proof: show that for any t, limsup |B_{t+h} - B_t|/|h| = ∞ a.s. Uses law of iterated logarithm: limsup(h→0) B_{t+h} - B_t / √(2h ln ln(1/h)) = 1 a.s. Since √(2h ln ln(1/h))/h = √(2 ln ln(1/h)/h) → ∞, the difference quotient is unbounded. Thus B is nowhere differentiable a.s.
**Skills Tested:** Stochastic processes, Brownian motion, path properties.

### COMBINATORICS / DISCRETE MATH 

**ID:** AAD-259
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Combinatorics — Generating Functions
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Use generating functions to find a closed form for the Fibonacci numbers F_n where F₀ = 0, F₁ = 1, F_n = F_{n-1} + F_{n-2}.
**Expected Answer:** Fₙ = (φⁿ - ψⁿ)/√5 where φ = (1+√5)/2, ψ = (1-√5)/2 (Binet's formula).
**Skills Tested:** Generating functions, recurrence relations, closed forms.

**ID:** AAD-260
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Combinatorics — Graph Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Prove that a graph G is a tree if and only if it is connected and has n-1 edges (where n is the number of vertices).
**Expected Answer:** (⇒) Tree is connected, acyclic. Prove by induction on n. Base: n=1, 0 edges ✓. Inductive step: tree with n vertices has at least one leaf (vertex of degree 1) — if all degrees ≥ 2, sum of degrees ≥ 2n, but sum = 2|E|, and connected with n vertices needs |E| ≥ n-1, so 2|E| ≥ 2n-2, could have all degree 2 (cycle), but tree is acyclic, contradiction. Remove leaf and its edge: get tree with n-1 vertices, by induction has n-2 edges. Original had (n-2)+1 = n-1 edges. (⇐) Connected with n-1 edges. Show acyclic. If has cycle, remove edge from cycle: still connected (cycle edge not a bridge), now n vertices, n-2 edges. Repeat until acyclic: still connected, acyclic = tree, has n-1 edges by (⇒). But we removed edges, contradiction. Thus originally acyclic, so tree.
**Skills Tested:** Graph theory, trees, proof techniques.

**ID:** AAD-261
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Combinatorics — Ramsey Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** State Ramsey's Theorem for graphs. Prove that R(3,3) = 6.
**Expected Answer:** Ramsey's Theorem: For any r, s, there exists minimum R(r,s) such that any 2-coloring of edges of complete graph K_n (n ≥ R(r,s)) contains either a red K_r or a blue K_s. R(3,3) = 6: need to show (1) any 2-coloring of K₆ has monochromatic triangle, (2) there exists 2-coloring of K₅ without monochromatic triangle. (1) Take any vertex v in K₆. It has 5 edges, by pigeonhole at least 3 are same color, say red, to vertices a,b,c. If any edge among a,b,c is red, we have red triangle with v. If all edges among a,b,c are blue, we have blue triangle. (2) K₅: color edges of pentagon red, diagonals blue (or vice versa). No monochromatic triangle: red edges form cycle C₅ (no triangles), blue edges form C₅ (no triangles). Thus R(3,3) = 6.
**Skills Tested:** Ramsey theory, graph coloring, combinatorial proofs.

**ID:** AAD-262
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Combinatorics — Extremal Graph Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** State and prove Turán's Theorem: the maximum number of edges in a graph on n vertices without a (r+1)-clique is achieved by the Turán graph T(n,r).
**Expected Answer:** Turán graph T(n,r): complete r-partite graph with part sizes as equal as possible (differ by at most 1). Turán's Theorem: ex(n, K_{r+1}) = |E(T(n,r))| = (1 - 1/r)n²/2 (approximately, exact formula depends on n mod r). Proof sketch (Zygmovitch induction): By induction on n. Base cases small. For general n: let G be K_{r+1}-free with max edges. G must contain K_r (otherwise can add edges). Let K_r have vertices v₁,...,v_r. Partition remaining vertices by neighborhood in K_r: A_i = {v : v adjacent to all of K_r except v_i}. Since G is K_{r+1}-free, no edges within A_i (else K_{r+1}). Edges between A_i and A_j: at most |A_i||A_j|. Total edges ≤ C(r,2) + Σ|A_i| + Σ_{i<j}|A_i||A_j|. Optimize: maximize when |A_i| as equal as possible. This gives Turán graph. Full proof requires careful induction and optimization.
**Skills Tested:** Extremal graph theory, Turán's theorem, optimization.

**ID:** AAD-263
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Combinatorics — Probabilistic Method
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Use the probabilistic method to prove that there exists a graph on n vertices with no cycles of length ≤ 4 and with at least n^{1+1/5}/10 edges (for large n).
**Expected Answer:** Probabilistic method: consider random graph G(n,p) with p = n^{-4/5}. Expected edges: C(n,2)p ≈ n²p/2 = n^{6/5}/2. Expected cycles of length k (for k = 3,4): number of potential k-cycles is C(n,k)·(k-1)!/2 ≈ n^k/(2k). Each present with probability p^k. Expected k-cycles ≈ n^k p^k / (2k) = n^k n^{-4k/5} / (2k) = n^{k/5} / (2k). For k=3: n^{3/5}/6. For k=4: n^{4/5}/8. Total expected short cycles: O(n^{4/5}). By linearity of expectation, expected (edges - short cycles) ≥ n^{6/5}/2 - O(n^{4/5}). For large n, this is positive, so there exists a graph with edges > short cycles. Remove one edge from each short cycle: remaining graph has no cycles of length ≤ 4 and has at least n^{6/5}/2 - O(n^{4/5}) > n^{1+1/5}/10 edges for large n.
**Skills Tested:** Probabilistic method, random graphs, extremal combinatorics.

**ID:** AAD-264
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Combinatorics — Pólya Enumeration
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Use Pólya enumeration to count the number of distinct ways to color the vertices of a square with 3 colors, up to rotation and reflection.
**Expected Answer:** Symmetry group of square: D₄ with 8 elements. Cycle index: (1) Identity: 4 fixed vertices, cycle type x₁⁴. (2) Rotation 90°: 1 cycle of length 4, x₄. (3) Rotation 180°: 2 cycles of length 2, x₂². (4) Rotation 270°: same as 90°, x₄. (5-6) Reflections through diagonals: 2 fixed, 1 swap, x₁²x₂. (7-8) Reflections through midpoints: 2 swaps, x₂². Cycle index: Z(D₄) = (1/8)(x₁⁴ + 2x₄ + x₂² + 2x₁²x₂ + 2x₂²) = (1/8)(x₁⁴ + 2x₁²x₂ + 3x₂² + 2x₄). Substitute x_k = 3 (3 colors): (1/8)(3⁴ + 2·3²·3 + 3·3² + 2·3) = (1/8)(81 + 54 + 27 + 6) = 168/8 = 21. So 21 distinct colorings.
**Skills Tested:** Pólya enumeration, group actions, counting under symmetry.

**ID:** AAD-265
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Combinatorics — Catalan Numbers
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Prove that the nth Catalan number C_n = C(2n,n)/(n+1) counts the number of valid parenthesizations of n+1 factors (e.g., ways to fully parenthesize a product of n+1 terms).
**Expected Answer:** Valid parenthesizations of n+1 factors correspond to binary trees with n internal nodes (each internal node represents a multiplication). Number of binary trees with n nodes = C_n. Proof: Let B_n be number of binary trees with n nodes. B₀ = 1 (empty tree). For n ≥ 1: root has left subtree with k nodes, right subtree with n-1-k nodes, for k = 0, 1, ..., n-1. So B_n = Σ(k=0 to n-1) B_k B_{n-1-k}. This is the Catalan recurrence! C_n = Σ(k=0 to n-1) C_k C_{n-1-k}. Since B₀ = C₀ = 1 and they satisfy same recurrence, B_n = C_n. Alternatively: valid parenthesizations of x₁·x₂·...·x_{n+1} correspond to ways to insert n pairs of parentheses. Last multiplication splits into (x₁·...·x_{k+1})·(x_{k+2}·...·x_{n+1}) for some k. Number of ways = C_k · C_{n-1-k}. Sum over k gives Catalan recurrence.
**Skills Tested:** Catalan numbers, combinatorial proofs, recurrence relations.

### NUMERICAL ANALYSIS / OPTIMIZATION 

**ID:** AAD-266
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Numerical Analysis — Root Finding
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Compare the bisection method and Newton's method for finding roots. Prove that Newton's method converges quadratically under suitable conditions.
**Expected Answer:** Bisection: bracket root, halve interval each step. Converges linearly: error ≤ (b-a)/2ⁿ after n steps. Guaranteed but slow. Newton's method: x_{n+1} = x_n - f(x_n)/f'(x_n). Geometrically: tangent line approximation. Quadratic convergence: if f(r) = 0, f'(r) ≠ 0, f'' continuous, and x₀ close to r, then |x_{n+1} - r| ≤ C|x_n - r|² for some constant C. Proof: Taylor expand f(r) around x_n: 0 = f(r) = f(x_n) + f'(x_n)(r-x_n) + f''(ξ)(r-x_n)²/2. Rearrange: f(x_n)/f'(x_n) = -(r-x_n) - f''(ξ)(r-x_n)²/(2f'(x_n)). So x_{n+1} - r = x_n - f(x_n)/f'(x_n) - r = (x_n - r) + (r-x_n) + f''(ξ)(r-x_n)²/(2f'(x_n)) = f''(ξ)(x_n-r)²/(2f'(x_n)). Thus |x_{n+1}-r| ≤ M|x_n-r|² where M = max|f''|/(2 min|f'|) near r. Quadratic convergence.
**Skills Tested:** Numerical methods, convergence analysis, root finding.

**ID:** AAD-267
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Numerical Analysis — Numerical Integration
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Derive Simpson's Rule for numerical integration and determine its degree of precision.
**Expected Answer:** Simpson's Rule: approximate ∫_a^b f(x) dx using quadratic interpolant through (a, f(a)), ((a+b)/2, f((a+b)/2)), (b, f(b)). Let h = (b-a)/2, m = (a+b)/2. Quadratic through these points: P₂(x) = f(a)L₀(x) + f(m)L₁(x) + f(b)L₂(x) where L_i are Lagrange basis polynomials. Integrate P₂: ∫_a^b P₂(x) dx = (h/3)[f(a) + 4f(m) + f(b)]. This is Simpson's Rule. Degree of precision: highest degree polynomial for which rule is exact. Test f(x) = 1: ∫_a^b 1 dx = b-a. Simpson: (h/3)[1 + 4 + 1] = 2h = b-a ✓. f(x) = x: ∫ x dx = (b²-a²)/2. Simpson: (h/3)[a + 4m + b] = (h/3)[a + 2(a+b) + b] = (h/3)(3a+3b) = h(a+b) = (b-a)(a+b)/2 = (b²-a²)/2 ✓. f(x) = x²: ∫ x² dx = (b³-a³)/3. Simpson: (h/3)[a² + 4m² + b²] = (h/3)[a² + (a+b)² + b²] = (h/3)(2a²+2ab+2b²) = 2h(a²+ab+b²)/3. Check: (b³-a³)/3 = (b-a)(a²+ab+b²)/3 = 2h(a²+ab+b²)/3 ✓. f(x) = x³: ∫ x³ = (b⁴-a⁴)/4. Simpson: (h/3)[a³ + 4m³ + b³]. This doesn't equal (b⁴-a⁴)/4 in general. So degree of precision = 3 (exact for polynomials up to degree 3).
**Skills Tested:** Numerical integration, quadrature, error analysis.

**ID:** AAD-268
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Numerical Analysis — Linear Systems
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain the LU decomposition of a matrix. When does it exist, and how is it used to solve Ax = b?
**Expected Answer:** LU decomposition: A = LU where L is lower triangular (with 1s on diagonal), U is upper triangular. Exists if all leading principal minors of A are nonzero (no zero pivots in Gaussian elimination without row exchanges). Algorithm: Gaussian elimination, storing multipliers in L. To solve Ax = b: (1) Decompose A = LU. (2) Solve Ly = b by forward substitution (L lower triangular). (3) Solve Ux = y by backward substitution (U upper triangular). Cost: decomposition O(n³), solves O(n²) each. Efficient for multiple right-hand sides. If row exchanges needed: PA = LU where P is permutation matrix (partial pivoting). Stability: partial pivoting ensures numerical stability (growth factor bounded).
**Skills Tested:** Numerical linear algebra, matrix factorization, solving linear systems.

**ID:** AAD-269
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Numerical Analysis — Eigenvalue Problems
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain the QR algorithm for computing eigenvalues. Why does it converge, and what is the role of shifts?
**Expected Answer:** QR algorithm: start with A₀ = A. Iterate: A_k = Q_k R_k (QR decomposition), A_{k+1} = R_k Q_k. Each A_k is similar to A (A_{k+1} = Q_k^T A_k Q_k), so same eigenvalues. Under mild conditions, A_k converges to upper triangular (or quasi-triangular for real matrices with complex eigenvalues), revealing eigenvalues on diagonal. Convergence: off-diagonal elements decay roughly as |λ_i/λ_j|^k where λ_i, λ_j are eigenvalues. Faster when eigenvalues are well-separated. Shifts: to accelerate convergence, use A_k - σ_k I = Q_k R_k, A_{k+1} = R_k Q_k + σ_k I. Choose σ_k close to an eigenvalue (e.g., Wilkinson shift: eigenvalue of bottom 2×2 block closest to bottom-right element). Shifted QR converges cubically. Practical algorithm: reduce to Hessenberg form first (O(n³)), then apply shifted QR (iterative, O(n²) per iteration).
**Skills Tested:** Numerical linear algebra, eigenvalue algorithms, convergence analysis.

**ID:** AAD-270
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Optimization — Convex Optimization
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** State the KKT (Karush-Kuhn-Tucker) conditions for constrained optimization. Use them to solve: minimize x² + y² subject to x + y ≥ 1.
**Expected Answer:** KKT conditions for min f(x) s.t. g_i(x) ≤ 0: (1) Stationarity: ∇f + Σ λ_i ∇g_i = 0. (2) Primal feasibility: g_i(x) ≤ 0. (3) Dual feasibility: λ_i ≥ 0. (4) Complementary slackness: λ_i g_i(x) = 0. For our problem: min x²+y² s.t. 1-x-y ≤ 0. f = x²+y², g = 1-x-y. ∇f = (2x, 2y), ∇g = (-1, -1). KKT: (2x, 2y) + λ(-1, -1) = 0 → 2x = λ, 2y = λ → x = y. Complementary slackness: λ(1-x-y) = 0. If λ = 0: x = y = 0, but constraint 1-0-0 = 1 > 0 violated. So λ > 0, constraint active: x+y = 1. With x = y: 2x = 1, x = y = 1/2. λ = 2x = 1 > 0 ✓. Solution: (x,y) = (1/2, 1/2), minimum value = 1/4 + 1/4 = 1/2.
**Skills Tested:** Optimization, KKT conditions, constrained minimization.

**ID:** AAD-271
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Optimization — Linear Programming
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Solve the linear program: maximize 3x + 2y subject to x + y ≤ 4, 2x + y ≤ 5, x, y ≥ 0 using the simplex method.
**Expected Answer:** Standard form: max 3x+2y s.t. x+y+s₁ = 4, 2x+y+s₂ = 5, x,y,s₁,s₂ ≥ 0. Initial tableau (basic variables s₁, s₂):
```
| 1  1  1  0 | 4 |
| 2  1  0  1 | 5 |
|-3 -2  0  0 | 0 |
```
Most negative in objective row: -3 (x column). Ratios: 4/1 = 4, 5/2 = 2.5. Pivot on row 2, column 1 (x enters, s₂ leaves). Divide row 2 by 2: [1, 1/2, 0, 1/2 | 5/2]. Row 1 = Row 1 - Row 2: [0, 1/2, 1, -1/2 | 3/2]. Obj = Obj + 3·Row 2: [0, -1/2, 0, 3/2 | 15/2]. Tableau:
```
| 0  1/2   1  -1/2 | 3/2 |
| 1  1/2   0   1/2 | 5/2 |
| 0 -1/2   0   3/2 |15/2 |
```
Most negative: -1/2 (y column). Ratios: (3/2)/(1/2) = 3, (5/2)/(1/2) = 5. Pivot on row 1, column 2 (y enters, s₁ leaves). Multiply row 1 by 2: [0, 1, 2, -1 | 3]. Row 2 = Row 2 - (1/2)Row 1: [1, 0, -1, 1 | 1]. Obj = Obj + (1/2)Row 1: [0, 0, 1, 1 | 9]. Final tableau:
```
| 0  1  2  -1 | 3 |
| 1  0 -1   1 | 1 |
| 0  0  1   1 | 9 |
```
All objective coefficients ≥ 0: optimal. Solution: x = 1, y = 3, max value = 9. Check: 3(1)+2(3) = 9. Constraints: 1+3=4 ≤ 4 ✓, 2(1)+3=5 ≤ 5 ✓.
**Skills Tested:** Linear programming, simplex method, optimization.

**ID:** AAD-272
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Differential Geometry — Curves and Surfaces
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Compute the Gaussian curvature and mean curvature of the surface z = x² + y² at the origin.
**Expected Answer:** Parametrize as r(x,y) = (x, y, x²+y²). r_x = (1, 0, 2x), r_y = (0, 1, 2y). First fundamental form: E = r_x·r_x = 1+4x², F = r_x·r_y = 4xy, G = r_y·r_y = 1+4y². At origin: E=1, F=0, G=1. r_xx = (0,0,2), r_xy = (0,0,0), r_yy = (0,0,2). Normal n = (r_x × r_y)/|r_x × r_y| = (-2x, -2y, 1)/√(1+4x²+4y²). At origin: n = (0,0,1). Second fundamental form: L = r_xx·n = 2, M = r_xy·n = 0, N = r_yy·n = 2. Gaussian curvature K = (LN-M²)/(EG-F²) = (4-0)/(1-0) = 4. Mean curvature H = (EN-2FM+GL)/(2(EG-F²)) = (2-0+2)/(2·1) = 2.
**Skills Tested:** Differential geometry, fundamental forms, curvature computation.

**ID:** AAD-273
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Algebraic Topology — Homotopy Groups
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Compute π₁(S¹ ∨ S¹) using the Seifert-van Kampen theorem.
**Expected Answer:** S¹ ∨ S¹ is wedge of two circles. Decompose as U = first circle plus small neighborhood of wedge point, V = second circle plus small neighborhood. U and V deformation retract to S¹, so π₁(U) = π₁(V) = Z. U ∩ V is contractible (small neighborhood), π₁(U ∩ V) = 0. By Seifert-van Kampen: π₁(S¹ ∨ S¹) = π₁(U) * π₁(V) = Z * Z = F₂ (free group on 2 generators).
**Skills Tested:** Algebraic topology, fundamental groups, Seifert-van Kampen.

**ID:** AAD-274
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Functional Analysis — Banach Spaces
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Prove that every finite-dimensional normed vector space is complete (hence a Banach space).
**Expected Answer:** Let V be finite-dimensional with basis {e₁, ..., eₙ}. Any x ∈ V can be written x = Σ aᵢeᵢ. Define ||x||₁ = Σ|aᵢ|. All norms on finite-dimensional space are equivalent, so it suffices to show V is complete under ||·||₁. Let (xₖ) be Cauchy in ||·||₁. Write xₖ = Σ aᵢ⁽ᵏ⁾eᵢ. Then |aᵢ⁽ᵏ⁾ - aᵢ⁽ᵐ⁾| ≤ ||xₖ - xₘ||₁ → 0, so each sequence (aᵢ⁽ᵏ⁾)ₖ is Cauchy in R. Since R is complete, aᵢ⁽ᵏ⁾ → aᵢ. Let x = Σ aᵢeᵢ. Then ||xₖ - x||₁ = Σ|aᵢ⁽ᵏ⁾ - aᵢ| → 0. Thus xₖ → x in V, so V is complete.
**Skills Tested:** Functional analysis, Banach spaces, norm equivalence.

**ID:** AAD-275
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Lie Groups and Lie Algebras
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Find the Lie algebra of SO(3) and compute its structure constants.
**Expected Answer:** SO(3) = {R ∈ M₃(R) : R^T R = I, det R = 1}. Lie algebra so(3) = tangent space at identity = {X ∈ M₃(R) : X^T + X = 0} (skew-symmetric matrices). Basis: J₁ = [[0,0,0],[0,0,-1],[0,1,0]], J₂ = [[0,0,1],[0,0,0],[-1,0,0]], J₃ = [[0,-1,0],[1,0,0],[0,0,0]]. Compute commutators: [J₁, J₂] = J₁J₂ - J₂J₁ = J₃. Similarly [J₂, J₃] = J₁, [J₃, J₁] = J₂. Structure constants: [Jᵢ, Jⱼ] = Σ εᵢⱼₖ Jₖ where εᵢⱼₖ is Levi-Civita symbol. So so(3) ≅ (R³, ×) with cross product.
**Skills Tested:** Lie theory, matrix groups, structure constants.

**ID:** AAD-276
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Representation Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Find all irreducible representations of S₃ (symmetric group on 3 elements).
**Expected Answer:** S₃ has 3 conjugacy classes: {e}, {(12), (13), (23)}, {(123), (132)}. So 3 irreducible representations. Sum of squares of dimensions = |S₃| = 6. Possibilities: 1² + 1² + 2² = 6. Two 1-dimensional reps: trivial (all elements map to 1) and sign (even permutations → 1, odd → -1). One 2-dimensional rep: standard representation on R³/(1,1,1). Explicitly: S₃ acts on {e₁, e₂, e₃} by permuting basis. Subspace V = {(x₁,x₂,x₃) : Σxᵢ = 0} is 2-dimensional invariant subspace. Basis: v₁ = (1,-1,0), v₂ = (1,1,-2). Action: (12) sends v₁ → -v₁, v₂ → v₂-v₁. Matrix: [[-1,-1],[0,1]]. (123) sends v₁ → v₂-v₁, v₂ → -v₁. Matrix: [[-1,-1],[1,0]]. These are irreducible (no 1-dim invariant subspace).
**Skills Tested:** Representation theory, symmetric groups, character theory.

**ID:** AAD-277
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Category Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Prove that in any category, if f: A → B has both a left inverse g and a right inverse h, then g = h and f is an isomorphism.
**Expected Answer:** Left inverse: g∘f = id_A. Right inverse: f∘h = id_B. Compute: g = g∘id_B = g∘(f∘h) = (g∘f)∘h = id_A∘h = h. So g = h. Call this common map f⁻¹. Then f⁻¹∘f = id_A and f∘f⁻¹ = id_B, so f is an isomorphism with inverse f⁻¹.
**Skills Tested:** Category theory, morphisms, inverses.

**ID:** AAD-278
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Homological Algebra
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Prove the Snake Lemma: given a commutative diagram with exact rows 0 → A → B → C → 0 and 0 → A' → B' → C' → 0 with vertical maps f, g, h, there is an exact sequence 0 → ker f → ker g → ker h → coker f → coker g → coker h → 0.
**Expected Answer:** Construct connecting homomorphism δ: ker h → coker f. Let c ∈ ker h. Since B → C is surjective, ∃b ∈ B mapping to c. Let b' = g(b) ∈ B'. Since diagram commutes, b' maps to h(c) = 0 in C', so b' ∈ ker(B' → C') = im(A' → B'). So ∃a' ∈ A' mapping to b'. Define δ(c) = [a'] ∈ coker f = A'/im(f). Well-defined: if chose different b, difference is in ker(B → C) = im(A → B), traces through to element in im(f). Exactness at each position: standard diagram chase. For example, exactness at ker h: im(ker g → ker h) = ker(δ). If c = image of b ∈ ker g, then g(b) = 0, so b' = 0, so a' = 0, so δ(c) = 0. Conversely, if δ(c) = 0, then a' = f(a) for some a, trace back to show c comes from ker g.
**Skills Tested:** Homological algebra, exact sequences, diagram chasing.

**ID:** AAD-279
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Algebraic Geometry — Varieties
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Prove that the projective variety V(x² + y² - z²) ⊂ P² is isomorphic to P¹.
**Expected Answer:** V is conic in P². Define map φ: P¹ → V by φ([s:t]) = [s²-t² : 2st : s²+t²]. Check well-defined: homogeneous of degree 2. Check image in V: (s²-t²)² + (2st)² = s⁴ - 2s²t² + t⁴ + 4s²t² = s⁴ + 2s²t² + t⁴ = (s²+t²)². ✓. Inverse: ψ: V → P¹ by ψ([x:y:z]) = [x+z : y] (when y ≠ 0) or [y : z-x] (when y = 0). Check: on V, x²+y² = z², so (x+z)(z-x) = z²-x² = y². So [x+z:y] = [y:z-x]. Well-defined and inverse to φ. Both maps are morphisms of varieties, so V ≅ P¹.
**Skills Tested:** Algebraic geometry, projective varieties, isomorphisms.

**ID:** AAD-280
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Commutative Algebra
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Prove that a Noetherian ring satisfies the ascending chain condition on ideals.
**Expected Answer:** Let R be Noetherian (every ideal finitely generated). Let I₁ ⊆ I₂ ⊆ ... be ascending chain. Let I = ∪Iₙ. I is ideal. Since R Noetherian, I = (a₁, ..., aₖ). Each aᵢ ∈ I_{nᵢ} for some nᵢ. Let N = max(nᵢ). Then all aᵢ ∈ I_N, so I ⊆ I_N. But I_N ⊆ I, so I = I_N. Thus Iₙ = I_N for all n ≥ N. Chain stabilizes. Conversely, if ACC holds and I is ideal, suppose I not finitely generated. Pick a₁ ∈ I. (a₁) ⊊ I, pick a₂ ∈ I \ (a₁). (a₁, a₂) ⊊ I, pick a₃. Continue: (a₁) ⊊ (a₁, a₂) ⊊ ... doesn't stabilize, contradicting ACC.
**Skills Tested:** Commutative algebra, Noetherian rings, ACC.

**ID:** AAD-281
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Galois Theory — Solvability
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Prove that a polynomial is solvable by radicals if and only if its Galois group is solvable.
**Expected Answer:** (⇒) If f solvable by radicals, roots lie in radical extension K/F. Radical extension has tower F = F₀ ⊆ F₁ ⊆ ... ⊆ Fₘ = K where Fᵢ₊₁ = Fᵢ(αᵢ) with αᵢ^{nᵢ} ∈ Fᵢ. Each step is cyclic extension (after adjoining roots of unity). Galois group of normal closure has solvable group (subquotients of cyclic groups are cyclic, hence abelian). (⇐) If Gal(K/F) solvable, have tower of normal subgroups with abelian quotients. Corresponding fixed fields give tower where each step has abelian Galois group. Abelian extensions are radical (by Kummer theory after adjoining roots of unity). So K contained in radical extension, roots expressible by radicals.
**Skills Tested:** Galois theory, solvability, radical extensions.

**ID:** AAD-282
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Algebraic Number Theory — Class Groups
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Compute the class group of Q(√-5).
**Expected Answer:** Ring of integers O_K = Z[√-5]. Minkowski bound: M = (2/π)√|d_K| = (2/π)√20 ≈ 2.85. Need to check primes ≤ 2. Only p = 2. Factor (2): x²+5 ≡ x²+1 ≡ (x+1)² (mod 2). So (2) = P₂² where P₂ = (2, 1+√-5). N(P₂) = 2. Is P₂ principal? If P₂ = (α), then N(α) = 2. But a²+5b² = 2 has no integer solutions. So P₂ not principal. P₂² = (2) is principal. Class group generated by [P₂] with [P₂]² = 1. So Cl(K) ≅ Z/2Z. Class number h = 2.
**Skills Tested:** Algebraic number theory, class groups, Minkowski bound.

**ID:** AAD-283
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Elliptic Curves
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Find the group law on the elliptic curve y² = x³ - x over Q. Compute 2P where P = (0, 0).
**Expected Answer:**. Line through P, P, Q gives P + P + Q = O. Here line is x = 0, meets at P (mult 2) and O. So 2P + O = O, giving 2P = O. So P has order 2. Check: P = (0,0), -P = (0, -0) = (0,0) = P. So P = -P, meaning 2P = O. ✓

**ID:** AAD-284
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Modular Forms
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Show that the Eisenstein series G₄(τ) = Σ'_{m,n} 1/(mτ+n)⁴ is a modular form of weight 4 for SL₂(Z).
