**Expected Answer:** Factorization algebras (Costello-Gwilliam): algebraic structure encoding observables in QFT. Setup: manifold M (spacetime). Factorization algebra F: assigns to open U ⊂ M: cochain complex F(U) (observables on U). For disjoint U₁, ..., Uₙ ⊂ V: factorization product ⊗ F(Uᵢ) → F(V). Construction from Lagrangian: (1) Classical BV formalism: fields = sections of bundle, action S, BV complex (fields + antifields). (2) Quantum BV: perturbative quantization via Feynman diagrams. (3) F(U) = quantum BV complex on U. Properties: (1) Cosheaf condition: local-to-global. (2) For M = R^n: E_n-algebra. (3) For conformal field theory: chiral algebra (factorization algebra on Riemann surface). Applications: (1) Perturbative QFT: compute correlation functions, partition functions. (2) Renormalization: Costello's approach via effective action. (3) Anomalies: obstruction to factorization algebra structure. (4) Wilson operators: defects in factorization algebra. Significance: (1) Rigorous framework for perturbative QFT. (2) Connects to mathematics (vertex algebras, chiral algebras). (3) Computational tool.
**Skills Tested:** Factorization algebras, quantum field theory, BV formalism.

**ID:** AAD-356
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Vertex Algebras — Moonshine
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain monstrous moonshine and the role of vertex algebras.
**Expected Answer:** Monstrous moonshine (Conway-Norton 1979): connection between Monster group M (largest sporadic simple group) and modular functions. Observation: dimensions of representations of M appear as Fourier coefficients of j-function: j(τ) = 1/q + 744 + 196884q + ... where 196884 = 196883 + 1 (sum of dimensions of trivial and 196883-dim rep). Conjecture: ∃ graded infinite-dimensional M-module V = ⊕ V_n with dim V_n = c(n) (coefficients of j). Construction (Frenkel-Lepowsky-Meurman 1988): Moonshine module V^♮ = vertex operator algebra (VOA) with V^♮_0 = C, V^♮_1 = 0, dim V^♮_2 = 196884. Monster acts on V^♮ preserving VOA structure. Proof of moonshine conjecture (Borcherds 1992): use V^♮ and no-name lemma (generalized Kac-Moody algebras). Compute trace functions: T_g(τ) = Σ tr(g|V^♮_n) q^{n-1} are Hauptmoduln (generate function fields of genus 0 curves). Significance: (1) Connects finite groups, modular forms, vertex algebras. (2) Borcherds won Fields Medal. (3) Active research (umbral moonshine, etc.).
**Skills Tested:** Vertex algebras, monstrous moonshine, modular forms.

**ID:** AAD-357
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Conformal Field Theory — Rational CFT
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain rational conformal field theory (RCFT) and its classification.
**Expected Answer:** RCFT: CFT with finitely many primary fields (irreducible representations of chiral algebra). Chiral algebra: vertex algebra V (e.g., Virasoro, affine Lie algebra, W-algebra). Properties: (1) Modular tensor category: representation category Rep(V) is modular (braided, ribbon, non-degenerate). (2) Modular invariance: partition function Z(τ) = Σ χᵢ(τ) M_{ij} χ̄_j(τ̄) where M is modular invariant matrix. (3) Fusion rules: N_{ij}^k = dim Hom(V_k, V_i ⊗ V_j) (Verlinde formula: N_{ij}^k = Σ S_{ia} S_{ja} S_{ka}* / S_{0a}). Classification: (1) c < 1: minimal models (classified by ADE). (2) c = 1: torus compactifications (classified by lattices). (3) WZW models: from affine Lie algebras (classified by level k). (4) Coset models: G/H. Applications: (1) Statistical mechanics (critical phenomena). (2) String theory (worldsheet CFT). (3) Knot invariants (Chern-Simons). (4) 2D gravity. Significance: (1) Complete classification in many cases. (2) Connects representation theory, topology, physics.
**Skills Tested:** Rational CFT, modular tensor categories, vertex algebras.

**ID:** AAD-358
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Topological Phases of Matter
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain topological phases of matter and their classification using tensor categories.
**Expected Answer:** Topological phases: gapped quantum systems with topological order (long-range entanglement). Properties: (1) Ground state degeneracy depends on topology of space. (2) Anyonic excitations (fractional statistics). (3) Topologically protected edge states. Classification: (1) 2+1D: modular tensor category C (anyons, fusion, braiding). (2) Kitaev: 2D topological phases classified by unitary modular tensor categories. (3) 3+1D: braided fusion 2-categories (active research). Examples: (1) Toric code: Z/2 gauge theory, anyons = {1, e, m, ε}. (2) Fractional quantum Hall: Laughlin states, anyons with fractional charge/statistics. (3) Topological insulators: SPT phases (classified by group cohomology). Construction: (1) Lattice models: Kitaev quantum double, Levin-Wen string-net. (2) Chern-Simons TQFT: action S = k/4π ∫ tr(A ∧ dA + 2/3 A³). (3) Boundary: chiral CFT (edge modes). Significance: (1) Quantum computation (topological quantum computers). (2) Condensed matter physics. (3) Mathematics: TQFT, category theory.
**Skills Tested:** Topological phases, tensor categories, condensed matter physics.

**ID:** AAD-359
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Higher Structures in Mathematical Physics
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain the role of higher algebraic structures (L_∞-algebras, A_∞-algebras, BV formalism) in mathematical physics.
**Expected Answer:** Higher structures: algebraic structures with operations satisfying relations up to coherent homotopy. L_∞-algebra: generalization of Lie algebra with brackets l_n: Λ^n V → V of degree 2-n satisfying homotopy Jacobi identities. A_∞-algebra: generalization of associative algebra with products m_n: V^{⊗n} → V satisfying homotopy associativity. BV formalism (Batalin-Vilkovisky): (1) BV algebra: graded commutative algebra with odd operator Δ (Δ² = 0). (2) BV formalism for gauge theories: fields + antifields, BV action S satisfying (S, S) = 0 (master equation). Applications: (1) Deformation quantization (Kontsevich): L_∞-morphisms. (2) String field theory: A_∞-algebra of string products. (3) Topological field theories: BV formalism for perturbative TQFT. (4) Homotopy transfer: A_∞/L_∞-structures on homology. (5) Derived geometry: L_∞-algebroids. Significance: (1) Systematic framework for gauge theories. (2) Connects homotopy theory, geometry, physics. (3) Computational tool (Feynman diagrams).
**Skills Tested:** Higher algebraic structures, BV formalism, mathematical physics.

**ID:** AAD-360
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Derived Symplectic Geometry
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain derived symplectic geometry and shifted symplectic structures.
**Expected Answer:** Derived symplectic geometry (Pantev-Toën-Vaquié-Vezzosi): symplectic geometry in derived algebraic geometry. Setup: derived stack X. n-shifted symplectic structure: closed 2-form ω of degree n (in de Rham complex) that is non-degenerate. Properties: (1) n = 0: classical symplectic structure. (2) n ≠ 0: "shifted" symplectic (new phenomenon). Examples: (1) X = BG (classifying stack of group G): 2-shifted symplectic (Killing form). (2) X = Map(Σ, Y) for Σ oriented surface, Y n-shifted symplectic: (n-2)-shifted symplectic. (3) X = Map(Σ, BG): (2-2)-shifted = 0-shifted (classical symplectic on character variety). (4) Moduli of perfect complexes: 2-shifted symplectic. Applications: (1) Lagrangian intersections: if L₁, L₂ ⊂ X are Lagrangian (isotropic of half dimension), intersection L₁ ∩_X L₂ has (n-1)-shifted symplectic structure. (2) Topological field theories: AKSZ construction. (3) Quantization: shifted Poisson structures. Significance: (1) Unified framework for symplectic geometry. (2) Connects to TQFT, representation theory.
**Skills Tested:** Derived symplectic geometry, shifted symplectic structures, derived algebraic geometry.

**ID:** AAD-361
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Factorization Homology — Higher Categories
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain the relation between factorization homology and higher categories.
**Expected Answer:** Factorization homology ∫_M A connects to higher categories in several ways: (1) E_n-algebras: algebras over little n-disks operad. For n=1: associative (A_∞-algebras). For n=2: braided monoidal. For n=∞: commutative (E_∞-algebras). (2) Factorization homology as higher categorical construction: ∫_M A computes "colimit" of A over configuration spaces of M. (3) Relation to TQFT: fully extended TQFTs classified by fully dualizable objects in symmetric monoidal (∞, n)-categories. Factorization homology constructs such TQFTs. (4) Higher categories of E_n-algebras: Morita categories, higher Morita categories. (5) Dunn additivity: E_n ≅ E_1 ⊗ ... ⊗ E_1 (n times). (6) Factorization homology with coefficients in higher categories: ∫_M C for C a symmetric monoidal (∞, n)-category. Applications: (1) Construct TQFTs from algebraic data. (2) Compute invariants of manifolds. (3) Relate to representation theory (quantum groups). (4) Connect to homotopy theory.
**Skills Tested:** Factorization homology, higher categories, E_n-algebras.

**ID:** AAD-362
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Topological Cyclic Homology — Computations
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain computations of topological cyclic homology TC and its applications to algebraic K-theory.
**Expected Answer:** TC(R): topological cyclic homology of ring R. Computed via: (1) TC(R) = holim (THH(R)^{C_{p^n}} ⇄ THH(R)^{C_{p^n}}) (inverse limit over fixed points of circle action). (2) For p-adic rings: syntomic cohomology (Bhatt-Morrow-Scholze). (3) Trace methods: cyclotomic trace K(R) → TC(R). Key computations: (1) TC(Z_p): computed by Bökstedt. (2) TC(Z): relates to K(Z) (algebraic K-theory of integers). (3) TC(O_K) for number field K: relates to K(O_K). (4) TC of finite fields, local fields. Applications: (1) Compute K-theory: K(R) ≃ TC(R) (after p-completion, for nice rings). (2) Redshift conjecture: K(R) has higher chromatic complexity than R. (3) Lichtenbaum-Quillen conjecture (proved by Voevodsky-Rost): relates K-theory to étale cohomology. (4) Compute K-theory of local fields (Madsen-Rognes). Significance: (1) Computational tool for K-theory. (2) Connects to chromatic homotopy. (3) Applications to number theory.
**Skills Tested:** Topological cyclic homology, algebraic K-theory, trace methods.

**ID:** AAD-363
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Chromatic Homotopy Theory — Computations
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain computations in chromatic homotopy theory and the chromatic spectral sequence.
**Expected Answer:** Chromatic homotopy theory: organize spectra by chromatic level n. Key tools: (1) Chromatic spectral sequence: E₁^{n,s} = π_*(L_{K(n)} S) ⇒ π_*(S) (stable homotopy of spheres). (2) Morava stabilizer group: G_n = Aut(Γ_n) (automorphisms of Honda formal group of height n). (3) K(n)-local sphere: L_{K(n)} S computed via continuous cohomology of G_n. (4) Hopkins-Miller theorem: E_n has E_∞-structure, G_n acts. Computations: (1) n=0: rational homotopy. (2) n=1: K(1)-local sphere relates to image of J (Adams). (3) n=2: K(2)-local sphere at p=2 computed by Henn, Goerss, et al. (4) Redshift: K(L_{K(n)} S) is K(n+1)-local (chromatic redshift). Applications: (1) Compute stable homotopy groups of spheres. (2) Understand structure of stable homotopy category. (3) Connect to number theory (Galois representations). (4) Topological modular forms (chromatic level 2). Significance: (1) Organizes stable homotopy. (2) Deep connections to arithmetic geometry. (3) Active research (computations at higher chromatic levels).
**Skills Tested:** Chromatic homotopy theory, spectral sequences, Morava stabilizer groups.

**ID:** AAD-364
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Elliptic Cohomology — Topological Modular Forms
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain the construction and properties of topological modular forms tmf.
**Expected Answer:** tmf: spectrum representing topological modular forms. Construction: (1) Moduli stack M_{ell} of elliptic curves. (2) Goerss-Hopkins-Miller: sheaf O^{top} of E_∞-ring spectra on M_{ell} (in étale topology). (3) tmf = Γ(M_{ell}, O^{top}) (global sections). Properties: (1) π_*(tmf) = ring of topological modular forms (related to classical modular forms). (2) tmf detects image of J (stable homotopy). (3) Ando-Hopkins-Rezk: string orientation MString → tmf. (4) tmf relates to 2d CFT (Segal's conjecture). (5) Chromatic level 2: tmf is "universal" elliptic cohomology. Applications: (1) Compute stable homotopy groups (tmf-based Adams spectral sequence). (2) String theory (partition functions). (3) Connect to elliptic curves, modular forms. (4) Topological field theories (2d). Significance: (1) Bridge between stable homotopy and arithmetic geometry. (2) Rich structure (chromatic, modular). (3) Active research (tmf-resolutions, etc.).
**Skills Tested:** Topological modular forms, elliptic cohomology, stable homotopy.

**ID:** AAD-365
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Algebraic K-Theory — Computations
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain computations of algebraic K-theory and the role of trace methods.
**Expected Answer:** Algebraic K-theory K(R): hard to compute directly. Trace methods: (1) Dennis trace: K(R) → HH(R) (Hochschild homology). (2) Cyclotomic trace: K(R) → TC(R) (topological cyclic homology). (3) For p-adic rings: K(R) ≃ TC(R) (after p-completion, Dundas-Goodwillie-McCarthy). Computations: (1) K(F_q): Quillen computed (K_0 = Z, K_1 = F_q*, K_{2i} = 0, K_{2i-1} = F_{q^i}*). (2) K(Z): Borel computed rationally. (3) K(Z_p): computed via TC (Bökstedt-Madsen). (4) K(O_K) for number field: Lichtenbaum conjecture (relates to zeta values). (5) K(R) for local fields: computed via TC. Applications: (1) Number theory (special values of zeta functions). (2) Manifold topology (Whitehead groups, h-cobordism). (3) Operator algebras (C*-algebra K-theory). Significance: (1) Connects algebra, topology, number theory. (2) Trace methods make computations feasible. (3) Active research (redshift, etc.).
**Skills Tested:** Algebraic K-theory, trace methods, computations.

**ID:** AAD-366
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Motivic Homotopy Theory — Computations
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain computations in motivic homotopy theory and the motivic Adams spectral sequence.
**Expected Answer:** Motivic homotopy theory: homotopy theory for algebraic varieties. Computations: (1) Motivic cohomology: H^{p,q}(X, Z) computed via Bloch's cycle complex. (2) Motivic stable homotopy groups: π_{p,q}(S) = [S^{p,q}, S]_{SH(k)}. (3) Motivic Adams spectral sequence: E₂ = Ext_{A^{mot}}(H^{*,*}(X), H^{*,*}) ⇒ π_{*,*}(X) (for p-local, A^{mot} = motivic Steenrod algebra). Key results: (1) Milnor conjecture (Voevodsky): proved using motivic Steenrod operations. (2) Motivic stable homotopy of spheres: computed over C (Isaksen, et al.). (3) Motivic J-homomorphism. (4) Realization functors: to classical homotopy, to derived category. Applications: (1) Algebraic geometry (Chow groups, algebraic cycles). (2) Number theory (motivic cohomology of number fields). (3) K-theory (motivic spectral sequence). Significance: (1) Algebraic analog of classical homotopy. (2) Rich structure (bigraded, realization functors). (3) Active research.
**Skills Tested:** Motivic homotopy theory, motivic Adams spectral sequence, computations.

**ID:** AAD-367
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Equivariant Stable Homotopy — Computations
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain computations in equivariant stable homotopy theory for finite groups.
**Expected Answer:** Equivariant stable homotopy: stable homotopy with G-action. Computations: (1) RO(G)-graded coefficients: π_V^G(E) = [S^V, E]_G for G-representations V. (2) Geometric fixed points: Φ^H(E) for H ⊂ G. (3) Tom Dieck splitting: π_*(Σ^∞_G X_+) = ⊕_{(H)} π_*(Σ^∞ (X^H / W_G H)_+). (4) Equivariant stable stems: π_{*,*}^G(S) for G = Z/2, Z/3, etc. Key results: (1) Segal conjecture (Carlsson): completion theorem for A(G). (2) Equivariant nilpotence theorem. (3) Equivariant chromatic homotopy. (4) Computations for G = Z/p (Hill-Hopkins-Ravenel for Kervaire invariant). Applications: (1) Kervaire invariant one problem (Hill-Hopkins-Ravenel): uses equivariant multiplicative stable homotopy. (2) Equivariant elliptic cohomology. (3) Topological field theories (equivariant). Significance: (1) Richer structure than non-equivariant. (2) Connects to representation theory. (3) Active research (higher groups, etc.).
**Skills Tested:** Equivariant stable homotopy, computations, Kervaire invariant.

**ID:** AAD-368
**Category:** Mathematics
**Subcategory:** Derived Algebraic Geometry — Applications
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain applications of derived algebraic geometry to enumerative geometry and moduli problems.
**Expected Answer:** Derived algebraic geometry (DAG): algebraic geometry with derived structure. Applications: (1) Enumerative geometry: (a) Gromov-Witten invariants: derived moduli of stable maps has perfect obstruction theory, gives virtual fundamental class. (b) Donaldson-Thomas invariants: derived moduli of sheaves. (c) Categorical DT theory: Joyce, Kontsevich-Soibelman. (2) Moduli problems: (a) Derived moduli of perfect complexes: Toën-Vaquié. (b) Derived moduli of local systems: Simpson. (c) Derived mapping stacks. (3) Deformation theory: derived structure encodes obstruction theory. (4) Shifted symplectic geometry: Pantev-Toën-Vaquié-Vezzosi (applications to enumerative geometry). (5) Factorization homology: applications to TQFT. Key tools: (1) Perfect obstruction theories. (2) Virtual fundamental classes. (3) Derived intersection theory. Significance: (1) Rigorous framework for enumerative geometry. (2) Connects to physics (B-model, Landau-Ginzburg). (3) Active research.
**Skills Tested:** Derived algebraic geometry, enumerative geometry, moduli problems.

**ID:** AAD-369

(c) Fargues-Scholze: geometrization of local Langlands using condensed mathematics. (2) Representation theory: (a) Smooth representations of p-adic groups: condensed framework. (b) Mod p representation theory. (c) Connections to p-adic Langlands. (3) Analytic geometry: (a) Analytic rings: framework for analytic geometry. (b) Connects algebraic and analytic geometry. **ID:** AAD-370

**ID:** AAD-371

**ID:** AAD-384
**Category:** Long-Context Document Analysis
**Subcategory:** Multi-Hop Reasoning
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Context:** [Excerpt from a 50-page historical biography] "...In 1842, Lord Ashbury introduced the Factory Act, limiting child labor to 8 hours a day. Industrialists fiercely opposed it, arguing it would bankrupt the mills. By 1845, mill profits had actually increased by 10%, as reported by the Board of Trade. Ashbury claimed this proved his act was economically beneficial. However, a later economic analysis revealed that the 1845 profit increase was primarily due to a simultaneous 30% drop in raw cotton prices caused by a bumper crop in the American South, not the labor reforms..."
**Question:** Did the Factory Act of 1842 cause the increase in mill profits by 1845, according to the text? Explain the causal chain.
**Expected Answer:** No. While Lord Ashbury claimed the Factory Act caused the 10% profit increase by 1845, the text states that a later economic analysis attributed the profit increase primarily to a 30% drop in raw cotton prices (due to a bumper crop in the American South), not the labor reforms.
**Skills Tested:** Multi-hop reasoning, causal attribution, historical analysis.

**ID:** AAD-385
**Category:** Long-Context Document Analysis
**Subcategory:** Summarization with Detail Preservation
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Context:** [Excerpt from a 40-page technical specification for a drone] "...The UAV features a carbon-fiber airframe weighing 1.2 kg. The onboard AI vision system requires 50W of continuous power. If the payload and vision system are both at maximum capacity, flight time is reduced by 40%. The emergency parachute system adds 150g to the total weight and deploys automatically if the IMU detects a freefall..."
**Question:** Calculate the maximum flight time if the drone is carrying a 400g payload, the AI vision system is running at full power, and the emergency parachute is installed. Assume optimal wind conditions.
**Expected Answer:** Base flight time: 35 mins. Battery capacity: 5000mAh. Base weight: 1.2kg. Parachute adds 150g (1.35kg total). Payload is 400g (max capacity). Vision system is at max power. The text states that if payload and vision system are at max capacity, flight time is reduced by 40%. 40% of 35 mins = 14 mins reduction. 35 - 14 = 21 minutes. (The parachute weight is already factored into the base 1.2kg or is an additional static weight, but the explicit 40% reduction rule for max payload + max vision system dictates the 21-minute answer).
**Skills Tested:** Summarization, mathematical extraction, constraint application.

**ID:** AAD-390

**Question:** Verify the mathematical accuracy of the projected net yield of 5.5% stated in the prospectus.

Russia mobilized to support Serbia. Germany, allied with Austria-Hungary, declared war on Russia and France. Britain entered the war when Germany invaded neutral Belgium. The Ottoman Empire joined the Central Powers in late 1914, hoping to regain lost territories in the Caucasus. This opened a new front, forcing Russia to divide its army..."
**Question:** Explain how the Ottoman Empire's entry into the war indirectly affected the Eastern Front.
**Expected Answer:** The Ottoman Empire joined the Central Powers to regain Caucasus territories. This opened a new front (Caucasus/Middle East), which forced Russia to divide its army to defend against the Ottomans, thereby weakening its forces on the primary Eastern Front against Germany and Austria-Hungary.
**Skills Tested:** Multi-hop reasoning, historical causality, geopolitical analysis.

## SECTION 12: CODE DEBUGGING & ANALYSIS 

**ID:** AAD-422
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / Concurrency
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Language:** Python
**Code:**
```python
import threading

counter = 0

def increment():
    global counter
    for _ in range(100000):
        counter += 1

threads = [threading.Thread(target=increment) for _ in range(10)]
for t in threads: t.start()
for t in threads: t.join()

print(counter)
```
**Question:** The code intends to print 1,000,000 but prints a lower, inconsistent number. Identify the bug, explain why it occurs, and provide a fixed version using a Lock.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: Race condition. `counter += 1` is not atomic (read-modify-write). Multiple threads read the same value, increment, and write back, losing updates. Fix: Use `threading.Lock()`.
```python
import threading
counter = 0
lock = threading.Lock()
def increment():
    global counter
    for _ in range(100000):
        with lock:
            counter += 1
# ... thread setup same ...
```
**Skills Tested:** Concurrency, race conditions, threading, Python GIL.

**ID:** AAD-423
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Syntax Error / Memory Leak
**Difficulty Level:** 6
**Language:** C++
**Code:**
```cpp
#include <iostream>
#include <vector>

class ResourceManager {
    int* data;
public:
    ResourceManager(int size) { data = new int[size]; }
    ~ResourceManager() { delete[] data; }
};

int main() {
    std::vector<ResourceManager> vec;
    vec.push_back(ResourceManager(10));
    return 0;
}
```
**Question:** This code compiles but crashes or exhibits undefined behavior (double free). Identify the root cause and provide a modern C++ fix.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: `ResourceManager` lacks a copy constructor and copy assignment operator. `push_back` copies the object, leading to two objects pointing to the same `data` pointer. When both are destroyed, `delete[]` is called twice (double free). Fix: Use `std::vector<std::unique_ptr<ResourceManager>>` or implement the Rule of Three/Five, or use `std::vector` with `emplace_back` and a move constructor. Best modern fix: use `std::unique_ptr` or `std::vector<int>` directly.
**Skills Tested:** C++ memory management, Rule of Three, move semantics, smart pointers.

**ID:** AAD-424
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Security Vulnerability
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Language:** JavaScript
**Code:**
```javascript
app.get('/search', (req, res) => {
    const query = req.query.q;
    const sql = `SELECT * FROM products WHERE name LIKE '%${query}%'`;
    db.execute(sql, (err, results) => {
        res.send(results);
    });
});
```
**Question:** Identify the security vulnerability in this Node.js/Express code and provide a secure refactored version.
**Expected Answer:** Vulnerability: SQL Injection. User input `query` is directly interpolated into the SQL string. An attacker can pass `q=' OR '1'='1` to dump the database. Fix: Use parameterized queries.
```javascript
app.get('/search', (req, res) => {
    const query = `%${req.query.q}%`;
    const sql = 'SELECT * FROM products WHERE name LIKE ?';
    db.execute(sql, [query], (err, results) => {
        res.send(results);
    });
});
```
**Skills Tested:** Security, SQL injection, parameterized queries, Node.js.

**ID:** AAD-425
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Performance Improvement
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Language:** Python
**Code:**
```python
def find_duplicates(lst):
    duplicates = []
    for i in range(len(lst)):
        for j in range(i + 1, len(lst)):
            if lst[i] == lst[j] and lst[i] not in duplicates:
                duplicates.append(lst[i])
    return duplicates
```
**Question:** The code finds duplicates in a list but is extremely slow for large lists. Analyze the time complexity and provide an O(N) solution.
**Expected Answer:** Current complexity: O(N^3) due to nested loops (O(N^2)) and `not in duplicates` list search (O(N)). Fix: Use a hash set to track seen items and another for duplicates.
```python
def find_duplicates(lst):
    seen = set()
    duplicates = set()
    for item in lst:
        if item in seen:
            duplicates.add(item)
        else:
            seen.add(item)
    return list(duplicates)
```
**Skills Tested:** Algorithmic complexity, hash sets, performance optimization, Python.

**ID:** AAD-426
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / Async
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Language:** JavaScript
**Code:**
```javascript
async function fetchUserData(userId) {
    let user;
    fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`)
        .then(res => res.json())
        .then(data => { user = data; });
    console.log(user.name);
    return user;
}
```
**Question:** The function throws a TypeError: Cannot read properties of undefined (reading 'name'). Explain why and fix it using modern async/await syntax.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: The `fetch` call is asynchronous, but the code doesn't `await` it. `console.log(user.name)` executes before the promise resolves, so `user` is still undefined. Fix: Use `await`.
```javascript
async function fetchUserData(userId) {
    const res = await fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`);
    const user = await res.json();
    console.log(user.name);
    return user;
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Asynchronous JavaScript, Promises, async/await, event loop.

**ID:** AAD-427
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Refactoring / Clean Code
**Difficulty Level:** 6
**Language:** Java
**Code:**
```java
public class OrderProcessor {
    public void process(Order o) {
        if (o.getType().equals("STANDARD")) {
            // 20 lines of standard processing
        } else if (o.getType().equals("EXPRESS")) {
            // 20 lines of express processing
        } else if (o.getType().equals("OVERNIGHT")) {
            // 20 lines of overnight processing
        }
    }
}
```
**Question:** This code violates the Open/Closed Principle and is hard to maintain. Refactor it using the Strategy Pattern.
**Expected Answer:** Refactor to use an interface and concrete strategy classes.
```java
public interface ProcessingStrategy {
    void process(Order o);
}
public class StandardStrategy implements ProcessingStrategy { ... }
public class ExpressStrategy implements ProcessingStrategy { ... }
public class OrderProcessor {
    private Map<String, ProcessingStrategy> strategies;
    // initialize map in constructor
    public void process(Order o) {
        strategies.get(o.getType()).process(o);
    }
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Design patterns, Strategy pattern, SOLID principles, Java refactoring.

**ID:** AAD-428
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Syntax Error / Type Mismatch
**Difficulty Level:** 5
**Language:** Rust
**Code:**
```rust
fn main() {
    let mut s = String::from("hello");
    let r1 = &s;
    let r2 = &s;
    let r3 = &mut s;
    println!("{}, {}, {}", r1, r2, r3);
}
```
**Question:** This code fails to compile. Identify the borrow checker error and explain how to fix it.
**Expected Answer:** Error: Cannot borrow `s` as mutable (`r3`) because it is also borrowed as immutable (`r1`, `r2`). Rust's borrow checker prevents mutable references while immutable references exist. Fix: Ensure immutable references are no longer used before the mutable borrow, or restructure the code.
```rust
fn main() {
    let mut s = String::from("hello");
    let r1 = &s;
    let r2 = &s;
    println!("{}, {}", r1, r2); // r1 and r2 no longer used after this
    let r3 = &mut s;
    println!("{}", r3);
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Rust borrow checker, memory safety, mutability rules.

**ID:** AAD-429
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Concurrency / Deadlock
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Language:** Go
**Code:**
```go
package main
import "sync"
var mu1 sync.Mutex
var mu2 sync.Mutex
func goroutineA() {
    mu1.Lock()
    mu2.Lock()
    mu2.Unlock()
    mu1.Unlock()
}
func goroutineB() {
    mu2.Lock()
    mu1.Lock()
    mu1.Unlock()
    mu2.Unlock()
}
func main() {
    go goroutineA()
    go goroutineB()
    select {}
}
```
**Question:** This program frequently deadlocks. Explain the cause and provide a fix to prevent it.
**Expected Answer:** Cause: Classic deadlock. Goroutine A locks mu1 then mu2. Goroutine B locks mu2 then mu1. If A gets mu1 and B gets mu2, they wait for each other forever. Fix: Always acquire locks in the same global order.
```go
func goroutineB() {
    mu1.Lock() // Changed order to match goroutineA
    mu2.Lock()
    mu2.Unlock()
    mu1.Unlock()
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Go concurrency, deadlocks, mutexes, synchronization.

**ID:** AAD-430
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / SQL
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Language:** SQL
**Code:**
```sql
SELECT e.name, d.department_name
FROM employees e
LEFT JOIN departments d ON e.department_id = d.id
WHERE d.department_name = 'Engineering';
```
**Question:** The developer intended to get all employees, including those without a department, but specifically highlight those in Engineering. This query fails to do that. Identify the logical bug and fix it.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: The `WHERE d.department_name = 'Engineering'` clause filters out all rows where `d.department_name` is NULL (i.e., employees without a department), effectively turning the `LEFT JOIN` into an `INNER JOIN`. Fix: Move the condition to the `ON` clause.
```sql
SELECT e.name, d.department_name
FROM employees e
LEFT JOIN departments d ON e.department_id = d.id AND d.department_name = 'Engineering';
```
**Skills Tested:** SQL joins, LEFT JOIN behavior, NULL filtering, query logic.

**ID:** AAD-431
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Security Vulnerability / XSS
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Language:** JavaScript
**Code:**
```javascript
function displayComment(comment) {
    const div = document.createElement('div');
    div.innerHTML = comment;
    document.body.appendChild(div);
}
```
**Question:** Identify the security vulnerability in this DOM manipulation code and provide a secure alternative.
**Expected Answer:** Vulnerability: Cross-Site Scripting (XSS). `innerHTML` parses the string as HTML, allowing malicious scripts (e.g., `<script>alert(1)</script>`) to execute. Fix: Use `textContent` to insert plain text safely.
```javascript
function displayComment(comment) {
    const div = document.createElement('div');
    div.textContent = comment;
    document.body.appendChild(div);
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Web security, XSS, DOM manipulation, JavaScript.

**ID:** AAD-432
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Performance / Algorithmic
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Language:** C++
**Code:**
```cpp
std::vector<int> get_large_data();
void process() {
    std::vector<int> data = get_large_data();
    for (int i = 0; i < data.size(); ++i) {
        std::cout << data[i] << " ";
    }
}
```
**Question:** This code suffers from unnecessary copies and slow I/O. Identify the performance bottlenecks and optimize the code.
**Expected Answer:** Bottlenecks: 1) `get_large_data()` might return by value, causing a copy (though RVO helps, passing by reference is safer). 2) `std::cout` in a loop is extremely slow due to formatting and flushing. Fix: Use `const auto&`, disable sync with stdio, and use `\n` instead of `std::endl`.
```cpp
std::vector<int> get_large_data();
void process() {
    const auto& data = get_large_data();
    std::ios_base::sync_with_stdio(false);
    std::cin.tie(NULL);
    for (int val : data) {
        std::cout << val << '\n';
    }
}
```
**Skills Tested:** C++ performance, I/O optimization, references, move semantics.

**ID:** AAD-433
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / Null Pointer
**Difficulty Level:** 6
**Language:** Java
**Code:**
```java
public String getUserName(User user) {
    return user.getAddress().getCity().getName();
}
```
**Question:** This method frequently throws a `NullPointerException`. Explain why and provide a robust fix using Java 8+ features.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: If `user`, `getAddress()`, `getCity()`, or `getName()` returns null, a NullPointerException is thrown. Fix: Use `Optional` or null checks.
```java
public String getUserName(User user) {
    return Optional.ofNullable(user)
        .map(User::getAddress)
        .map(Address::getCity)
        .map(City::getName)
        .orElse("Unknown");
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Java NullPointerException, Optional API, defensive programming.

**ID:** AAD-434
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Refactoring / Code Smell
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Language:** Python
**Code:**
```python
def process_data(data):
    result = []
    for item in data:
        if item['type'] == 'A':
            result.append(item['value'] * 2)
        elif item['type'] == 'B':
            result.append(item['value'] + 10)
        else:
            result.append(item['value'])
    return sum(result)
```
**Question:** This code works but is verbose and hard to extend. Refactor it using a dictionary mapping and functional programming concepts.
**Expected Answer:** Refactor to use a dispatch dictionary and `sum()` with a generator expression.
```python
def process_data(data):
    operations = {
        'A': lambda x: x * 2,
        'B': lambda x: x + 10
    }
    return sum(
        operations.get(item['type'], lambda x: x)(item['value'])
        for item in data
    )
```
**Skills Tested:** Python refactoring, dictionary dispatch, functional programming, code smells.

**ID:** AAD-435
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Concurrency / Goroutine Leak
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Language:** Go
**Code:**
```go
func fetchAll(urls []string) {
    ch := make(chan string)
    for _, url := range urls {
        go func(u string) {
            resp, _ := http.Get(u)
            ch <- u
        }(url)
    }
    for range urls {
        fmt.Println(<-ch)
    }
}
```
**Question:** If one of the HTTP requests hangs indefinitely, what happens to this program? Identify the concurrency bug and fix it using `context`.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: Goroutine leak and main function deadlock. If one request hangs, the goroutine never sends to `ch`. The main loop waits forever for `len(urls)` messages, causing a deadlock. Fix: Use `context.WithTimeout` to cancel hanging requests.
```go
func fetchAll(urls []string) {
    ch := make(chan string)
    ctx, cancel := context.WithTimeout(context.Background(), 5*time.Second)
    defer cancel()
    for _, url := range urls {
        go func(u string) {
            req, _ := http.NewRequestWithContext(ctx, "GET", u, nil)
            resp, err := http.DefaultClient.Do(req)
            if err != nil {
                ch <- u + " failed"
                return
            }
            resp.Body.Close()
            ch <- u
        }(url)
    }
    // ... read from ch ...
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Go concurrency, goroutine leaks, context package, HTTP timeouts.

**ID:** AAD-436
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Syntax Error / Lifetime
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Language:** Rust
**Code:**
```rust
struct Config {
    name: &str,
}
impl Config {
    fn new(n: &str) -> Config {
        Config { name: n }
    }
}
```
**Question:** This code fails to compile due to a missing lifetime specifier. Add the correct lifetimes and explain why they are necessary.
**Expected Answer:** Fix: Add lifetime `'a` to the struct and the reference.
```rust
struct Config<'a> {
    name: &'a str,
}
impl<'a> Config<'a> {
    fn new(n: &'a str) -> Config<'a> {
        Config { name: n }
    }
}
```
Explanation: Rust requires explicit lifetimes for references in structs to ensure the referenced data lives at least as long as the struct itself.
**Skills Tested:** Rust lifetimes, struct references, borrow checker.

**ID:** AAD-437
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / Off-by-One
**Difficulty Level:** 5
**Language:** Python
**Code:**
```python
def get_last_n_items(lst, n):
    return lst[len(lst) - n : len(lst)]
```
**Question:** If `n` is 0, this function returns an empty list, which is correct. But if `n` is greater than the length of the list, it behaves unexpectedly. Identify the edge case bug and fix it.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: If `n > len(lst)`, `len(lst) - n` is negative. Python handles negative slicing by counting from the end, which might return the whole list or unexpected slices, but conceptually it's an off-by-one / bounds error. Fix: Clamp `n` or use standard slicing.
```python
def get_last_n_items(lst, n):
    if n <= 0: return []
    return lst[-n:] if n <= len(lst) else lst[:]
```
**Skills Tested:** Python slicing, edge cases, off-by-one errors, bounds checking.

**ID:** AAD-438
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Security Vulnerability / Path Traversal
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Language:** JavaScript
**Code:**
```javascript
app.get('/file', (req, res) => {
    const filename = req.query.name;
    const path = require('path');
    const filePath = path.join(__dirname, 'uploads', filename);
    res.sendFile(filePath);
});
```
**Question:** Identify the security vulnerability that allows an attacker to read arbitrary files on the server and provide a secure fix.
**Expected Answer:** Vulnerability: Path Traversal (Directory Traversal). An attacker can pass `name=../../../etc/passwd` to read sensitive files. Fix: Validate the filename, use `path.resolve` and ensure it stays within the `uploads` directory.
```javascript
app.get('/file', (req, res) => {
    const filename = req.query.name;
    const path = require('path');
    const uploadsDir = path.join(__dirname, 'uploads');
    const filePath = path.resolve(uploadsDir, filename);
    
    if (!filePath.startsWith(uploadsDir)) {
        return res.status(403).send('Access denied');
    }
    res.sendFile(filePath);
});
```
**Skills Tested:** Web security, path traversal, Node.js, file system safety.

**ID:** AAD-439

**Expected Answer:** Bug: Applying a function `YEAR()` to the column `order_date` prevents the database from using the index (non-SARGable). Fix: Use a range query.

**ID:** AAD-440
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / Floating Point
**Difficulty Level:** 6
**Language:** C++
**Code:**
```cpp
double a = 0.1;
double b = 0.2;
if (a + b == 0.3) {
    std::cout << "Equal\n";
} else {
    std::cout << "Not equal\n";
}
```
**Question:** This code prints "Not equal". Explain the root cause of this behavior in floating-point arithmetic and provide a robust way to compare the values.
**Expected Answer:** Cause: Floating-point precision errors. 0.1 and 0.2 cannot be represented exactly in binary IEEE 754, so their sum is slightly off from 0.3 (e.g., 0.30000000000000004). Fix: Compare with an epsilon.
```cpp
#include <cmath>
if (std::abs((a + b) - 0.3) < 1e-9) {
    std::cout << "Equal\n";
}
```
**Skills Tested:** C++ floating-point arithmetic, IEEE 754, epsilon comparison.

```java
    return price;
}
```
**Question:** This code is hard to read and maintain. Refactor it to eliminate magic numbers and improve clarity using an Enum.
**Expected Answer:** Refactor using an Enum to represent discount types and their multipliers.

**ID:** AAD-442
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Concurrency / Channel Deadlock
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Language:** Go
**Code:**
```go
func main() {
    ch := make(chan int)
    ch <- 42
    fmt.Println(<-ch)
}
```
**Question:** This program deadlocks immediately. Explain why and provide two different ways to fix it.
**Expected Answer:** Cause: Unbuffered channel. Sending to an unbuffered channel blocks until a receiver is ready. Since the send and receive are in the same goroutine sequentially, it blocks forever on the send. Fix 1: Use a buffered channel. Fix 2: Use a separate goroutine for sending.
```go
// Fix 1
ch := make(chan int, 1)
ch <- 42
fmt.Println(<-ch)

// Fix 2
ch := make(chan int)
go func() { ch <- 42 }()
fmt.Println(<-ch)
```
**Skills Tested:** Go channels, deadlocks, buffered vs unbuffered, goroutines.

**ID:** AAD-443
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Syntax Error / Borrowing
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Language:** Rust
**Code:**
```rust
fn main() {
    let mut v = vec![1, 2, 3];
    for i in &v {
        v.push(*i * 2);
    }
}
```
**Question:** This code fails to compile. Identify the borrow checker violation and explain how to fix it without changing the intended logic.
**Expected Answer:** Error: Cannot borrow `v` as mutable (`v.push`) while it is borrowed as immutable (`&v` in the for loop). Fix: Iterate over a clone or collect indices first.
```rust
fn main() {
    let mut v = vec![1, 2, 3];
    let additions: Vec<i32> = v.iter().map(|&i| i * 2).collect();
    v.extend(additions);
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Rust borrow checker, vector mutation, iterator invalidation.

**ID:** AAD-447
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / Integer Overflow
**Difficulty Level:** 6
**Language:** Java
**Code:**
```java
public int calculateMid(int left, int right) {
    return (left + right) / 2;
}
```
**Question:** This binary search midpoint calculation can fail for large arrays. Identify the bug and provide a safe alternative.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: Integer overflow. If `left` and `right` are large positive integers, their sum can exceed `Integer.MAX_VALUE`, resulting in a negative number. Fix: Use `left + (right - left) / 2` or unsigned shift.
```java
public int calculateMid(int left, int right) {
    return left + (right - left) / 2;
    // or: return (left + right) >>> 1;
}
```

**ID:** AAD-449
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Concurrency / Race Condition
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Language:** Go
**Code:**
```go
type Counter struct {
    val int
}
func (c *Counter) Increment() {
    c.val++
}
func main() {
    c := &Counter{}
    var wg sync.WaitGroup
    for i := 0; i < 1000; i++ {
        wg.Add(1)
        go func() {
            c.Increment()
            wg.Done()
        }()
    }
    wg.Wait()
    fmt.Println(c.val)
}
```
**Question:** This code prints a number less than 1000. Identify the race condition and fix it using Go's `sync/atomic` package.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: Data race. `c.val++` is not atomic. Multiple goroutines read/write `val` simultaneously. Fix: Use `atomic.Int64` or `atomic.AddInt64`.
```go
import "sync/atomic"

type Counter struct {
    val int64
}
func (c *Counter) Increment() {
    atomic.AddInt64(&c.val, 1)
}
// ... rest same, print atomic.LoadInt64(&c.val)
```
**Skills Tested:** Go concurrency, data races, atomic operations, sync package.

**ID:** AAD-451

```sql
FROM employees
WHERE AVG(salary) > 50000
GROUP BY department_id;
```
**Question:** This query throws a syntax error. Identify the misuse of aggregate functions and fix it.
**Expected Answer:** Error: Cannot use an aggregate function `AVG()` in the `WHERE` clause. `WHERE` filters rows before aggregation. Fix: Use the `HAVING` clause to filter on aggregated results.

```sql
SELECT department_id, AVG(salary)
```

**ID:** AAD-452
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Performance / N+1 Query
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Language:** JavaScript
**Code:**
```javascript
async function getAuthorsWithBooks() {
    const authors = await db.query('SELECT * FROM authors');
    const result = [];
    for (const author of authors) {
        const books = await db.query('SELECT * FROM books WHERE author_id = ?', [author.id]);
        result.push({ ...author, books });
    }
    return result;
}
```
**Question:** This code suffers from the N+1 query problem. Explain the performance impact and refactor it to use a single query or `JOIN`.
**Expected Answer:** Impact: Executes 1 query for authors, then N queries for books (one per author). Extremely slow for large datasets. Fix: Use a `JOIN` or `IN` clause.
```javascript
async function getAuthorsWithBooks() {
    const rows = await db.query(`
        SELECT a.*, b.id as book_id, b.title 
        FROM authors a 
        LEFT JOIN books b ON a.id = b.author_id
    `);
    // Group by author in JS
    const authorsMap = {};
    for (const row of rows) {
        if (!authorsMap[row.id]) {
            authorsMap[row.id] = { ...row, books: [] };
        }
        if (row.book_id) authorsMap[row.id].books.push({ id: row.book_id, title: row.title });
    }
    return Object.values(authorsMap);
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Database performance, N+1 problem, SQL JOINs, ORM patterns.

```javascript
console.log(1);

console.log(4);
```
**Question:** What is the exact output order of this code? Explain the interaction between the call stack, microtask queue, and macrotask queue.
**Expected Answer:** Output: 1, 4, 3, 2. Explanation: 1 and 4 are synchronous (call stack). 3 is a microtask (Promise), which runs before macrotasks. 2 is a macrotask (setTimeout), which runs after microtasks and the next event loop tick.

**ID:** AAD-454
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Security Vulnerability / Insecure Deserialization
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Language:** Python
**Code:**
```python
import pickle
import base64

def load_user_data(encoded_data):
    data = base64.b64decode(encoded_data)
    user = pickle.loads(data)
    return user
```
**Question:** Identify the critical security vulnerability in this deserialization code and explain how an attacker could exploit it. Suggest a safe alternative.

```python
import json

    return json.loads(data)
```

**ID:** AAD-456
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Concurrency / Mutex
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Language:** C++
**Code:**
```cpp
#include <mutex>
#include <vector>

std::mutex mtx;
std::vector<int> data;

void add_data(int val) {
    mtx.lock();
    data.push_back(val);
    // forgot to unlock
}
```
**Question:** This code will eventually deadlock. Identify the issue and provide a modern C++ fix using RAII.
**Expected Answer:** Issue: `mtx.lock()` is called but never unlocked. If an exception occurs or the function returns, the mutex remains locked, causing deadlock on the next call. Fix: Use `std::lock_guard` for RAII unlocking.
```cpp
void add_data(int val) {
    std::lock_guard<std::mutex> lock(mtx);
    data.push_back(val);
}
```

**ID:** AAD-458
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Performance / String Concatenation
**Difficulty Level:** 6
**Language:** Java
**Code:**
```java
public String buildReport(List<String> lines) {
    String report = "";
    for (String line : lines) {
        report += line + "\n";
    }
    return report;
}
```
**Question:** This code is highly inefficient for large lists. Explain why and provide an optimized version.
**Expected Answer:** Issue: Strings are immutable in Java. `report += ...` creates a new String object in every iteration, leading to O(N^2) time complexity and excessive garbage collection. Fix: Use `StringBuilder`.
```java
public String buildReport(List<String> lines) {
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    for (String line : lines) {
        sb.append(line).append("\n");
    }
    return sb.toString();
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Java performance, String immutability, `StringBuilder`, memory efficiency.

**ID:** AAD-460
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / Closure Scope
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Language:** JavaScript
**Code:**
```javascript
const buttons = document.querySelectorAll('button');
for (var i = 0; i < buttons.length; i++) {
    buttons[i].addEventListener('click', function() {
        console.log('Button ' + i + ' clicked');
    });
}
```
**Question:** Clicking any button always logs "Button 5 clicked" (assuming 5 buttons). Explain the closure scope bug and provide two different fixes.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: `var` is function-scoped, not block-scoped. By the time the click handlers execute, the loop has finished, and `i` is 5. All closures share the same `i`. Fix 1: Use `let` instead of `var`. Fix 2: Use an IIFE or `forEach`.
```javascript
// Fix 1
for (let i = 0; i < buttons.length; i++) { ... }

// Fix 2
buttons.forEach((btn, i) => {
    btn.addEventListener('click', () => console.log('Button ' + i));
});
```
**Skills Tested:** JavaScript closures, `var` vs `let`, block scope, event listeners.

**ID:** AAD-463
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / State Mutation
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Language:** JavaScript
**Code:**
```javascript
function updateScores(players) {
    players.forEach(p => p.score += 10);
    return players;
}
const originalPlayers = [{name: 'A', score: 0}, {name: 'B', score: 0}];
const updated = updateScores(originalPlayers);
console.log(originalPlayers[0].score);
```
**Question:** The code logs `10` instead of `0`. Explain why the original array was mutated and provide a pure function alternative.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: Objects in JavaScript are passed by reference. `forEach` mutates the original objects in the array. Fix: Return a new array with new objects (pure function).
```javascript
function updateScores(players) {
    return players.map(p => ({ ...p, score: p.score + 10 }));
```

**ID:** AAD-464
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Concurrency / False Sharing
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Language:** C++
**Code:**
```cpp
#include <thread>
#include <vector>

struct Counter { int val1; int val2; };
Counter c = {0, 0};

void thread1() { for(int i=0; i<1000000; ++i) c.val1++; }
void thread2() { for(int i=0; i<1000000; ++i) c.val2++; }

int main() {
    std::thread t1(thread1);
    std::thread t2(thread2);
    t1.join(); t2.join();
}
```
**Question:** This code runs much slower than expected on multi-core systems. Identify the hardware-level performance bug and fix it using padding.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: False sharing. `val1` and `val2` are adjacent in memory and likely reside on the same CPU cache line. When thread1 modifies `val1`, it invalidates the cache line for thread2, causing excessive cache coherence traffic. Fix: Pad the struct to separate the variables onto different cache lines.
```cpp
struct Counter { 
    int val1; 
    char pad[60]; // Assuming 64-byte cache line
    int val2; 
};
```
**Skills Tested:** C++ concurrency, false sharing, CPU cache lines, hardware-level optimization.

**ID:** AAD-465
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Refactoring / DRY Principle
**Difficulty Level:** 6
**Language:** Python
**Code:**
```python
def process_admin(user):
    if user.role == 'admin':
        log_action(user, 'admin_access')
        grant_admin_privileges(user)
    else:
        raise PermissionError()

def process_editor(user):
    if user.role == 'editor':
        log_action(user, 'editor_access')
        grant_editor_privileges(user)
    else:
        raise PermissionError()
```
**Question:** This code violates the DRY (Don't Repeat Yourself) principle. Refactor it to eliminate duplication.
**Expected Answer:** Refactor using a dictionary mapping or a generic function.
```python
def process_user(user, role, privilege_func):
    if user.role == role:
        log_action(user, f'{role}_access')
        privilege_func(user)
    else:
        raise PermissionError()

# Usage:
process_user(user, 'admin', grant_admin_privileges)
process_user(user, 'editor', grant_editor_privileges)
```
**Skills Tested:** Python refactoring, DRY principle, higher-order functions.

**ID:** AAD-466
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / SQL Window Functions
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Language:** SQL
**Code:**
```sql
SELECT 
    employee_id,
    department_id,
    salary,
    RANK() OVER (ORDER BY salary DESC) as rank
FROM employees;
```
**Question:** The developer wanted to rank employees *within their respective departments*, but this query ranks them across the entire company. Fix the window function.
**Expected Answer:** Fix: Add `PARTITION BY department_id` to the window function.
```sql
SELECT 
    employee_id,
    department_id,
    salary,
    RANK() OVER (PARTITION BY department_id ORDER BY salary DESC) as dept_rank
FROM employees;
```
**Skills Tested:** SQL window functions, `PARTITION BY`, ranking, analytical queries.

**ID:** AAD-467
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Security Vulnerability / Insecure Direct Object Reference
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Language:** JavaScript
**Code:**
```javascript
app.get('/invoice/:id', (req, res) => {
    const invoice = db.findInvoice(req.params.id);
    res.json(invoice);
});
```
**Question:** Identify the security vulnerability that allows users to access other users' invoices and provide a fix.
**Expected Answer:** Vulnerability: Insecure Direct Object Reference (IDOR). The endpoint retrieves an invoice by ID without verifying if the logged-in user owns it. Fix: Check ownership.
```javascript
app.get('/invoice/:id', (req, res) => {
    const invoice = db.findInvoice(req.params.id);
    if (!invoice || invoice.userId !== req.user.id) {
        return res.status(403).send('Forbidden');
    }
    res.json(invoice);
});
```
**Skills Tested:** Web security, IDOR, authorization, access control.

**ID:** AAD-468
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Performance / Lazy Evaluation
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Language:** Python
**Code:**
```python
def get_large_dataset():
    return [x**2 for x in range(10000000)]

def process():
    data = get_large_dataset()
    for item in data:
        if item > 100:
            print(item)
            break
```
**Question:** This code consumes excessive memory. Refactor it using generators to implement lazy evaluation.
**Expected Answer:** Fix: Use a generator expression or `yield` to produce items on demand.
```python
def get_large_dataset():
    return (x**2 for x in range(10000000))

def process():
    data = get_large_dataset()
    for item in data:
        if item > 100:
            print(item)
            break
```
**Skills Tested:** Python performance, generators, lazy evaluation, memory efficiency.

**ID:** AAD-469
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Syntax Error / Ownership
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Language:** Rust
**Code:**
```rust
fn main() {
    let s1 = String::from("hello");
    let s2 = s1;
    println!("{}", s1);
}
```
**Question:** This code fails to compile with a "value used here after move" error. Explain Rust's ownership model and how to fix it.
**Expected Answer:** Error: `String` does not implement `Copy`. Assigning `s1` to `s2` moves ownership. `s1` is no longer valid. Fix: Clone the string or use a reference.
```rust
// Fix 1: Clone
let s2 = s1.clone();

// Fix 2: Reference
let s2 = &s1;
```
**Skills Tested:** Rust ownership, move semantics, `Clone` vs references.

**ID:** AAD-470
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Logical Bug / Async Waterfall
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Language:** JavaScript
**Code:**
```javascript
async function processUsers(userIds) {
    userIds.forEach(async (id) => {
        const user = await fetchUser(id);
        await updateUser(user);
    });
    console.log('All users processed');
}
```
**Question:** The "All users processed" log appears before the users are actually updated. Explain why `forEach` fails with async/await and provide the correct approach.
**Expected Answer:** Bug: `forEach` does not wait for async callbacks to complete. It fires them all concurrently and moves on. Fix: Use `for...of` for sequential execution, or `Promise.all` with `map` for concurrent execution.
```javascript
// Sequential
async function processUsers(userIds) {
    for (const id of userIds) {
        const user = await fetchUser(id);
        await updateUser(user);
    }
    console.log('All users processed');
}

// Concurrent
async function processUsers(userIds) {
    await Promise.all(userIds.map(async (id) => {
        const user = await fetchUser(id);
        await updateUser(user);
    }));
    console.log('All users processed');
}
```
**Skills Tested:** JavaScript async/await, `forEach` limitations, `Promise.all`, concurrency control.

**ID:** AAD-471
**Category:** Code Debugging & Analysis
**Subcategory:** Refactoring / SOLID Principles
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Language:** Java
**Code:**
```java
public class Animal {
    public void makeSound() {
        // generic sound
    }
}
public class Dog extends Animal {
    @Override
    public void makeSound() { System.out.println("Woof"); }
}
public class Cat extends Animal {
    @Override
    public void makeSound() { System.out.println("Meow"); }
}
public class AnimalHandler {
    public void handle(Dog d) { d.makeSound(); }
    public void handle(Cat c) { c.makeSound(); }
}
```
**Question:** This code violates the Open/Closed Principle and the Liskov Substitution Principle. Refactor `AnimalHandler` to properly use polymorphism.
**Expected Answer:** Refactor `AnimalHandler` to accept the base `Animal` type, allowing it to work with any current or future subclass without modification.
```java
public class AnimalHandler {
    public void handle(Animal a) {
        a.makeSound();
    }
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Java polymorphism, SOLID principles, Liskov Substitution, Open/Closed Principle.

**ID:** AAD-472
**Category:** Humanities
**Subcategory:** Philosophy — Ethics
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** In a variation of the Trolley Problem, the single person on the side track is the surgeon who invented the very mechanism that stops the trolley. A Kantian deontologist and a Rule Utilitarian are asked whether to pull the lever. Analyze how each framework would rule on this specific variation, focusing on the concepts of "using as a means" and "rule internalization."
**Expected Answer:** A Kantian would argue against pulling the lever if pulling it treats the surgeon merely as a means to save the five, violating the categorical imperative. However, if the surgeon's death is a foreseen but unintended side effect (Doctrine of Double Effect), it might be permissible. A Rule Utilitarian would evaluate the long-term consequences of adopting a rule that allows sacrificing inventors/creators. They would likely rule against pulling the lever, as a rule permitting the sacrifice of key societal contributors would destroy incentives for innovation and reduce overall utility in the long run.
**Skills Tested:** Applied ethical theory, nuanced philosophical distinction, Rule Utilitarianism vs. Kantianism.

**ID:** AAD-473
**Category:** Humanities
**Subcategory:** History — Comparative Modernization
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Compare the Tanzimat Reforms in the Ottoman Empire (1839-1876) with the Meiji Restoration in Japan (1868-1912). Both were responses to Western imperial pressure. Explain why the Meiji Restoration resulted in a centralized industrial powerhouse while the Tanzimat Reforms ultimately failed to prevent the Ottoman Empire's dissolution, focusing on land tenure and military reform.
**Expected Answer:** Meiji successfully abolished feudal land tenure (han system) and centralized tax collection, funding rapid industrialization and a conscripted national army loyal to the state. The Tanzimat attempted to modernize land tenure (Land Code of 1858) but inadvertently empowered local notables (ayan) and tribal leaders who registered land in their own names, decentralizing power. Furthermore, Ottoman military reforms relied heavily on foreign mercenaries and debt, whereas Japan sent state-sponsored missions (Iwakura) to systematically absorb Western technology, maintaining sovereign control over their modernization process.
**Skills Tested:** Comparative history, institutional analysis, economic history.

**ID:** AAD-474
**Category:** Humanities
**Subcategory:** Linguistics — Historical Linguistics
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain Jespersen’s Cycle in the context of grammaticalization. Trace the cycle of negation from Old French to Modern colloquial French, and explain the cognitive/linguistic mechanism that drives the renewal of the negative marker.
**Expected Answer:** Jespersen’s Cycle describes the historical process by which a language's negative marker is renewed. In Old French, negation was a single pre-verbal particle "ne". Due to phonetic reduction and semantic bleaching, "ne" lost its emphatic force. Speakers began adding minimizers (like "pas" meaning step, "point" meaning dot) for emphasis: "ne... pas". Over time, "pas" grammaticalized into the primary negative marker, and "ne" became optional, eventually dropping entirely in Modern colloquial French ("Je sais pas"). The driving mechanism is the need to maintain expressive force (pragmatic strengthening) as original markers undergo phonetic erosion and semantic bleaching.
**Skills Tested:** Historical linguistics, grammaticalization, semantic bleaching.

**ID:** AAD-475
**Category:** Humanities
**Subcategory:** Art History — Iconography
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** In Jan van Eyck’s *Arnolfini Portrait* (1434), a convex mirror is painted on the back wall. Analyze the iconographic significance of the mirror, specifically regarding the reflection it shows, the frame's medallions, and its function as a "witness" in the context of 15th-century marriage contracts.
**Expected Answer:** The convex mirror reflects the backs of the couple and two other figures in the doorway, one believed to be van Eyck himself (indicated by the signature "Johannes de eyck fuit hic. 1434" above the mirror). The ten medallions on the frame depict the Passion of Christ, linking the marital union to divine sacrifice and sanctity. In the context of 15th-century Flanders, marriages could be legally binding through mutual consent (verba de praesenti) without a priest. The mirror, showing the witnesses, acts as a visual legal affidavit, proving the presence of witnesses to validate the clandestine marriage contract.
**Skills Tested:** Art historical analysis, iconography, 15th-century legal/social history.

**ID:** AAD-476
**Category:** Humanities
**Subcategory:** Literature — Narrative Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Compare the narrative unreliability in Vladimir Nabokov’s *Lolita* with Kazuo Ishiguro’s *The Remains of the Day*. How does Humbert Humbert’s unreliability differ fundamentally from Stevens’ unreliability in terms of authorial intent, reader complicity, and the ultimate revelation of truth?
**Expected Answer:** Humbert Humbert’s unreliability is active, manipulative, and malicious; he uses aesthetic brilliance to seduce the reader (the "jury") into complicity with his crimes, and the truth is hidden through deliberate obfuscation and stylistic dazzle. Stevens’ unreliability is passive, psychological, and tragic; he uses the language of duty and professionalism to repress his own emotions and avoid confronting his complicity in a morally bankrupt system. The reader is not seduced by Stevens but pities him. Humbert forces the reader to confront their own moral failings; Stevens forces the reader to confront the tragedy of self-deception.
**Skills Tested:** Literary analysis, narrative theory, comparative literature.

**ID:** AAD-477
**Category:** Humanities
**Subcategory:** Philosophy of Mind — Epistemology
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Frank Jackson’s "Knowledge Argument" (Mary’s Room) posits that a super-scientist who knows all physical facts about color vision but has never seen color learns something new upon seeing red for the first time. Explain the "Ability Hypothesis" response to this argument (championed by David Lewis) and evaluate its strength.
**Expected Answer:** The Ability Hypothesis argues that Mary does not learn a new *propositional fact* (qualia) when she sees red; rather, she acquires new *abilities* (know-how). She learns how to recognize, imagine, and remember the experience of red. Since physicalism only requires that all *facts* are physical, and Mary only gained abilities, physicalism is not disproven. Evaluation: Critics argue that knowing how to recognize red implies knowing *what it is like* to see red, which seems to be a factual state of knowledge (phenomenal knowledge) that cannot be reduced merely to a set of behavioral abilities.
**Skills Tested:** Philosophy of mind, epistemology, qualia debate.

**ID:** AAD-478
**Category:** Humanities
**Subcategory:** History — Global Economic History
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Analyze the macroeconomic impact of the Potosí silver mines (discovered 1545) on Ming Dynasty China. How did the influx of New World silver via the Manila Galleons and European trade routes alter the Chinese economy and ultimately contribute to the Ming collapse?
**Expected Answer:** The Ming Dynasty had transitioned to a silver-based economy (Single Whip Reform) but lacked domestic silver reserves. The massive influx of cheap New World silver (via Acapulco-Manila and European绕航) caused severe inflation in China and disrupted the traditional copper-silver exchange rate. When the Spanish crown restricted silver exports in the early 17th century, and Japanese silver exports halted, China experienced a severe silver shortage (deflation). Peasants, who paid taxes in silver but earned in copper, faced crushing tax burdens, leading to widespread rebellions (Li Zicheng) that, combined with the Little Ice Age and Manchu invasions, caused the Ming collapse.
**Skills Tested:** Global history, macroeconomic history, monetary policy.

**ID:** AAD-479
**Category:** Humanities
**Subcategory:** Linguistics — Syntax and Typology
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Contrast ergative-absolutive morphosyntactic alignment (as seen in Basque) with nominative-accusative alignment (as seen in Latin). Explain how the verb agreement and case marking differ for transitive vs. intransitive subjects in both systems.
**Expected Answer:** In nominative-accusative (Latin), the subject of an intransitive verb (S) and the subject of a transitive verb (A) are marked the same way (nominative case, same verb agreement), while the object (O) is marked differently (accusative). In ergative-absolutive (Basque), the subject of an intransitive verb (S) and the object of a transitive verb (O) are marked the same way (absolutive case, unmarked), while the subject of a transitive verb (A) is marked differently (ergative case, takes a suffix like -k). Verb agreement in Basque must index the absolutive argument (S or O) and the ergative argument (A) separately, unlike Latin which primarily indexes the nominative subject.
**Skills Tested:** Linguistic typology, syntax, morphosyntactic alignment.

**ID:** AAD-480
**Category:** Humanities
**Subcategory:** Ethics — AI Philosophy
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Apply Aristotle’s concept of *phronesis* (practical wisdom) to the problem of AI alignment. Why is a purely rule-based or utility-maximizing AI insufficient for ethical decision-making in novel, real-world scenarios, and how might *phronesis* be computationally modeled?
**Expected Answer:** Aristotle argued that ethics cannot be reduced to rigid rules (like Kant) or simple calculations (like Utilitarianism); it requires *phronesis*—the context-sensitive ability to perceive the morally salient features of a unique situation and deliberate toward the mean. A purely rule-based AI fails when rules conflict in novel scenarios; a utility-maximizer fails when values are incommensurable or unquantifiable. Modeling *phronesis* computationally requires moving beyond static reward functions to context-aware, narrative-based learning, where the AI develops a "perceptual" capacity to recognize moral salience through vast exposure to human ethical reasoning, case studies, and nuanced feedback, rather than explicit programming.
**Skills Tested:** Virtue ethics, AI alignment, computational philosophy.

**ID:** AAD-481
**Category:** Humanities
**Subcategory:** Musicology — Form and Analysis
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Trace the evolution of the Sonata-Allegro form from its origins in the Scarlatti/Kuhnau binary forms to its expansion in Beethoven’s middle period. Specifically, what structural innovations did Beethoven introduce to the Development and Coda sections?
**Expected Answer:** Early binary forms (Scarlatti) featured two sections of roughly equal length, with the second section developing motifs from the first. By the Classical era (Haydn/Mozart), this evolved into Sonata form with distinct Exposition, Development, and Recapitulation. Beethoven radically expanded the structural weight of the Development section, transforming it from a brief transitional passage into a massive, dramatic arena for thematic fragmentation, extreme modulatory exploration, and contrapuntal complexity. Furthermore, Beethoven elevated the Coda from a mere concluding cadence to a "second development," providing profound structural and emotional resolution that balances the expanded Development section.
**Skills Tested:** Music theory, historical analysis, formal analysis.
