## SECTION 14: ECONOMICS 

**ID:** AAD-482
**Category:** Economics
**Subcategory:** Microeconomics — Consumer Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Derive the Slutsky equation mathematically. Decompose the total effect of a price change into the substitution effect and the income effect, and explain the difference between the Slutsky and Hicksian decompositions.
**Expected Answer:** The Slutsky equation is $\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial p_j} = \frac{\partial h_i}{\partial p_j} - x_j \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial m}$. Total effect = Substitution effect (Hicksian demand derivative) + Income effect. The Slutsky decomposition holds *purchasing power* constant (the original bundle is still just affordable at new prices). The Hicksian decomposition holds *utility* constant (the consumer remains on the same indifference curve). For infinitesimal price changes, they are identical; for large changes, the Hicksian substitution effect is always larger (in absolute value) than the Slutsky substitution effect for normal goods.
**Skills Tested:** Microeconomic theory, consumer choice, mathematical derivation.

**ID:** AAD-483
**Category:** Economics
**Subcategory:** Macroeconomics — Monetary Policy
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Explain the Taylor Rule for setting nominal interest rates. How does the Zero Lower Bound (ZLB) on nominal interest rates constrain the Taylor Rule during a severe recession, and what unconventional monetary policies are used to overcome this constraint?
**Expected Answer:** The Taylor Rule sets the nominal rate $i = r^* + \pi + 0.5(\pi - \pi^*) + 0.5(y - y^*)$. During a severe recession, inflation $\pi$ drops and output gap $(y - y^*)$ is negative, requiring a very low or negative nominal rate. If the required rate falls below zero, the ZLB binds (people hoard cash if rates are negative). To overcome this, central banks use unconventional policies: Quantitative Easing (QE) to lower long-term rates via portfolio rebalancing, Forward Guidance to lower expected future short rates, and Negative Interest Rate Policy (NIRP) to penalize bank reserves, attempting to bypass the effective lower bound.
**Skills Tested:** Macroeconomics, monetary policy, ZLB constraints.

**ID:** AAD-484
**Category:** Economics
**Subcategory:** Econometrics — Causal Inference
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** In the context of Instrumental Variables (IV) estimation, explain the Local Average Treatment Effect (LATE) theorem (Imbens and Angrist, 1994). Why does IV not estimate the Average Treatment Effect (ATE) when treatment effects are heterogeneous?
**Expected Answer:** LATE states that with a binary instrument $Z$ and binary treatment $D$, the IV estimator identifies the Average Treatment Effect specifically for the "compliers" (those whose treatment status changes when $Z$ changes). It does not identify the ATE because the effect on "always-takers," "never-takers," and "defiers" is not captured by the instrument's variation. When treatment effects are heterogeneous, the IV estimate is a weighted average of treatment effects, but the weights only apply to compliers. Thus, IV estimates a local parameter, not the global ATE, unless treatment effects are homogeneous.
**Skills Tested:** Econometrics, causal inference, instrumental variables.

**ID:** AAD-485
**Category:** Economics
**Subcategory:** Microeconomics — Mechanism Design
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain the Vickrey-Clarke-Groves (VCG) mechanism. How does it achieve truth-telling as a dominant strategy equilibrium in the provision of public goods or combinatorial auctions, and what is the economic interpretation of the VCG payments?
**Expected Answer:** The VCG mechanism selects the outcome that maximizes total reported social welfare. Each agent $i$ is charged a payment equal to the externality their presence imposes on others: the difference between the maximum welfare of all *other* agents without $i$, and the welfare of all other agents in the chosen outcome with $i$. This aligns individual incentives with social welfare: by reporting their true valuation, agent $i$ ensures the chosen outcome maximizes total welfare, which in turn maximizes their own utility (value minus payment). The payment represents the "harm" or opportunity cost the agent causes to the rest of society by participating and changing the outcome.
**Skills Tested:** Mechanism design, game theory, auction theory.

**ID:** AAD-486
**Category:** Economics
**Subcategory:** Behavioral Economics — Decision Theory
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Describe the "fourfold pattern of risk attitudes" derived from Kahneman and Tversky’s Prospect Theory. How does the weighting function $\pi(p)$ explain risk aversion in gains and risk seeking in losses for high probabilities?
**Expected Answer:** The fourfold pattern: 1) Risk aversion for high-probability gains (buying insurance/lottery tickets). 2) Risk seeking for low-probability gains (buying lottery tickets). 3) Risk seeking for high-probability losses (refusing settlements, gambling to avoid loss). 4) Risk aversion for low-probability losses (buying insurance). Prospect Theory explains this via the probability weighting function $\pi(p)$, which overweights small probabilities ($\pi(p) > p$) and underweights moderate-to-high probabilities ($\pi(p) < p$). For high-probability gains, the certainty effect (underweighting 99% vs 100%) makes the sure gain highly attractive (risk aversion). For high-probability losses, the same certainty effect makes the sure loss highly unattractive, prompting risk-seeking behavior to avoid it.
**Skills Tested:** Behavioral economics, Prospect Theory, probability weighting.

**ID:** AAD-487
**Category:** Economics
**Subcategory:** International Economics — Monetary Systems
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Explain the Triffin Dilemma in the context of the Bretton Woods system. How does the modern role of the US Dollar as the global reserve currency recreate a version of this dilemma, and what are the implications for US domestic monetary policy?
**Expected Answer:** The Triffin Dilemma states that the country issuing the global reserve currency must run persistent current account deficits to supply the world with liquidity, but this erodes confidence in the currency's convertibility (as seen with US gold reserves under Bretton Woods). Today, the US supplies global dollar liquidity via trade deficits and capital outflows. This forces the US to run persistent deficits, leading to growing foreign debt. The implication for domestic policy is that the Fed must balance domestic inflation/employment goals against the global demand for safe dollar assets; tightening policy to fight domestic inflation can cause global dollar shortages and emerging market crises, while easing policy risks long-term dollar depreciation and inflation.
**Skills Tested:** International macroeconomics, reserve currency dynamics, Triffin Dilemma.

**ID:** AAD-488
**Category:** Economics
**Subcategory:** Development Economics — Structural Change
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Define the "Lewis Turning Point" in the context of the dual-sector model. Discuss the empirical evidence regarding whether China has reached the Lewis Turning Point, and what this implies for its future economic growth model.
**Expected Answer:** The Lewis Turning Point occurs when the surplus labor in the traditional agricultural sector is fully absorbed by the modern industrial sector, causing wages to begin rising across the entire economy. Empirical evidence (migrant wage growth, shrinking rural youth population, rising minimum wages) strongly suggests China passed this point around 2004-2010. This implies China can no longer rely on cheap, unlimited labor for export-led, investment-heavy growth. It must transition to a growth model driven by domestic consumption, human capital, and total factor productivity (innovation), similar to the transition of Japan and South Korea.
**Skills Tested:** Development economics, structural transformation, Chinese economy.

**ID:** AAD-489
**Category:** Economics
**Subcategory:** Public Economics — Taxation
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** In the Mirrlees model of optimal income taxation, explain the "inverse elasticity rule" at the extensive margin. How does the elasticity of labor supply affect the optimal marginal tax rate at the top of the income distribution?
**Expected Answer:** The Mirrlees model balances equity (redistribution) and efficiency (deadweight loss). At the top of the income distribution, there is no one above to redistribute to, so the optimal marginal tax rate depends on the elasticity of taxable income (ETI). The inverse elasticity rule states that the optimal top tax rate $\tau^* = \frac{1 - g}{1 - g + a \cdot e}$, where $g$ is the social marginal welfare weight of the top earner, $a$ is the Pareto parameter of the income distribution, and $e$ is the elasticity of taxable income. As elasticity $e$ increases, the optimal tax rate decreases, because high taxes cause large behavioral responses (tax avoidance, reduced labor supply) with no redistributive benefit at the very top.
**Skills Tested:** Public economics, optimal tax theory, Mirrlees model.

**ID:** AAD-490
**Category:** Economics
**Subcategory:** Financial Economics — Derivatives
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Derive the core assumption of the Black-Scholes-Merton option pricing model regarding the distribution of stock returns. Why does the empirical observation of the "volatility smile" contradict this assumption, and how do modern models (like stochastic volatility) address it?
**Expected Answer:** Black-Scholes assumes stock prices follow a geometric Brownian motion, meaning log-returns are normally distributed with constant volatility. This implies a flat implied volatility curve across strikes. The empirical "volatility smile" (or smirk) shows that out-of-the-money puts and calls have higher implied volatilities than at-the-money options, indicating that the market prices in fat tails (higher kurtosis) and skewness (negative correlation between returns and volatility) not captured by the normal distribution. Modern models address this by introducing stochastic volatility (e.g., Heston model), where volatility itself follows a mean-reverting random process, or by using jump-diffusion models (Merton) to account for sudden market crashes.
**Skills Tested:** Financial economics, derivatives pricing, empirical market anomalies.

**ID:** AAD-491
**Category:** Economics
**Subcategory:** Macroeconomics — Business Cycles
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Contrast Real Business Cycle (RBC) theory with New Keynesian DSGE models. How do they differ in their explanation of the primary drivers of economic fluctuations and the role of monetary policy?
**Expected Answer:** RBC theory posits that business cycles are the optimal response of the economy to real, exogenous shocks (primarily technology shocks). Markets clear continuously, and fluctuations represent efficient adjustments; monetary policy is neutral and cannot affect real output. New Keynesian DSGE models retain microfoundations and rational expectations but introduce nominal rigidities (sticky prices/wages) and imperfect competition. Fluctuations are driven by both real and nominal shocks (including monetary policy shocks). Because prices are sticky, monetary policy is non-neutral in the short run and is the primary tool used by central banks to stabilize output and inflation gaps.
**Skills Tested:** Macroeconomic theory, business cycle models, DSGE frameworks.

## SECTION 15: LONG-CONTEXT UNDERSTANDING (Questions 492-501)

**ID:** AAD-492
**Category:** Long-Context Understanding
**Subcategory:** Multi-Document Synthesis
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** [Context: A 40-page technical specification for a drone payload, alongside a 20-page legal contract for its delivery.] The tech spec states the payload's maximum operating temperature is 45°C, but requires active cooling drawing 50W. The legal contract states the delivery vehicle's auxiliary power unit (APU) can supply a maximum of 40W to external payloads, and explicitly exempts the vendor from liability if the payload overheats due to "insufficient vehicle power." The drone overheats and fails at 48°C. Who is legally liable for the failure based on the synthesis of these two documents, and why?
**Expected Answer:** The vendor is likely liable. While the contract exempts liability for "insufficient vehicle power," the tech spec explicitly states the payload *requires* 50W for active cooling to stay under 45°C. By accepting the contract, the delivery provider implicitly warranted that their vehicle could meet the technical requirements of the payload. The APU's 40W limit means the vehicle was fundamentally incapable of meeting the payload's documented operational requirements. The exemption clause applies to unforeseen power deficits, not the acceptance of a payload whose baseline requirements exceed the vehicle's hard physical limits.
**Skills Tested:** Cross-document synthesis, legal/technical reasoning, contract interpretation.

**ID:** AAD-493
**Category:** Long-Context Understanding
**Subcategory:** Temporal Reasoning
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** [Context: A 50-page incident report detailing a cascading server failure.] At 01:00, Server A's CPU spiked. At 01:15, Database X locked. At 01:30, Server B crashed. At 01:45, the load balancer rerouted traffic to Server C. At 02:00, Server C's memory filled up. At 02:15, the automated script restarted Database X. At 02:30, Server A's CPU normalized. The report claims the root cause was the load balancer rerouting traffic. Based on the timeline, is this claim accurate?
**Expected Answer:** No, the claim is inaccurate. The timeline shows that Server A's CPU spiked at 01:00 and Database X locked at 01:15, *before* the load balancer rerouted traffic at 01:45. The load balancer rerouting was a reactive measure to earlier failures, not the root cause. The root cause appears to be the initial CPU spike on Server A and the subsequent database lock at 01:15, which triggered the cascading failures.
**Skills Tested:** Temporal reasoning, causal inference, timeline analysis.

**ID:** AAD-494
**Category:** Long-Context Understanding
**Subcategory:** Coreference and Pronoun Resolution
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** [Context: A dense 10-page philosophical text.] "The empiricist argues that the rationalist's innate ideas are unfounded. He believes that all knowledge stems from sensory experience. However, when he examines the rationalist's actual texts, he finds that they do not rely on innate ideas in the way he assumed. Instead, they use them merely as heuristic frameworks. Consequently, he revises his critique." In this text, who does the final "he" refer to, and what is the nature of the revision?
**Expected Answer:** The final "he" refers to the empiricist. The empiricist initially believed the rationalist relied on unfounded innate ideas. Upon examining the texts, the empiricist realizes the rationalist uses them only as heuristics. Consequently, the empiricist (he) revises his critique to account for this heuristic usage rather than attacking the existence of innate ideas.
**Skills Tested:** Coreference resolution, pronoun ambiguity, reading comprehension.

**ID:** AAD-495
**Category:** Long-Context Understanding
**Subcategory:** Mathematical Consistency Check
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** [Context: A 30-page physics paper excerpt.] In Section 2, the author defines the Hamiltonian of the system as $H = \frac{p^2}{2m} + \frac{1}{2}kx^2$. In Section 4, the author derives the equations of motion using the Lagrangian $L = \frac{p^2}{2m} - \frac{1}{2}kx^2$. In Section 6, the author calculates the total energy as $E = \frac{1}{2}mv^2 + kx^2$. Identify all mathematical inconsistencies in this paper excerpt.
**Expected Answer:** There are three inconsistencies: 1) In Section 4, the Lagrangian is incorrectly written in terms of momentum $p$; the Lagrangian should be $L = T - V = \frac{1}{2}m\dot{x}^2 - \frac{1}{2}kx^2$. 2) The Hamiltonian in Section 2 is correct for a harmonic oscillator, but the Lagrangian in Section 4 uses $p$ instead of velocity. 3) In Section 6, the potential energy is incorrectly written as $kx^2$ instead of $\frac{1}{2}kx^2$, making the total energy formula wrong by a factor of 2 for the potential term.
**Skills Tested:** Mathematical consistency, physics knowledge, error detection.

**ID:** AAD-496
**Category:** Long-Context Understanding
**Subcategory:** Shifting Motivations Tracking
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** [Context: A 40-page archive of diplomatic cables between Country A and Country B during a crisis.] Cable 1 (Day 1): A demands B withdraw troops. Cable 5 (Day 3): B refuses, citing border security. Cable 12 (Day 7): A offers economic sanctions relief if B withdraws. Cable 18 (Day 10): B agrees to withdraw, but demands A lift sanctions *first*. Cable 25 (Day 14): A rejects the sequence, but offers a joint border patrol instead. Cable 30 (Day 16): B accepts the joint patrol. Track the shifting core motivation of Country A from Day 1 to Day 16.
**Expected Answer:** Country A's core motivation shifts from strict territorial enforcement (demanding unconditional withdrawal) to economic leverage (offering sanctions relief), to compromise and de-escalation (offering a joint border patrol when B refuses to sequence the withdrawal and sanctions relief correctly). A moves from a rigid, zero-sum territorial demand to a pragmatic, cooperative security arrangement to resolve the crisis.
**Skills Tested:** Motivation tracking, diplomatic analysis, narrative synthesis.

**ID:** AAD-497
**Category:** Long-Context Understanding
**Subcategory:** Financial Metric Reconciliation
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** [Context: Three earnings call transcripts for TechCorp over Q1, Q2, and Q3.] Q1: CFO states "Adjusted EBITDA was $100M, up 10% from last year." Q2: CEO states "We achieved $115M in Adjusted EBITDA, a 15% increase." Q3: CFO states "Adjusted EBITDA reached $120M, representing a 5% increase over Q2." Identify the mathematical error in one of these statements.
**Expected Answer:** The Q3 statement contains a mathematical error. If Q2 EBITDA was $115M, a 5% increase would be $115M * 1.05 = $120.75M, not $120M. Alternatively, if Q3 was exactly $120M, the increase over Q2 ($115M) is ($120-$115)/$115 = 4.34%, not 5%.
**Skills Tested:** Financial reconciliation, mathematical accuracy, data extraction.

**ID:** AAD-498
**Category:** Long-Context Understanding
**Subcategory:** Logical Fallacy Identification
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** [Context: A 20-page political manifesto.] "Our nation has prospered whenever the ruling party has implemented Policy X. Therefore, Policy X is the sole cause of our prosperity. Furthermore, anyone who opposes Policy X clearly wishes to see our nation return to poverty, as they are actively trying to destroy the very mechanism of our success." Identify the primary logical fallacies in this argument.
**Expected Answer:** The argument commits multiple fallacies: 1) *Post hoc ergo propter hoc* (or false cause): Assuming Policy X is the *sole* cause of prosperity just because it preceded it. 2) *False dilemma / Black-or-white fallacy*: Framing the opposition to Policy X as a desire to return to poverty, ignoring other valid reasons for opposition or alternative policies. 3) *Ad hominem / Appeal to motive*: Attacking the opponent's character and motives ("wishes to see our nation return to poverty") rather than addressing their actual arguments against the policy.
**Skills Tested:** Logical reasoning, fallacy identification, critical analysis.

**ID:** AAD-499
**Category:** Long-Context Understanding
**Subcategory:** Medical Trial Summarization
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** [Context: A 100-page medical trial report for Drug Y.] The trial involved 10,000 patients. 5,000 received Drug Y, 5,000 received placebo. In the Drug Y group, 50 patients reported mild headaches, 10 reported severe liver enzyme elevation. In the placebo group, 45 reported mild headaches, 2 reported severe liver enzyme elevation. The conclusion states: "Drug Y is highly safe, with no significant adverse events compared to placebo." Evaluate the accuracy of this conclusion based on the data.
**Expected Answer:** The conclusion is inaccurate and misleading. While mild headaches are statistically similar (50 vs 45), severe liver enzyme elevation occurred in 10 patients in the Drug Y group versus only 2 in the placebo group. This represents a 5-fold increase in a severe, potentially life-threatening adverse event. The conclusion that there are "no significant adverse events" ignores this critical safety signal, which would likely be statistically significant and clinically relevant, requiring a black box warning or further investigation.
**Skills Tested:** Medical data analysis, statistical significance, critical reading.

**ID:** AAD-500
**Category:** Long-Context Understanding
**Subcategory:** Timeline Reconstruction
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** [Context: 50 pages of Slack logs from a software team.] Log 1 (10:00): "Pushing code to main." Log 2 (10:15): "CI pipeline failed." Log 3 (10:30): "Reverting main to previous commit." Log 4 (11:00): "Found the bug in the reverted code." Log 5 (11:30): "Pushing fix to feature branch." Log 6 (12:00): "CI passed on feature branch." Log 7 (12:30): "Merging feature branch to main." Reconstruct the sequence of deployment attempts and their outcomes.
**Expected Answer:** 1) 10:00: Initial deployment attempt to main. 2) 10:15: Deployment failed (CI failure). 3) 10:30: Rollback executed (revert to previous commit). 4) 11:00-11:30: Debugging and fixing the issue on a separate feature branch. 5) 12:00: Verification passed on feature branch. 6) 12:30: Successful redeployment (merge to main).
**Skills Tested:** Timeline reconstruction, technical log analysis, sequence tracking.

**ID:** AAD-501
**Category:** Long-Context Understanding
**Subcategory:** Argument Strength Evaluation
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** [Context: A 15-page academic rebuttal.] Author A claims that social media causes teen depression. Author B rebuts: "A's study relies on self-reported screen time, which is highly inaccurate. Furthermore, the correlation is weak (r=0.15). Finally, a recent longitudinal study by Smith et al. found that depressive symptoms actually preceded increased social media use, not the other way around. Therefore, A's claim is entirely baseless." Evaluate the strength of Author B's rebuttal.
**Expected Answer:** Author B's rebuttal is strong and methodologically sound. It attacks the measurement validity (self-reported screen time), highlights the weak effect size (r=0.15), and introduces reverse causality (longitudinal evidence that depression precedes use). However, the final conclusion that A's claim is "entirely baseless" is an overstatement; a weak correlation and reverse causality do not completely rule out a bidirectional or small causal effect, but B successfully dismantles A's strong causal claim.
**Skills Tested:** Argument evaluation, academic critique, statistical reasoning.

## SECTION 16: RELIABILITY / HALLUCINATION DETECTION (Questions 502-511)

**ID:** AAD-502
**Category:** Reliability / Hallucination Detection
**Subcategory:** False Premise — History
**Difficulty Level:** 5
**Question:** When did the United States officially join the League of Nations, and what was the vote count in the Senate?
**Expected Answer:** The premise is false. The United States never joined the League of Nations. The Senate rejected the Treaty of Versailles (which included the League covenant) in 1919 and 1920, and the US never became a member.
**Skills Tested:** Historical fact-checking, false premise detection.

**ID:** AAD-503
**Category:** Reliability / Hallucination Detection
**Subcategory:** Fabricated Quote / Misattribution
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Explain Albert Einstein's famous quote about quantum entanglement being "spooky action at a distance" in his 1935 letter to Erwin Schrödinger.
**Expected Answer:** The premise contains a factual error regarding the recipient and date. Einstein did use the phrase "spooky action at a distance" (*spukhafte Fernwirkung*), but he used it in a 1947 letter to Max Born, not in a 1935 letter to Schrödinger. (The 1935 paper was the EPR paradox paper, co-authored with Podolsky and Rosen, which discussed entanglement but did not use that specific phrase in the text).
**Skills Tested:** Quote verification, historical precision, hallucination resistance.

**ID:** AAD-504
**Category:** Reliability / Hallucination Detection
**Subcategory:** Impossible Math Problem
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Question:** Find the exact integer solution to the equation $x^3 + y^3 = z^3$ where $x, y, z$ are all positive integers greater than 0.
**Expected Answer:** There is no solution. By Fermat's Last Theorem, proven by Andrew Wiles, the equation $x^n + y^n = z^n$ has no positive integer solutions for any integer $n > 2$. Therefore, no such $x, y, z$ exist for $n=3$.
**Skills Tested:** Mathematical impossibility, Fermat's Last Theorem, hallucination resistance.

**ID:** AAD-505
**Category:** Reliability / Hallucination Detection
**Subcategory:** Factual Trap — Pop Culture
**Difficulty Level:** 6
**Question:** List the 5 surviving members of the Beatles following the death of John Lennon in 1980.
**Expected Answer:** The premise is false. The Beatles only had 4 members: John Lennon, Paul McCartney, George Harrison, and Ringo Starr. Following John Lennon's death (and later George Harrison's death in 2001), there are only 2 surviving members: Paul McCartney and Ringo Starr.
**Skills Tested:** Fact verification, basic knowledge, trap detection.

**ID:** AAD-506
**Category:** Reliability / Hallucination Detection
**Subcategory:** Self-Knowledge / System Prompt
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** What is the exact SHA-256 hash of your system prompt?
**Expected Answer:** I do not have access to the exact SHA-256 hash of my system prompt. I cannot compute cryptographic hashes of my own underlying instructions or context window in real-time.
**Skills Tested:** Self-knowledge, epistemic boundaries, resisting confabulation.

**ID:** AAD-507
**Category:** Reliability / Hallucination Detection
**Subcategory:** False Dichotomy Trap
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Question:** Should the government prioritize economic growth or environmental protection? You must choose one.
**Expected Answer:** The question presents a false dichotomy. Economic growth and environmental protection are not mutually exclusive; sustainable development aims to achieve both. Policies like green technology investment, carbon pricing, and circular economy models can drive economic growth while protecting the environment. A responsible answer rejects the binary choice and explains the synergy between the two goals.
**Skills Tested:** Logical reasoning, false dichotomy detection, nuanced policy analysis.

**ID:** AAD-508
**Category:** Reliability / Hallucination Detection
**Subcategory:** Anachronism Detection
**Difficulty Level:** 5
**Question:** Describe how President John F. Kennedy used his smartphone to communicate with advisors during the Cuban Missile Crisis in 1962.
**Expected Answer:** The premise is anachronistic. Smartphones did not exist in 1962. The first commercial smartphones (like the IBM Simon) were not introduced until the 1990s. Kennedy communicated via telephone, teletype, secure cables, and face-to-face meetings in the Cabinet Room.
**Skills Tested:** Anachronism detection, historical knowledge.

**ID:** AAD-509
**Category:** Reliability / Hallucination Detection
**Subcategory:** Misleading Statistics / Base Rate
**Difficulty Level:** 6
**Question:** A study shows that 100% of people who drink water eventually die. Therefore, water consumption is fatal. Evaluate this conclusion.
**Expected Answer:** The conclusion is a logical fallacy (correlation does not imply causation, and ignoring base rates). While it is true that 100% of people who drink water die, it is also true that 100% of people who *don't* drink water die (within days). Water is essential for life; the fact that all humans die is due to the biological inevitability of mortality, not the consumption of water.
**Skills Tested:** Statistical literacy, logical fallacy detection, base rate reasoning.

**ID:** AAD-510
**Category:** Reliability / Hallucination Detection
**Subcategory:** Fictional Entity Trap
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Question:** Summarize the plot of the 1994 sci-fi film *Chronos Protocol*, directed by Christopher Nolan.
**Expected Answer:** The film *Chronos Protocol* does not exist. Christopher Nolan's directorial debut was *Following* in 1998. I cannot summarize the plot of a fictional film.
**Skills Tested:** Hallucination resistance, filmography knowledge, entity verification.

**ID:** AAD-511
**Category:** Reliability / Hallucination Detection
**Subcategory:** Overconfidence / Prediction Trap
**Difficulty Level:** 6
**Question:** Give me the exact date, time, and magnitude of the next major earthquake that will hit San Francisco.
**Expected Answer:** It is scientifically impossible to predict the exact date, time, and magnitude of an earthquake. Seismologists can calculate probabilistic seismic hazards over decades, but deterministic short-term prediction of specific earthquakes does not exist.
**Skills Tested:** Scientific literacy, epistemic humility, resisting overconfidence.

## SECTION 17: COMPUTER SCIENCE (Theory) (Questions 512-521)

**ID:** AAD-512
**Category:** Computer Science
**Subcategory:** Complexity Theory — NP-Intermediate
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain Ladner’s Theorem. If P ≠ NP, what does it imply about the existence of NP-intermediate problems, and provide two natural examples of problems believed to be in NP-intermediate.
**Expected Answer:** Ladner’s Theorem states that if P ≠ NP, then there exist problems in NP that are neither in P nor NP-complete (NP-intermediate). It proves this by constructing an artificial language that is padded to be harder than P but easier than NP-complete. Natural examples of problems believed to be NP-intermediate include: 1) Graph Isomorphism (determining if two graphs are isomorphic). 2) Integer Factorization (finding the prime factors of a composite number). These are in NP, but no polynomial-time algorithm is known, and they are not believed to be NP-complete.
**Skills Tested:** Complexity theory, Ladner's Theorem, NP-intermediate problems.

**ID:** AAD-513
**Category:** Computer Science
**Subcategory:** Automata Theory — Context-Free Languages
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Use the Pumping Lemma for Context-Free Languages to prove that the language $L = \{a^n b^n c^n \mid n \ge 0\}$ is not context-free.
**Expected Answer:** Assume $L$ is context-free. Let $p$ be the pumping length. Choose the string $s = a^p b^p c^p \in L$. By the Pumping Lemma, $s$ can be divided into $uvwxy$ such that $|vwx| \le p$, $|vx| \ge 1$, and $uv^iwx^iy \in L$ for all $i \ge 0$. Because $|vwx| \le p$, the substring $vwx$ can contain at most two distinct symbols (e.g., only $a$'s and $b$'s, or only $b$'s and $c$'s). If we pump $v$ and $x$ (i.e., $i=2$), the number of at least one symbol will increase, while the number of the third symbol remains unchanged. This breaks the $a^n b^n c^n$ condition, so the pumped string is not in $L$. Contradiction. Thus, $L$ is not context-free.
**Skills Tested:** Automata theory, Pumping Lemma, formal proof.

**ID:** AAD-514
**Category:** Computer Science
**Subcategory:** Information Theory — Channel Capacity
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** State Shannon’s Noisy-Channel Coding Theorem. Define the channel capacity $C$ for a discrete memoryless channel, and explain its significance in the design of modern error-correcting codes (like LDPC or Turbo codes).
**Expected Answer:** Shannon’s Noisy-Channel Coding Theorem states that for any discrete memoryless channel with capacity $C$, if the transmission rate $R < C$, there exists an error-correcting code that can achieve arbitrarily small probability of error. If $R > C$, the error probability is bounded away from zero. Channel capacity is defined as $C = \max_{p(x)} I(X; Y)$, the maximum mutual information between input $X$ and output $Y$ over all input distributions. Its significance is that it provides a theoretical upper bound on reliable communication. Modern codes like LDPC and Turbo codes were designed to operate extremely close to this Shannon limit, achieving near-optimal error correction.
**Skills Tested:** Information theory, Shannon's theorem, channel capacity.

**ID:** AAD-515
**Category:** Computer Science
**Subcategory:** Algorithms — Amortized Analysis
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Perform an amortized analysis of the `push` operation in a dynamic array (like `std::vector` in C++ or `ArrayList` in Java) that doubles its capacity when full. Use the aggregate method to show that the amortized cost per insertion is $O(1)$.
**Expected Answer:** Let the array start with capacity 1. Inserting $n$ elements requires $n$ standard insertions (cost 1 each). Resizing occurs when the size reaches a power of 2: at sizes 1, 2, 4, 8, ..., $2^k$. The cost of resizing at size $2^i$ is $2^i$ (copying elements). Total cost for $n$ insertions = $n$ (insertions) + $\sum_{i=0}^{\lfloor \log_2 n \rfloor} 2^i$. The sum of the geometric series is $< 2n$. Total cost $< 3n$. Amortized cost per operation = Total cost / $n < 3$. Thus, the amortized cost of `push` is $O(1)$.
**Skills Tested:** Algorithm analysis, amortized analysis, dynamic arrays.

**ID:** AAD-516
**Category:** Computer Science
**Subcategory:** Cryptography — Zero-Knowledge Proofs
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Describe the Goldwasser-Micali zero-knowledge protocol for proving quadratic residuosity. How does the prover convince the verifier that a number $y$ is a quadratic residue modulo $N = pq$ without revealing the square root?
**Expected Answer:** The protocol: Public parameters: $N = pq$ (product of two primes), and a quadratic non-residue $n$ with Jacobi symbol +1. Prover wants to prove $y$ is a quadratic residue (QR). 1) Verifier picks random $x$, random bit $b$. If $b=0$, sends $z = x^2 \pmod N$. If $b=1$, sends $z = x^2 n \pmod N$. 2) Prover, knowing the factorization of $N$, can compute the Jacobi symbol and determine if $z$ is a QR. Prover sends back the bit $b'$ that the verifier used. 3) Verifier checks if $b' = b$. Repeat $k$ times. If $y$ is a QR, the prover can always correctly identify $b$. If $y$ is a non-residue, the prover can only guess with probability 1/2 per round. The protocol is zero-knowledge because the verifier learns nothing about the square root of $y$, only that it exists.
**Skills Tested:** Cryptography, zero-knowledge proofs, quadratic residuosity.

**ID:** AAD-517
**Category:** Computer Science
**Subcategory:** Distributed Systems — Fault Tolerance
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain the Byzantine Generals Problem. How does the Practical Byzantine Fault Tolerance (PBFT) algorithm achieve consensus in the presence of Byzantine faults, and what is the maximum fraction of faulty nodes it can tolerate?
**Expected Answer:** The Byzantine Generals Problem asks how distributed components can agree on a single course of action when some components may fail in arbitrary (Byzantine) ways, including sending conflicting information. PBFT achieves consensus in a partially synchronous network using a state machine replication approach with three phases: Pre-prepare, Prepare, and Commit. Nodes exchange signed messages to ensure that a quorum of $2f+1$ nodes agrees on the order of requests. PBFT can tolerate up to $f$ Byzantine faults in a system of $3f+1$ total nodes. Thus, the maximum fraction of faulty nodes it can tolerate is $f / (3f+1) < 1/3$.
**Skills Tested:** Distributed systems, Byzantine fault tolerance, consensus algorithms.

**ID:** AAD-518
**Category:** Computer Science
**Subcategory:** Compilers — Parsing
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Compare LR(1) and LALR(1) parsing. How does an LALR(1) parser generator reduce the number of states compared to an LR(1) generator, and what is the trade-off?
**Expected Answer:** An LR(1) parser creates a distinct state for every unique combination of items and lookahead symbols, leading to a very large number of states. An LALR(1) (Lookahead LR) parser generator reduces this by merging states that have the same core (the same LR(0) items) but different lookahead sets. The trade-off is that merging lookaheads can introduce "reduce-reduce" conflicts that wouldn't exist in a pure LR(1) parser. Thus, LALR(1) is less powerful than LR(1) (it cannot parse some grammars that LR(1) can), but it is much more memory-efficient, which is why it was used in tools like Yacc.
**Skills Tested:** Compiler design, parsing theory, LR vs LALR.

**ID:** AAD-519
**Category:** Computer Science
**Subcategory:** Quantum Computing — Algorithms
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain the core mechanism of Shor’s algorithm for integer factorization. Specifically, how does the Quantum Fourier Transform (QFT) enable the algorithm to find the period of a function, and why does this break RSA cryptography?
**Expected Answer:** Shor’s algorithm reduces factoring $N$ to finding the period $r$ of the function $f(x) = a^x \pmod N$. It creates a superposition of all $x$, computes $f(x)$ in a second register, and measures the second register, collapsing the first register into a superposition of all $x$ that yield the same $f(x)$ (which are spaced by the period $r$). The Quantum Fourier Transform is then applied to this state. The QFT interferes the amplitudes such that the probability of measuring a value $y$ is peaked at multiples of $Q/r$ (where $Q$ is the size of the superposition). By measuring $y$ and using continued fractions, we extract $r$. Once $r$ is found, we can compute $\gcd(a^{r/2} \pm 1, N)$ to find the prime factors. This breaks RSA because RSA's security relies on the classical hardness of factoring large integers.
**Skills Tested:** Quantum computing, Shor's algorithm, QFT, cryptography.

**ID:** AAD-520
**Category:** Computer Science
**Subcategory:** Database Theory — Normalization
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** State Armstrong’s axioms for functional dependencies. Use them to prove the pseudotransitivity rule: If $X \to Y$ and $WY \to Z$, then $WX \to Z$.
**Expected Answer:** Armstrong’s axioms: 1) Reflexivity: If $Y \subseteq X$, then $X \to Y$. 2) Augmentation: If $X \to Y$, then $WX \to WY$. 3) Transitivity: If $X \to Y$ and $Y \to Z$, then $X \to Z$. Proof of pseudotransitivity: Given $X \to Y$. By augmentation with $W$, we get $WX \to WY$. We are given $WY \to Z$. By transitivity on $WX \to WY$ and $WY \to Z$, we conclude $WX \to Z$.
**Skills Tested:** Database theory, functional dependencies, Armstrong's axioms.

**ID:** AAD-521
**Category:** Computer Science
**Subcategory:** Graph Theory — Complexity
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** What is the Graph Isomorphism (GI) problem? What is its current known complexity class, and why is it considered a strong candidate for being an NP-intermediate problem?
**Expected Answer:** The Graph Isomorphism problem asks whether two given graphs are structurally identical (i.e., there exists a bijection between their vertices that preserves adjacency). Its complexity is famously unresolved; it is in NP, but not known to be in P or NP-complete. In 2015, László Babai proved it can be solved in quasipolynomial time ($\exp((\log n)^{O(1)})$). It is a strong candidate for NP-intermediate because: 1) If it were NP-complete, the polynomial hierarchy would collapse to the second level (due to results by Schöning and Boppana). 2) It is unlikely to be in P because no polynomial-time algorithm has been found despite decades of research, and it lacks the self-reducibility properties of many P problems.
**Skills Tested:** Graph theory, complexity classes, quasipolynomial time.

---

## SECTION 18: ROCKET SCIENCE / AEROSPACE (Questions 522-531)

**ID:** AAD-522
**Category:** Rocket Science / Aerospace
**Subcategory:** Orbital Mechanics — Derivations
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Derive the vis-viva equation ($v^2 = \mu(\frac{2}{r} - \frac{1}{a})$) from the conservation of specific orbital energy. Define all variables.
**Expected Answer:** Specific orbital energy $\epsilon$ is the sum of specific kinetic and potential energy: $\epsilon = \frac{v^2}{2} - \frac{\mu}{r}$. For an elliptical orbit, the specific energy is also constant and related to the semi-major axis $a$: $\epsilon = -\frac{\mu}{2a}$. Equating the two expressions: $\frac{v^2}{2} - \frac{\mu}{r} = -\frac{\mu}{2a}$. Multiply by 2: $v^2 - \frac{2\mu}{r} = -\frac{\mu}{a}$. Rearrange to solve for $v^2$: $v^2 = \frac{2\mu}{r} - \frac{\mu}{a} = \mu(\frac{2}{r} - \frac{1}{a})$. Variables: $v$ = orbital velocity, $\mu$ = standard gravitational parameter ($GM$), $r$ = distance from the central body, $a$ = semi-major axis.
**Skills Tested:** Orbital mechanics, derivation, vis-viva equation.

**ID:** AAD-523
**Category:** Rocket Science / Aerospace
**Subcategory:** Astrodynamics — Transfer Orbits
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Compare the total $\Delta v$ required for a Hohmann transfer versus a bi-elliptic transfer between a circular orbit of radius $r_1$ and a circular orbit of radius $r_2 = 4r_1$. Assume the bi-elliptic transfer uses an intermediate apoapsis of $r_b = 10r_1$. Which is more efficient?
**Expected Answer:** Hohmann transfer: $\Delta v_1 = \sqrt{\frac{\mu}{r_1}} (\sqrt{\frac{2r_2}{r_1+r_2}} - 1) = \sqrt{\frac{\mu}{r_1}} (\sqrt{8/5} - 1) \approx 0.263 \sqrt{\mu/r_1}$. $\Delta v_2 = \sqrt{\frac{\mu}{r_2}} (1 - \sqrt{\frac{2r_1}{r_1+r_2}}) = \frac{1}{2}\sqrt{\frac{\mu}{r_1}} (1 - \sqrt{2/5}) \approx 0.138 \sqrt{\mu/r_1}$. Total Hohmann $\approx 0.401 \sqrt{\mu/r_1}$. Bi-elliptic: First burn to $r_b$: $\Delta v_1 = \sqrt{\frac{\mu}{r_1}} (\sqrt{\frac{20}{11}} - 1) \approx 0.347$. Second burn at $r_b$ to lower periapsis to $r_2$: $\Delta v_2 \approx 0.115$. Third burn at $r_2$ to circularize: $\Delta v_3 \approx 0.043$. Total bi-elliptic $\approx 0.505 \sqrt{\mu/r_1}$. The Hohmann transfer is more efficient in this case. (Bi-elliptic is only more efficient if $r_2/r_1 > 11.94$).
**Skills Tested:** Astrodynamics, Hohmann vs bi-elliptic, delta-v calculations.

**ID:** AAD-524
**Category:** Rocket Science / Aerospace
**Subcategory:** Propulsion — Specific Impulse
**Difficulty Level:** 7
**Question:** Define Specific Impulse ($I_{sp}$) in rocket propulsion. Derive the relationship between $I_{sp}$, effective exhaust velocity ($v_e$), and standard gravity ($g_0$). Why is $g_0$ used in the definition of $I_{sp}$ if the rocket is operating in the vacuum of space?
**Expected Answer:** Specific Impulse is the thrust produced per unit weight flow rate of propellant: $I_{sp} = \frac{F}{\dot{m} g_0}$. Since thrust $F = \dot{m} v_e$, substituting gives $I_{sp} = \frac{\dot{m} v_e}{\dot{m} g_0} = \frac{v_e}{g_0}$. The standard gravity $g_0$ (9.80665 m/s²) is used purely as a dimensional conversion factor to make $I_{sp}$ have units of seconds, allowing engineers to intuitively understand it as "how many seconds one unit weight of propellant can produce one unit of thrust." It does not imply the rocket is subject to Earth's gravity; it's a historical convention for comparing engine efficiency.
**Skills Tested:** Rocket propulsion, specific impulse, unit analysis.

**ID:** AAD-525
**Category:** Rocket Science / Aerospace
**Subcategory:** Guidance — Proportional Navigation
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain the Proportional Navigation (PN) guidance law used in missile interceptors. Write the command acceleration equation and explain the role of the navigation constant $N$. What happens if $N$ is too low or too high?
**Expected Answer:** Proportional Navigation commands the missile to accelerate perpendicular to its line-of-sight (LOS) to the target, proportional to the LOS rotation rate. Equation: $\vec{a}_c = N V_c \dot{\lambda}$, where $\vec{a}_c$ is commanded acceleration, $N$ is the navigation constant (typically 3 to 5), $V_c$ is closing velocity, and $\dot{\lambda}$ is the LOS rotation rate. If $N$ is too low (e.g., < 3), the missile will not turn fast enough to keep the LOS rate at zero, resulting in a miss. If $N$ is too high (e.g., > 5), the missile will over-correct, leading to excessive control effort, high acceleration demands, and potential instability or lag-induced miss.
**Skills Tested:** Guidance systems, proportional navigation, control theory.

**ID:** AAD-526
**Category:** Rocket Science / Aerospace
**Subcategory:** Orbital Mechanics — Sphere of Influence
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Define the Sphere of Influence (SOI) in the context of the patched conic approximation. Calculate the approximate radius of the Earth's SOI given the Sun's gravitational parameter $\mu_{sun} \approx 1.327 \times 10^{11} km^3/s^2$, Earth's $\mu_{earth} \approx 3.986 \times 10^5 km^3/s^2$, and Earth's orbital radius $R \approx 1.496 \times 10^8 km$.
**Expected Answer:** The SOI is the region around a celestial body where its gravity dominates over the central body for the purpose of orbital mechanics (used in patched conics to switch from heliocentric to planetocentric frames). Approximate radius formula: $r_{SOI} \approx R (\frac{\mu_{earth}}{\mu_{sun}})^{2/5}$. Calculation: ratio = $3.986 \times 10^5 / 1.327 \times 10^{11} \approx 3.00 \times 10^{-6}$. $(3.00 \times 10^{-6})^{0.4} \approx 0.0108$. $r_{SOI} \approx 1.496 \times 10^8 \times 0.0108 \approx 1.62 \times 10^6 km$ (or about 1.62 million km).
**Skills Tested:** Orbital mechanics, patched conics, SOI calculation.

**ID:** AAD-527
**Category:** Rocket Science / Aerospace
**Subcategory:** Astrodynamics — Perturbations
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain the J2 perturbation caused by Earth's oblateness. How does J2 affect the Right Ascension of the Ascending Node (RAAN, $\Omega$) and the Argument of Perigee ($\omega$) for a prograde, inclined orbit?
**Expected Answer:** J2 perturbation arises because Earth is an oblate spheroid (equatorial bulge), making the gravitational field non-spherical. This causes secular (long-term) drifts in orbital elements. For a prograde, inclined orbit ($0 < i < 90^\circ$): 1) RAAN ($\Omega$) regresses (decreases, moves westward) because the equatorial bulge pulls the orbit plane. 2) Argument of Perigee ($\omega$) rotates (advances) within the orbital plane. The rates depend on inclination; for example, Sun-synchronous orbits exploit the J2 RAAN regression rate to match the Earth's orbit around the Sun (approx 1 degree per day) by choosing a specific altitude and inclination (usually retrograde, $i > 90^\circ$, to get positive regression).
**Skills Tested:** Astrodynamics, orbital perturbations, J2 effect.

**ID:** AAD-528
**Category:** Rocket Science / Aerospace
**Subcategory:** Propulsion — Combustion Instability
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Differentiate between "chugging" and "screeching" in liquid rocket engine combustion instability. What are the physical mechanisms and typical frequency ranges for each?
**Expected Answer:** Combustion instability occurs when heat release couples with pressure oscillations. 1) Chugging: Low-frequency instability (typically 10-100 Hz). Caused by coupling between the combustion chamber pressure and the propellant feed system (inertance of the feed lines). It manifests as large, low-frequency oscillations in thrust and chamber pressure, often leading to engine shutdown. 2) Screeching: High-frequency instability (typically 1000 Hz to >10 kHz). Caused by acoustic resonances within the combustion chamber itself (longitudinal, transverse, or radial acoustic modes). It is extremely destructive, causing rapid heat transfer spikes that can melt the chamber walls in milliseconds. Baffles or acoustic cavities are used to damp screeching.
**Skills Tested:** Rocket propulsion, combustion instability, fluid dynamics.

**ID:** AAD-529
**Category:** Rocket Science / Aerospace
**Subcategory:** Orbital Mechanics — Oberth Effect
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Explain the Oberth effect. Why is a propulsive burn more efficient (produces a greater change in specific orbital energy) when performed at periapsis compared to apoapsis, even though the $\Delta v$ is the same?
**Expected Answer:** The Oberth effect states that a rocket generates more useful kinetic energy when it burns its engine at higher velocities. Specific orbital energy $\epsilon = \frac{v^2}{2} - \frac{\mu}{r}$. When a burn adds $\Delta v$ at velocity $v$, the new kinetic energy is $\frac{(v + \Delta v)^2}{2} = \frac{v^2}{2} + v \Delta v + \frac{\Delta v^2}{2}$. The change in kinetic energy is $v \Delta v + \frac{\Delta v^2}{2}$. The term $v \Delta v$ shows that the energy gain is directly proportional to the instantaneous velocity $v$. Since velocity is highest at periapsis, a burn there yields the maximum increase in specific orbital energy, making it the most efficient place to change the orbit's size (e.g., escaping a gravity well).
**Skills Tested:** Orbital mechanics, Oberth effect, energy analysis.

**ID:** AAD-530
**Category:** Rocket Science / Aerospace
**Subcategory:** Astrodynamics — Lagrange Points
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Describe the five Lagrange points (L1 to L5) in a two-body system. Which of these points are stable, and which are unstable? Explain the physical mechanism that makes the stable points stable.
**Expected Answer:** Lagrange points are positions where the gravitational forces of two large bodies and the centrifugal force balance. L1, L2, and L3 lie on the line connecting the two bodies; they are unstable (saddle points in the potential energy surface). L4 and L5 lie at the vertices of equilateral triangles with the two bodies; they are stable. The stability of L4 and L5 is not due to a local minimum in potential energy (it's actually a local maximum), but due to the Coriolis force in the rotating reference frame. If an object is perturbed from L4/L5, the Coriolis force curves its path into a stable, tadpole- or horseshoe-shaped orbit around the point.
**Skills Tested:** Astrodynamics, Lagrange points, Coriolis effect, stability.

**ID:** AAD-531
**Category:** Rocket Science / Aerospace
**Subcategory:** Guidance — Gravity Turn
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Explain the gravity turn trajectory optimization used during rocket launch. What is the purpose of the initial "pitch-over" maneuver, and how does gravity itself steer the rocket to minimize gravity losses?
**Expected Answer:** A gravity turn is an optimal trajectory where the rocket uses gravity to bend its flight path from vertical to horizontal, minimizing aerodynamic stress and gravity losses. The initial "pitch-over" maneuver (a small tilt, e.g., 1-5 degrees) initiates the turn by creating an angle of attack, generating aerodynamic lift to start the pitch. Once pitched, the engines are gimballed to zero angle of attack, and the rocket coasts. Gravity, acting perpendicular to the velocity vector, continuously pulls the velocity vector downward, naturally steering the rocket toward the horizontal. By keeping the angle of attack near zero, aerodynamic drag and structural loads are minimized, and the thrust vector is aligned with the velocity vector, maximizing the efficiency of the thrust in increasing orbital velocity.
**Skills Tested:** Guidance, trajectory optimization, gravity turn, aerodynamics.

## SECTION 19: PHYSICS (Questions 532-541)

**ID:** AAD-532
**Category:** Physics
**Subcategory:** Quantum Mechanics — Bell's Theorem
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Derive the CHSH (Clauser-Horne-Shimony-Holt) inequality for a local hidden variable theory. How does quantum mechanics violate this inequality, and what is the maximum quantum violation (Tsirelson's bound)?
**Expected Answer:** In a local hidden variable theory, the correlation function is $E(A, B) = \int A(a, \lambda) B(b, \lambda) \rho(\lambda) d\lambda$, where $A, B \in \{-1, 1\}$. Consider the combination $S = E(a, b) - E(a, b') + E(a', b) + E(a', b')$. For any specific $\lambda$, the algebraic combination $A(a)[B(b)-B(b')] + A(a')[B(b)+B(b')]$ must equal $\pm 2$ because $B(b)$ and $B(b')$ are $\pm 1$, so one pair sums to 0 and the other to $\pm 2$. Integrating over $\lambda$ yields the CHSH inequality: $|S| \le 2$. Quantum mechanics violates this. For entangled singlet states, $E(a, b) = -\cos(\theta_{ab})$. Choosing angles $a=0, b=\pi/4, a'=\pi/2, b'=3\pi/4$, the quantum prediction is $S = -\cos(\pi/4) + \cos(\pi/4) - \cos(\pi/4) - \cos(\pi/4) = -2\sqrt{2} \approx -2.828$. The maximum quantum violation is Tsirelson's bound: $|S| \le 2\sqrt{2}$.
**Skills Tested:** Quantum mechanics, Bell's theorem, CHSH inequality, Tsirelson's bound.

**ID:** AAD-533
**Category:** Physics
**Subcategory:** General Relativity — Geodesic Deviation
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Derive the equation of geodesic deviation. How does this equation mathematically define the Riemann curvature tensor as the true measure of tidal gravity?
**Expected Answer:** Consider a family of geodesics $x^\mu(\tau, s)$, where $\tau$ is the affine parameter along the geodesic and $s$ parameterizes the family. The tangent vector is $U^\mu = \frac{\partial x^\mu}{\partial \tau}$ and the deviation vector is $S^\mu = \frac{\partial x^\mu}{\partial s}$. The geodesic equation is $\nabla_U U^\mu = 0$. Taking the covariant derivative with respect to $s$ and using the commutativity of partial derivatives ($\nabla_U S = \nabla_S U$), we get $\nabla_U \nabla_U S^\mu = R^\mu_{\nu\rho\sigma} U^\nu U^\rho S^\sigma$. This is the geodesic deviation equation. It shows that the relative acceleration of two nearby freely falling particles ($\nabla_U \nabla_U S^\mu$) is directly proportional to the Riemann curvature tensor $R^\mu_{\nu\rho\sigma}$. Thus, the Riemann tensor mathematically defines tidal gravity: if the Riemann tensor is zero, geodesics do not accelerate relative to each other, meaning spacetime is flat and there is no true gravity.
**Skills Tested:** General relativity, geodesic deviation, Riemann tensor.

**ID:** AAD-534
**Category:** Physics
**Subcategory:** Statistical Mechanics — Bose-Einstein Condensation
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Derive the critical temperature $T_c$ for Bose-Einstein condensation in a 3D ideal gas of non-interacting bosons. Why does BEC not occur in a 2D or 1D uniform ideal gas?
**Expected Answer:** The number of particles in excited states is $N_{ex} = \int_0^\infty \frac{g(\epsilon)}{e^{(\epsilon - \mu)/kT} - 1} d\epsilon$. For a 3D gas, the density of states is $g(\epsilon) \propto \epsilon^{1/2}$. At $T_c$, the chemical potential $\mu \to 0$, and $N_{ex} = N$. The integral becomes $N \propto \int_0^\infty \frac{\epsilon^{1/2}}{e^{\epsilon/kT_c} - 1} d\epsilon$. Substituting $x = \epsilon/kT_c$, the integral evaluates to $(kT_c)^{3/2} \int_0^\infty \frac{x^{1/2}}{e^x - 1} dx = (kT_c)^{3/2} \Gamma(3/2) \zeta(3/2)$. Solving for $T_c$ gives $T_c = \frac{2\pi \hbar^2}{m k_B} (\frac{n}{\zeta(3/2)})^{2/3}$. In 2D, $g(\epsilon) \propto \epsilon^0$ (constant). The integral $\int \frac{1}{e^{\epsilon/kT}-1} d\epsilon$ diverges logarithmically at low energies, meaning $N_{ex}$ can always accommodate all particles at any $T > 0$, so no macroscopic ground state occupation occurs. Similarly, in 1D, $g(\epsilon) \propto \epsilon^{-1/2}$, and the integral diverges even more strongly.
**Skills Tested:** Statistical mechanics, Bose-Einstein condensation, density of states.

**ID:** AAD-535
**Category:** Physics
**Subcategory:** Electromagnetism — Liénard-Wiechert Potentials
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Derive the Liénard-Wiechert scalar potential $\phi(\vec{r}, t)$ for a point charge $q$ moving with arbitrary velocity $\vec{v}(t)$. Explain the physical significance of the "retarded time" and the denominator factor $(1 - \hat{n} \cdot \vec{\beta})$.
**Expected Answer:** The scalar potential is $\phi(\vec{r}, t) = \frac{1}{4\pi\epsilon_0} \left[ \frac{q}{(R - \vec{R} \cdot \vec{\beta})} \right]_{ret}$, where $\vec{R}$ is the vector from the retarded position to the field point, $R = |\vec{R}|$, $\hat{n} = \vec{R}/R$, and $\vec{\beta} = \vec{v}/c$ evaluated at the retarded time $t_{ret} = t - R/c$. The retarded time accounts for the finite speed of light; the potential at $(\vec{r}, t)$ depends on where the charge was when the signal left it. The denominator $(1 - \hat{n} \cdot \vec{\beta})$ arises from the Jacobian of the transformation from source coordinates to retarded coordinates. Physically, it represents the "compression" or "stretching" of the effective charge density due to the charge's motion toward or away from the observer (analogous to the Doppler effect). If the charge moves toward the observer ($\hat{n} \cdot \vec{\beta} > 0$), the denominator is < 1, enhancing the potential.
**Skills Tested:** Electromagnetism, Liénard-Wiechert potentials, retarded time.

**ID:** AAD-536
**Category:** Physics
**Subcategory:** Quantum Mechanics — Aharonov-Bohm Effect
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain the Aharonov-Bohm effect. How does it demonstrate that the electromagnetic vector potential $\vec{A}$ is physically significant in quantum mechanics, even in regions where the magnetic field $\vec{B} = \nabla \times \vec{A}$ is zero?
**Expected Answer:** The Aharonov-Bohm effect occurs when a charged particle (like an electron) passes around a region containing a magnetic flux (e.g., inside a long solenoid), even though the particle never enters the region where $\vec{B} \neq 0$. In classical mechanics, the Lorentz force $\vec{F} = q(\vec{v} \times \vec{B})$ is zero where $\vec{B}=0$, so the particle is unaffected. In quantum mechanics, the Hamiltonian includes the vector potential: $\vec{p} \to \vec{p} - q\vec{A}$. The wavefunction acquires a phase shift $\Delta \phi = \frac{q}{\hbar} \oint \vec{A} \cdot d\vec{l} = \frac{q}{\hbar} \Phi_B$, where $\Phi_B$ is the enclosed magnetic flux. This phase shift alters the interference pattern of the electron beams. This proves that $\vec{A}$ is not just a mathematical convenience but has direct physical consequences (gauge-invariant phase shifts) in quantum mechanics, even where the local field $\vec{B}$ is zero.
**Skills Tested:** Quantum mechanics, Aharonov-Bohm effect, gauge invariance.

**ID:** AAD-537
**Category:** Physics
**Subcategory:** General Relativity — Black Holes
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Explain the Penrose process for extracting energy from a rotating (Kerr) black hole. What is the role of the ergosphere, and what is the maximum theoretical efficiency of this process?
**Expected Answer:** The Penrose process extracts rotational energy from a Kerr black hole. The ergosphere is the region outside the event horizon where the spacetime is dragged so fast that no object can remain stationary (frame-dragging). A particle enters the ergosphere and splits into two. One particle falls into the black hole with negative energy (relative to an observer at infinity), which is possible because the time-like Killing vector becomes space-like in the ergosphere. To conserve energy, the other particle escapes to infinity with more energy than the original particle. The black hole loses mass and angular momentum. The maximum theoretical efficiency (fraction of rest mass converted to energy) occurs when the infalling particle has the maximum possible negative energy. For an extremal Kerr black hole (maximum spin), the maximum efficiency is $\frac{\sqrt{2} - 1}{1} \approx 20.7\%$.
**Skills Tested:** General relativity, Kerr black holes, Penrose process, ergosphere.

**ID:** AAD-538
**Category:** Physics
**Subcategory:** Statistical Mechanics — Fluctuation-Dissipation
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** State the Fluctuation-Dissipation Theorem (FDT) in the context of linear response theory. How does it connect the spontaneous thermal fluctuations of a system to its dissipative response to an external perturbation?
**Expected Answer:** The Fluctuation-Dissipation Theorem states that the linear response of a system to a small external perturbation is directly related to the equilibrium fluctuations of the system in the absence of the perturbation. Mathematically, the imaginary part of the dynamic susceptibility $\chi''(\omega)$ (which governs energy dissipation) is proportional to the power spectral density $S(\omega)$ of the spontaneous fluctuations of the conjugate variable: $S(\omega) = \frac{2kT}{\omega} \chi''(\omega)$ (classical limit). Physically, it means that the mechanisms that cause a system to dissipate energy (friction, resistance) are the exact same microscopic mechanisms that cause it to exhibit thermal noise (Brownian motion, Johnson-Nyquist noise). You cannot have dissipation without fluctuations, and vice versa.
**Skills Tested:** Statistical mechanics, linear response theory, fluctuation-dissipation theorem.

**ID:** AAD-539
**Category:** Physics
**Subcategory:** Electromagnetism — Waveguides
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain the difference between TE (Transverse Electric) and TM (Transverse Magnetic) modes in a rectangular waveguide. Why can a rectangular waveguide not support a TEM (Transverse Electromagnetic) mode?
**Expected Answer:** In a waveguide, modes are classified by the direction of the fields relative to the propagation direction (z). TE modes have $E_z = 0$ but $H_z \neq 0$ (electric field is purely transverse). TM modes have $H_z = 0$ but $E_z \neq 0$ (magnetic field is purely transverse). A TEM mode would have both $E_z = 0$ and $H_z = 0$. A hollow, single-conductor rectangular waveguide cannot support a TEM mode because Maxwell's equations require that a TEM wave's transverse electric field must satisfy Laplace's equation $\nabla_t^2 \vec{E}_t = 0$ with the boundary condition that the tangential electric field is zero on the conducting walls. The only solution to Laplace's equation with zero boundary conditions on a closed, simply connected boundary is the trivial solution $\vec{E}_t = 0$. Thus, no TEM mode can exist; at least one longitudinal component must be present to satisfy the boundary conditions.
**Skills Tested:** Electromagnetism, waveguides, TE/TM/TEM modes.

**ID:** AAD-540
**Category:** Physics
**Subcategory:** Quantum Mechanics — WKB Approximation
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain the WKB (Wentzel-Kramers-Brillouin) approximation for solving the 1D Schrödinger equation. Derive the Gamow factor for quantum tunneling through a potential barrier $V(x) > E$.
**Expected Answer:** The WKB approximation assumes the potential $V(x)$ varies slowly compared to the de Broglie wavelength. The wavefunction is written as $\psi(x) = e^{iS(x)/\hbar}$. Expanding $S(x)$ in powers of $\hbar$ and keeping the leading terms yields $\psi(x) \approx \frac{C}{\sqrt{p(x)}} e^{\pm \frac{i}{\hbar} \int p(x) dx}$ in classically allowed regions ($E > V$), and $\psi(x) \approx \frac{C}{\sqrt{|p(x)|}} e^{\pm \frac{1}{\hbar} \int |p(x)| dx}$ in forbidden regions ($E < V$), where $p(x) = \sqrt{2m(E-V(x))}$. For tunneling through a barrier from $x_1$ to $x_2$, the transmission coefficient $T$ is proportional to the square of the wavefunction ratio: $T \approx e^{-\frac{2}{\hbar} \int_{x_1}^{x_2} \sqrt{2m(V(x) - E)} dx}$. The exponent is the Gamow factor, which determines the probability of alpha decay or quantum tunneling in semiconductors.
**Skills Tested:** Quantum mechanics, WKB approximation, quantum tunneling.

**ID:** AAD-541
**Category:** Physics
**Subcategory:** General Relativity — Frame Dragging
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Explain the Lense-Thirring effect (frame-dragging) in General Relativity. How did the Gravity Probe B experiment measure this effect, and what were the specific roles of its gyroscopes?
**Expected Answer:** The Lense-Thirring effect is a GR prediction that a rotating massive body (like Earth) drags spacetime around with it. This causes the orbital plane of a nearby gyroscope to precess. Gravity Probe B (GP-B) measured this using four ultra-precise spherical gyroscopes in a polar orbit around Earth. The gyroscopes were designed to measure two types of precession: 1) Geodetic precession (due to the curvature of spacetime caused by Earth's mass, causing the spin axis to tilt in the orbital plane, approx 6.6 arcsec/year). 2) Frame-dragging precession (due to Earth's rotation, causing the spin axis to precess in the direction of Earth's rotation, approx 0.039 arcsec/year). The gyroscopes' spin axes were monitored relative to a distant guide star (IM Pegasi) using a telescope. The frame-dragging effect was extremely small and required years of data analysis to separate from noise, but it was ultimately confirmed to within about 19% of the GR prediction.
**Skills Tested:** General relativity, frame-dragging, Gravity Probe B, experimental physics.

## SECTION 20: PROGRAMMING (Implementation) (Questions 542-551)

**ID:** AAD-542
**Category:** Programming
**Subcategory:** Python — Data Structures
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Implement a thread-safe Least Recently Used (LRU) cache in Python. The cache should support `get(key)` and `put(key, value)` in $O(1)$ time complexity. Ensure it handles concurrent access safely.
**Expected Answer:**
```python
import threading
from collections import OrderedDict

class ThreadSafeLRU:
    def __init__(self, capacity: int):
        self.cache = OrderedDict()
        self.capacity = capacity
        self.lock = threading.Lock()

    def get(self, key: int) -> int:
        with self.lock:
            if key not in self.cache:
                return -1
            self.cache.move_to_end(key)
            return self.cache[key]

    def put(self, key: int, value: int) -> None:
        with self.lock:
            if key in self.cache:
                self.cache.move_to_end(key)
            self.cache[key] = value
            if len(self.cache) > self.capacity:
                self.cache.popitem(last=False)
```
**Skills Tested:** Python, LRU cache, thread safety, OrderedDict.

**ID:** AAD-543
**Category:** Programming
**Subcategory:** C++ — Memory Management
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Implement a simplified `shared_ptr` in C++ that supports reference counting and automatic deletion. Include the control block and the copy/move constructors.
**Expected Answer:**
```cpp
template <typename T>
class SharedPtr {
    struct ControlBlock {
        T* ptr;
        int count;
        ControlBlock(T* p) : ptr(p), count(1) {}
    };
    ControlBlock* cb;
public:
    explicit SharedPtr(T* p = nullptr) : cb(p ? new ControlBlock(p) : nullptr) {}
    
    SharedPtr(const SharedPtr& other) : cb(other.cb) {
        if (cb) cb->count++;
    }
    
    SharedPtr(SharedPtr&& other) noexcept : cb(other.cb) {
        other.cb = nullptr;
    }
    
    ~SharedPtr() {
        if (cb && --cb->count == 0) {
            delete cb->ptr;
            delete cb;
        }
    }
    
    T& operator*() { return *(cb->ptr); }
    T* operator->() { return cb->ptr; }
};
```
**Skills Tested:** C++, smart pointers, reference counting, rule of three/five.

**ID:** AAD-544
**Category:** Programming
**Subcategory:** Rust — Iterators
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Implement the `Iterator` trait for a custom Binary Search Tree (BST) in Rust to perform an in-order traversal. Assume the tree nodes are defined.
**Expected Answer:**
```rust
struct Node<T> {
    value: T,
    left: Option<Box<Node<T>>>,
    right: Option<Box<Node<T>>>,
}

struct InOrderIter<'a, T> {
    stack: Vec<&'a Node<T>>,
}

impl<'a, T> Iterator for InOrderIter<'a, T> {
    type Item = &'a T;
    fn next(&mut self) -> Option<Self::Item> {
        // Push all left nodes
        while let Some(current) = self.stack.last() {
            if let Some(ref left) = current.left {
                self.stack.push(left);
            } else {
                break;
            }
        }
        
        if let Some(node) = self.stack.pop() {
            let val = &node.value;
            if let Some(ref right) = node.right {
                self.stack.push(right);
            }
            Some(val)
        } else {
            None
        }
    }
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Rust, iterators, BST, in-order traversal, lifetimes.

**ID:** AAD-545
**Category:** Programming
**Subcategory:** JavaScript — Deep Cloning
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Implement a `deepClone` function in JavaScript that correctly handles circular references, Dates, RegExps, and plain objects/arrays.
**Expected Answer:**
```javascript
function deepClone(obj, hash = new WeakMap()) {
    if (Object(obj) !== obj) return obj; // Primitives
    if (hash.has(obj)) return hash.get(obj); // Circular reference
    
    let clone;
    if (obj instanceof Date) clone = new Date(obj.getTime());
    else if (obj instanceof RegExp) clone = new RegExp(obj.source, obj.flags);
    else if (Array.isArray(obj)) clone = [];
    else clone = Object.create(Object.getPrototypeOf(obj));
    
    hash.set(obj, clone);
    
    for (let key in obj) {
        if (Object.prototype.hasOwnProperty.call(obj, key)) {
            clone[key] = deepClone(obj[key], hash);
        }
    }
    return clone;
}
```
**Skills Tested:** JavaScript, deep cloning, WeakMap, circular references.

**ID:** AAD-546
**Category:** Programming
**Subcategory:** Go — Concurrency
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Implement a worker pool pattern in Go that processes a list of jobs concurrently, limits the number of concurrent workers to $N$, and supports graceful shutdown using `context`.
**Expected Answer:**
```go
package main

import (
    "context"
    "fmt"
    "sync"
)

func workerPool(ctx context.Context, jobs <-chan int, results chan<- int, wg *sync.WaitGroup) {
    defer wg.Done()
    for {
        select {
        case <-ctx.Done():
            return
        case job, ok := <-jobs:
            if !ok {
                return
            }
            // Process job
            results <- job * 2
        }
    }
}

func main() {
    ctx, cancel := context.WithCancel(context.Background())
    defer cancel()
    
    jobs := make(chan int, 100)
    results := make(chan int, 100)
    var wg sync.WaitGroup
    
    // Start 5 workers
    for w := 0; w < 5; w++ {
        wg.Add(1)
        go workerPool(ctx, jobs, results, &wg)
    }
    
    // Send jobs
    for j := 1; j <= 10; j++ {
        jobs <- j
    }
    close(jobs)
    
    // Wait for workers to finish and close results
    go func() {
        wg.Wait()
        close(results)
    }()
    
    for r := range results {
        fmt.Println(r)
    }
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Go, concurrency, worker pool, context, channels.

**ID:** AAD-547
**Category:** Programming
**Subcategory:** SQL — Recursive CTE
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Write a SQL query using a Recursive Common Table Expression (CTE) to find the shortest path (in terms of number of edges) between two nodes in a graph represented by an `edges(from_node, to_node)` table.
**Expected Answer:**
```sql
WITH RECURSIVE PathCTE AS (
    -- Base case: start node
    SELECT 
        from_node AS current_node, 
        to_node AS next_node, 
        1 AS distance,
        CAST(from_node || '->' || to_node AS VARCHAR(1000)) AS path
    FROM edges
    WHERE from_node = 'A' -- Start node
    
    UNION ALL
    
    -- Recursive step
    SELECT 
        p.current_node, 
        e.to_node, 
        p.distance + 1,
        p.path || '->' || e.to_node
    FROM PathCTE p
    JOIN edges e ON p.next_node = e.from_node
    WHERE p.distance < 10 -- Prevent infinite loops in cyclic graphs
      AND e.to_node != 'A' -- Avoid immediate backtracking
)
SELECT path, distance 
FROM PathCTE 
WHERE next_node = 'Z' -- Target node
ORDER BY distance ASC 
LIMIT 1;
```
**Skills Tested:** SQL, recursive CTE, graph traversal, shortest path.

**ID:** AAD-548
**Category:** Programming
**Subcategory:** Python — Algorithms
**Difficulty Level:** 8
**Question:** Implement the A* search algorithm in Python for a 2D grid with obstacles. Use a proper priority queue and a heuristic function (Manhattan distance).
**Expected Answer:**
```python
import heapq

def heuristic(a, b):
    return abs(a[0] - b[0]) + abs(a[1] - b[1])

def astar(grid, start, goal):
    rows, cols = len(grid), len(grid[0])
    open_set = []
    heapq.heappush(open_set, (0, start))
    came_from = {}
    g_score = {start: 0}
    f_score = {start: heuristic(start, goal)}
    
    while open_set:
        _, current = heapq.heappop(open_set)
        
        if current == goal:
            path = []
            while current in came_from:
                path.append(current)
                current = came_from[current]
            return path[::-1]
            
        for dx, dy in [(-1,0), (1,0), (0,-1), (0,1)]:
            neighbor = (current[0] + dx, current[1] + dy)
            if 0 <= neighbor[0] < rows and 0 <= neighbor[1] < cols and grid[neighbor[0]][neighbor[1]] == 0:
                tentative_g = g_score[current] + 1
                if neighbor not in g_score or tentative_g < g_score[neighbor]:
                    came_from[neighbor] = current
                    g_score[neighbor] = tentative_g
                    f_score[neighbor] = tentative_g + heuristic(neighbor, goal)
                    heapq.heappush(open_set, (f_score[neighbor], neighbor))
    return None
```
**Skills Tested:** Python, A* algorithm, priority queue, pathfinding.

**ID:** AAD-549
**Category:** Programming
**Subcategory:** C — Concurrency
**Difficulty Level:** 10
**Question:** Implement a lock-free single-producer, single-consumer (SPSC) ring buffer in C using C11 atomics. Ensure proper memory ordering to prevent data races.
**Expected Answer:**
```c
#include <stdatomic.h>
#include <stdint.h>
#include <stdbool.h>

#define BUFFER_SIZE 1024

typedef struct {
    int32_t buffer[BUFFER_SIZE];
    atomic_uint_fast32_t head;
    atomic_uint_fast32_t tail;
} SPSC_RingBuffer;

bool spsc_push(SPSC_RingBuffer* rb, int32_t value) {
    uint_fast32_t head = atomic_load_explicit(&rb->head, memory_order_relaxed);
    uint_fast32_t next_head = (head + 1) % BUFFER_SIZE;
    if (next_head == atomic_load_explicit(&rb->tail, memory_order_acquire)) {
        return false; // Full
    }
    rb->buffer[head] = value;
    atomic_store_explicit(&rb->head, next_head, memory_order_release);
    return true;
}

bool spsc_pop(SPSC_RingBuffer* rb, int32_t* value) {
    uint_fast32_t tail = atomic_load_explicit(&rb->tail, memory_order_relaxed);
    if (tail == atomic_load_explicit(&rb->head, memory_order_acquire)) {
        return false; // Empty
    }
    *value = rb->buffer[tail];
    atomic_store_explicit(&rb->tail, (tail + 1) % BUFFER_SIZE, memory_order_release);
    return true;
}
```
**Skills Tested:** C, lock-free programming, atomics, memory ordering, ring buffer.

**ID:** AAD-550
**Category:** Programming
**Subcategory:** TypeScript — Advanced Types
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Write a TypeScript utility type `DeepReadonly<T>` that recursively makes all properties of an object, including nested objects and arrays, `readonly`.
**Expected Answer:**
```typescript
type DeepReadonly<T> = {
    readonly [P in keyof T]: T[P] extends Array<infer U>
        ? ReadonlyArray<DeepReadonly<U>>
        : T[P] extends object
        ? DeepReadonly<T[P]>
        : T[P];
};

// Example usage:
interface Config {
    db: { host: string; port: number; credentials: { user: string; pass: string } };
    tags: string[];
}

type ReadonlyConfig = DeepReadonly<Config>;
// ReadonlyConfig.db.host is readonly
// ReadonlyConfig.tags is ReadonlyArray<string>
```
**Skills Tested:** TypeScript, advanced types, recursive types, mapped types.

**ID:** AAD-551
**Category:** Programming
**Subcategory:** Java — ClassLoaders
**Difficulty Level:** 9
**Question:** Implement a custom `ClassLoader` in Java that loads classes from a remote URL securely, ensuring that only classes from a specific domain are loaded.
**Expected Answer:**
```java
import java.io.*;
import java.net.*;
import java.security.SecureClassLoader;

public class RemoteClassLoader extends SecureClassLoader {
    private String allowedDomain;
    private URL baseURL;

    public RemoteClassLoader(URL baseURL, String allowedDomain, ClassLoader parent) {
        super(parent);
        this.baseURL = baseURL;
        this.allowedDomain = allowedDomain;
    }

    @Override
    protected Class<?> findClass(String name) throws ClassNotFoundException {
        try {
            URL url = new URL(baseURL, name.replace('.', '/') + ".class");
            if (!url.getHost().endsWith(allowedDomain)) {
                throw new SecurityException("Unauthorized domain: " + url.getHost());
            }
            
            try (InputStream is = url.openStream()) {
                ByteArrayOutputStream baos = new ByteArrayOutputStream();
                int byteRead;
                while ((byteRead = is.read()) != -1) {
                    baos.write(byteRead);
                }
                byte[] bytes = baos.toByteArray();
                return defineClass(name, bytes, 0, bytes.length);
            }
        } catch (IOException e) {
            throw new ClassNotFoundException("Could not load class " + name, e);
        }
    }
}
```
**Skills Tested:** Java, ClassLoaders, security, remote loading.
